Enumerating Possible Meanings of Logical Terms
The author lists seven meanings of 'first section' and five of 'logic', yielding thirty-five combinations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to enumerate the possible meanings of a key term in a logical commentary and compute the total number of interpretations.

Core Idea
One term can carry multiple meanings; understanding the combinatorial possibilities sharpens exegesis.

In this lesson, you will see how the commentator dissects the terms 'first section' and 'logic' to reveal seven and five meanings respectively. By multiplying them, he arrives at 35 possible interpretations. This exercise demonstrates the precision required in reading logical texts. Your task is to learn this method of enumerating meanings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the target terms
Locate the terms 'القسم الأول' and 'المنطق' in the passage. These are the two key terms being analyzed.
2
Extract the list of meanings for each term
From the passage, note that 'القسم الأول' can mean words, meanings, written forms, or combinations of two or three. 'المنطق' can mean the faculty, knowledge of all problems, sufficient knowledge for infallibility, the problems themselves, or the sufficient amount.
Count: 7 for 'first section', 5 for 'logic'.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the lists
The commentator derived these meanings by considering possible referents: the words, the meanings they convey, the written script, and their combinations for 'first section'; for 'logic', he considered the mental faculty, the knowledge, the problems, and degrees of knowledge.
The enumeration is exhaustive for the context.
4
Compute total combinations
Multiply the number of meanings: 7 × 5 = 35. This gives all possible pairings of meanings for the phrase 'القسم الأول في المنطق'.
Not all combinations are equally plausible; sound reason selects the appropriate one.
5
Apply the method to a new term
Choose another term from Islamic sciences, such as 'أمر' (command) in usul al-fiqh, and try to enumerate its possible meanings. This reinforces the skill.
The goal is to internalize the habit of careful semantic analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله «قوام» ای ما یقوم به امره قوله «التأييد» اى التقوية، من «الايد» بمعنى القوة. قوله «عصام» ای ما يحفظ به امره من الزلل قوله « وعلى الله » قدم الظرف هيهنا لقصد الحصر وفي قوله: «به» لرعاية السجع ايضاً قوله «التوكل» هو التمسك بالحق والانقطاع عن الخلق قوله « الاعتصام هو التشبث والتمسك قوله «القسم الاول : لما علم ضمنا من قوله في تحرير المنطق و الكلام، ان كتابه على قسمين لم يحتج الى التصريح بهذا فصح تعريف القسم الأول بلام العهد لكونه معهوداً ضمنا و هذا بخلاف المقدمة فانها لم يعلم وجودها سابقاً فلم تكن معهودة فلهذا نكرها وقال: «مقدمة». قوله «فى المنطق» ان قيل ليس المراد بالقسم الاول الا المسائل المنطقية فما توجيه الظرفية؟ قلت: يجوز ان يراد بالقسم الاول الالفاظ و العبارات و بالمنطق المعاني فيكون المعنى : ان هذه الالفاظ في بيان هذه المعانى ويحتمل وجوهاً أخر. والتفصيل : ان القسم الاول عبارة عن احد معان سبعة : اما الالفاظ او المعاني او النقوش او المركب من الاثنين او الثلاثة، والمنطق عبارة عن احد معان خمسة اما الملكة او العلم بجميع المسائل او بالقدر المعتد به الذي يحصل به العصمة او نفس المسائل جميعاً او نفس القدر المعتد به، فيحصل من ملاحظة الخمسة مع السبعة خمسة وثلاثون احتمالا يقدر في بعضها البيان و في بعضها التحصيل او الحصول حيث ما وجده العقل السليم مناسباً.
His saying: 'support (qiwam)' is that by which his affair stands firm. His saying: 'confirmation (ta'yid)' is strengthening, from 'al-ayd' meaning strength. His saying: 'bond (isam)' is that which preserves his affair from slipping. His saying: 'And upon Allah' — the adverb is placed here for the purpose of restriction, and in His saying: 'by Him' for the sake of rhyme as well. His saying: 'reliance (tawakkul)' is clinging to the Truth and cutting off from creation. His saying: 'holding fast (i'tisam)' is clinging and grasping. His saying: 'The first section' — because it was implicitly known from his saying in 'Tahrir al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam' that his book is on two divisions, he did not need to make this explicit, so it is correct to define 'al-qism al-awwal' with the definite article of covenant (lam al-'ahd) because it is implicitly well-known. This is unlike the introduction, for its existence was not previously known, so it was not well-known, hence he made it indefinite and said: 'An introduction.' His saying: 'on logic (fi al-mantiq)' — if it is said that by 'first section' is meant only the logical problems, then what is the justification of the preposition 'fi'? I say: it is possible that by 'first section' is meant the words and expressions, and by 'logic' the meanings, so the meaning is: these words are in explaining these meanings. And there may be other aspects. The detail is: 'al-qism al-awwal' is one of seven meanings: either the words, or the meanings, or the written forms, or the combination of two, or the combination of three. And 'al-mantiq' is one of five meanings: either the faculty, or the knowledge of all problems, or of the sufficient amount by which infallibility is attained, or the problems themselves all together, or the sufficient amount itself. So from considering the five with the seven, thirty-five possibilities are obtained. In some of them, explanation (bayan) is intended, and in some, acquisition (tahsil) or obtaining (husul), wherever sound reason finds it appropriate.
Source pages p. 8

Commentary
This passage continues the commentary on the author's introductory words, now focusing on the phrase 'القسم الأول: في المنطق'. The commentator begins by explaining the preceding words ('قوام', 'تأييد', 'عصام', etc.) which are part of the author's blessing: 'قوام' means that by which his affair stands, 'تأييد' is strengthening, 'عصام' is what preserves from error. Then he interprets 'التوكل' as clinging to truth and cutting off from creation, and 'الاعتصام' as clinging. Then he turns to 'القسم الأول'. He notes that the book is divided into two parts, as indicated in the author's other work 'تحرير المنطق والكلام', so the first section is definite because it is implicitly known. The introduction, however, is indefinite because it was not previously mentioned. Next, he addresses the preposition 'في' in 'في المنطق'. If someone objects that 'first section' only means the logical issues, how can it be said 'in logic'? The commentator replies that 'first section' could mean the expressions, and 'logic' the meanings, so the expression means 'these words are in explaining these meanings'. To elaborate, he enumerates possible meanings: 'القسم الأول' can be one of seven things: words, meanings, written forms, or combinations of two or three. 'المنطق' can be one of five: the faculty (malaka), knowledge of all logical problems, sufficient knowledge that gives infallibility, the problems themselves, or the sufficient amount itself. Multiplying the seven by five gives thirty-five possible interpretations. The commentator concludes that sound reason can determine which interpretation is appropriate for explanation or acquisition. This enumeration is a careful logical exercise demonstrating the polysemy of key terms, typical of scholarly commentary precision.

المنطق

Key Takeaway
The commentator's enumeration of seven and five meanings yields 35 possibilities, illustrating the need for precise interpretation of key terms.

?

The Definition of Nazar: Genus and Differentia
Author defines nazar as contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to extract the genus and differentia from the definition of nazar and evaluate whether it is a complete hadd.

Core Idea
نظر is a directed mental movement from known universals to unknown conclusions.

In logic, every definition must specify a genus and a differentia. The author defines nazar as 'contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown.' Let's break down this definition to see how it works and why it is considered a complete logical definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Find the definiendum in the Arabic. The passage begins with 'و الاكتساب بالنظر' but the definition is given as 'هو ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول'. So the definiendum is 'النظر' (reflection/inspection).
2
Extract the genus (jins)
The genus is the broader category that nazar belongs to. Here it is 'ملاحظة' (contemplation). This places nazar under the category of mental acts of observation.
3
Extract the differentia (fasl)
The differentia distinguishes nazar from other types of contemplation. It is 'المعقول لتحصيل المجهول' — it is contemplation specifically of the intelligible (universal concepts) for the purpose of acquiring the unknown.
4
Test the definition's completeness
Does this definition cover all instances of nazar? Consider the examples given: reflection to know the reality of angels or that the world is originated. Are these covered? Yes, they involve moving from known universals to unknown conclusions. Does it exclude things that are not nazar? Necessary knowledge (like heat/cold) is excluded because it doesn't involve acquiring the unknown. This suggests a complete hadd.
The commentator notes that the word 'ma'qul' is used to specify universals, avoiding ambiguity.
5
Determine the type of definition
Is this a hadd haqiqi (real definition) or rasm? Since it gives the essence of nazar as a mental operation moving from known to unknown, it appears to be a hadd haqiqi. The genus and differentia capture the core nature.
Later texts may distinguish between nazar and fikr, but here it is treated as a unified concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الاكتساب بالنظر ((ه) و هو ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول. الاستدلال كما ارتكبه القوم وذلك لانا اذا رجعنا الى وجداننا وجدنا ان من التصورات ما هو حاصل لنا بلانظر كتصور الحرارة والبرودة ومنها ما هو حاصل لنا بالنظر والفكر كتصور حقيقة الملك والجن و كذا من التصديقات ما يحصل لنا بلانظر كالتصديق بان الشمس مشرقة والنار محرقة ومنها ما يحصل لنا بالنظر كالتصديق بان العالم حادث و الصانع موجود. قوله « وهو ملاحظة المعقول» اى النظر توجه النفس نحو الامر المعقول اى المعلوم لتحصيل امر غير معلوم وفى العدول عن لفظ (المعلوم» الى (المعقول ،فوائد منها : التحرز عن استعمال اللفظ المشترك فى التعريف، ومنها: التنبيه على ان الفكر الما في المعقولات اى الامور الكلية.
And acquisition is by reflection. (He said) It is the contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown. [This is] the inference as the people have practiced it. And that is because if we return to our inner experience, we find that some conceptions are attained by us without reflection, like the conception of heat and cold, and some are attained by us through reflection and thought, like the conception of the reality of an angel and a jinn. Similarly, some assents are attained by us without reflection, like the assent that the sun is shining and fire is burning, and some are attained by us through reflection, like the assent that the world is originated and the Maker exists. His statement 'it is the contemplation of the intelligible' means reflection is the direction of the soul towards the intelligible thing, i.e., the known, to acquire something unknown. And in deviating from the term 'the known' to 'the intelligible' there are benefits: among them, avoiding the use of a homonym in the definition, and among them, drawing attention to the fact that thought is only in intelligibles, i.e., universal matters.
Source pages p. 11

Commentary
This passage defines nazar (reflection/inspection) and provides examples. The matn says 'acquisition is by reflection' and then defines nazar as 'contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown'. The commentator first explains that this is the method of inference (istidlal) used by scholars. He justifies the need for nazar by appealing to self-evident experience: we find that some conceptions are attained without reflection, like the conception of heat and cold (these are necessary), while others require reflection, like the conception of the reality of angels or jinn. Similarly, assents: the belief that the sun is shining or fire is burning is necessary, while the belief that the world is originated or that God exists requires reflection. This shows that knowledge is divided into necessary and acquired, and acquired knowledge requires nazar. Then the commentator unpacks the definition: 'contemplation of the intelligible' means the soul's direction towards a known intelligible (al-ma'qul, i.e., a universal concept) to acquire something unknown. The author (matn) used the word 'al-ma'qul' (the intelligible) instead of 'al-ma'lum' (the known) to avoid ambiguity (since 'al-ma'lum' could also mean 'the known' in a general sense) and to indicate that thought operates on universal concepts, not particulars. The key idea: nazar is the mental process of moving from known universals to unknown conclusions.

Key Terms
النظر
المعقول
المجهول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the genus in the definition of nazar as given in the passage?
A) الاكتساب (acquisition)
B) ملاحظة (contemplation)
C) المعقول (the intelligible)
D) تحصيل المجهول (acquiring the unknown)

Key Takeaway
Nazar is defined by its genus (contemplation) and differentia (of the intelligible to acquire the unknown), making it a complete hadd that distinguishes it from other mental acts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Using the definition of nazar as 'contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown', determine whether the following qualifies as nazar: A scholar reflects on the verse 'And those who guard their private parts' (Qur'an 23:5) and concludes that guarding the private parts is obligatory in all circumstances. Defend your answer by referencing the genus and differentia.

Why al-Ma'qul? The Commentator's Refinement of the Definition
Author defines nazar as contemplation of the intelligible to acquire the unknown.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain two reasons why the author chose 'al-ma'qul' over 'al-ma'lum' in the definition of nazar.

Core Idea
Word choice in a definition matters: using 'al-ma'qul' avoids homonymy and confines nazar to universals.

The definition of nazar uses 'al-ma'qul' (the intelligible) rather than 'al-ma'lum' (the known). Why did the author prefer this term? The commentator highlights two benefits that refine the definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the alternative wording
The passage notes that the matn could have used 'al-ma'lum' (the known) but instead used 'al-ma'qul' (the intelligible). Find where this is mentioned in the Arabic: 'وفى العدول عن لفظ «المعلوم» الى «المعقول» فوائد'.
2
Understand the first benefit: avoiding homonymy
The term 'al-ma'lum' can mean both 'the known' and 'the well-known' or have multiple senses. Using 'al-ma'qul' avoids ambiguity in the definition. Why is this important? A definition must use clear, unambiguous terms.
3
Understand the second benefit: restricting to universals
The commentator says: 'التنبيه على ان الفكر ما في المعقولات اى الامور الكلية' — drawing attention that thought (fikr) is only in intelligibles, i.e., universal matters. This restricts nazar to universal concepts, excluding particular sensory data from the starting point.
This aligns with the purpose of logic: it deals with universals, not particulars.
4
Evaluate the impact on the definition
How does this choice strengthen the definition? By using 'ma'qul', the definition precisely captures the domain of nazar: it begins with known universals. It also avoids the confusion that might arise if one thought nazar could start from particular knowns.
The commentator's analysis shows the depth of thought in crafting definitions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و الاكتساب بالنظر ((ه) و هو ملاحظة المعقول لتحصيل المجهول. الاستدلال كما ارتكبه القوم وذلك لانا اذا رجعنا الى وجداننا وجدنا ان من التصورات ما هو حاصل لنا بلانظر كتصور الحرارة والبرودة ومنها ما هو حاصل لنا بالنظر والفكر كتصور حقيقة الملك والجن و كذا من التصديقات ما يحصل لنا بلانظر كالتصديق بان الشمس مشرقة والنار محرقة ومنها ما يحصل لنا بالنظر كالتصديق بان العالم حادث و الصانع موجود. قوله « وهو ملاحظة المعقول» اى النظر توجه النفس نحو الامر المعقول اى المعلوم لتحصيل امر غير معلوم وفى العدول عن لفظ (المعلوم» الى (المعقول ،فوائد منها : التحرز عن استعمال اللفظ المشترك فى التعريف، ومنها: التنبيه على ان الفكر الما في المعقولات اى الامور الكلية.

Defining Qanun: The Universal Rule in Logic
Author defines the term 'qanun' and illustrates its usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the technical meaning of 'qanun' in Islamic logic and apply it to an example from the Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
A law (qanun) is a universal proposition that governs particular cases.

In this passage, the author clarifies that the term 'law' (qanun) in logic does not refer to a physical ruler but to a universal proposition from which particular rulings are derived. This concept is essential for understanding how logical rules apply to specific arguments.

Guided Path
1
Locate the etymology of qanun
Find where the author says qanun is a Greek or Syriac word originally meaning a scribe's ruler. This helps understand its shift to a technical term.
The original meaning is a ruler for writing straight lines.
2
Identify the technical definition
The author defines qanun as 'a universal proposition from which are known the rulings of its particular subjects'. Why must a law be universal? Because particulars alone cannot serve as a rule.
Universality allows application to many cases.
3
Analyse the grammatical example
The example 'Every subject is in the nominative case' is a universal ruling. Show how it applies to particular subjects like 'Zaid' or 'the book'.
The example is from Arabic grammar (nahw).
4
Reflect on the need for universal rules
Why can't particular matters themselves be a law? The author says 'the particular cannot be acquired nor be acquisitive'.
Particulars are limited; only universals provide a standard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد يقع فيه الخطأ فاحتيج إلى قانون تعصم الحاصلة في العقل دون الأمور الجزئية فإن الجزئي لا يكون كاسباً ولا مكتسباً ومنها رعاية السجع. قوله «قانون»: هو لفظ يوناني أو لفظ سرياني موضوع في الأصل لمسطر الكتابة وفي الاصطلاح قضية كلية تعرف منها أحكام جزئيات موضوعها كقول النحاة: «كل فاعل مرفوع فإنه حكم كلي يعلم منه أحكام جزئيات الفاعل. قوله «وقد يقع فيه الخطأ»: بدليل أن الفكر قد ينتهي إلى نتيجة كحدوث العالم وقد ينتهي إلى نقيضها كقدم العالم فأحد الفكرين خطأ لا محالة واللازم اجتماع النقيضين فلابد من قاعدة كلية لو روعيت لم يقع الخطأ في الفكر وهو المنطق، ضعيف مريض فإنه لا يتمكن أن يثبت المنطق من وراء تلك المقدمات التي ساقها كما رأيت فإن المنطق ليس به لمحدوديته على القيام بصلاح المواد التي تؤخذ في طرق الأدلة والأقيسة غاية ما هناك هو لا يزال يكرر الوصايا بأن مقدمات القياس الفلاني ومواده يلزم أن تكون كذا فهو كواعظ لا سلطان مسيطر. وهل يبتنى على الوعظ نظام عام؟ حاشا وكلا وهذه الملحوظة يجب الالتفات إليها والتفطن لها ولا يؤخذ قولهم: المنطق يعصم الذهن عن الخطأ في الفكر، بتسليم كلي.
And error may occur in it, so a law is needed that safeguards the obtained [concepts] in the mind, not particular matters, for the particular cannot be acquisitive nor acquired. And among them [the principles] is observance of rhyme. His saying 'a law': it is a Greek word or a Syriac word originally placed for the scribe's ruler, and in terminology it is a universal proposition from which are known the rulings of its particular subjects, like the grammarians' saying: 'Every subject is in the nominative case, for it is a universal ruling from which the rulings of particular subjects are known.' His saying 'And error may occur in it': as evidence that thought may reach a result like the origination of the world, and may reach its opposite like the eternity of the world; then one of the two thoughts is necessarily erroneous, otherwise the two contradictories would coexist. So a universal rule is necessary such that if observed, error would not occur in thought, and that is logic. [This evidence] is weak and sick, for it cannot prove logic from those premises he presented, as you have seen, because logic, due to its limitation, cannot undertake the correction of the materials taken in the paths of proofs and syllogisms. The utmost there is that it continually repeats the recommendations that the premises of such-and-such a syllogism and its materials must be such and such; so it is like a preacher without controlling authority. Can a general system be built on preaching? Far from it! And this observation must be attended to and understood, and their statement 'Logic safeguards the mind from error in thought' should not be accepted with complete submission.
Source pages p. 12

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary. The author first states the need for a law to safeguard the mind from error, noting that error occurs in reflection (nazar). He then defines 'law' (qanun) as a Greek or Syriac word meaning a scribe's ruler, but in logical terminology it is a universal proposition from which particular rulings are derived, giving the example from grammar: 'Every subject is in the nominative case' – a universal rule covering particular subjects. Next, he presents the standard argument that because thought can reach contradictory conclusions (e.g., the world is originated vs. eternal), a universal law is needed to prevent error, and that law is logic. He then launches a strong critique: this argument is weak because logic cannot fix material errors – it only deals with form. Logic cannot distinguish true premises from false; it merely recommends that premises be certain, but does not judge certainty. It is like a preacher without authority. Therefore, the claim that logic fully safeguards the mind should not be accepted without qualification. This is a key limitation of logic that students must understand.

قضية كلية

Key Takeaway
Qanun is a universal rule that applies to particular cases, essential for systematic reasoning.

The Limits of Logic: Why Form Alone Cannot Prevent Error
Author critiques the argument that logic prevents error, highlighting its formal-only scope.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the author's critique of the claim that logic safeguards the mind from error and explain why logic cannot correct material premises.

Core Idea
Logic ensures formal validity but cannot guarantee the truth of premises.

The author presents the standard argument that because thought can reach contradictory conclusions, a universal rule (logic) is needed to prevent error. He then launches a powerful critique: this argument fails because logic can only advise on form, not on the truth of the material premises. Logic is like a powerless preacher, not an authoritative safeguard.

Guided Path
1
Extract the original argument for logic's necessity
Find the lines where the author presents the evidence: thought can lead to contradictory results (e.g., world originated vs. eternal), so a universal rule (logic) is required to avoid error.
This is the argument the author critiques.
2
Identify the critique's core point
The author says 'logic cannot be responsible for correcting the materials taken in the paths of proofs and syllogisms'. What does this mean?
Logic only checks form; it does not verify if premises are true.
3
Understand the analogy of the preacher
The author compares logic to a preacher who gives recommendations but has no authority. How does this illustrate logic's limitation?
A preacher can advise but cannot enforce; similarly, logic can recommend valid forms but cannot ensure true premises.
4
Assess the conclusion
The author concludes that the claim 'Logic safeguards the mind from error in thought' should not be accepted unconditionally. Why?
Because logic only covers one aspect of reasoning (form), not material truth.

And error may occur in it, so a law is needed that safeguards the obtained [concepts] in the mind, not particular matters, for the particular cannot be acquisitive nor acquired. And among them [the principles] is observance of rhyme. His saying 'a law': it is a Greek word or a Syriac word originally placed for the scribe's ruler, and in terminology it is a universal proposition from which are known the rulings of its particular subjects, like the grammarians' saying: 'Every subject is in the nominative case, for it is a universal ruling from which the rulings of particular subjects are known.' Three Premises for the Necessity of Logic
Epistemological proof for the necessity of logic and its definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three-premise argument for the necessity of logic and state the definition of logic as a law safeguarding the mind.

Core Idea
Logic is a law that safeguards the mind from error in thought — proven by three simple premises.

In this passage, the author provides the epistemological foundation for the entire discipline of logic. By laying out three premises about knowledge and reflection, he shows why a law of thought is indispensable. This argument is not just a theoretical exercise; it justifies why every student of Islamic sciences must master logic before engaging in deeper inquiry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premises
Read the Arabic text and locate the three numbered premises. They are introduced by 'الأولى', 'الثانية', 'الثالثة'.
Each premise is a simple statement about knowledge and reflection.
2
Understand each premise
Premise 1 divides knowledge into tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent). Premise 2 says each can be acquired without reflection (badihi) or by reflection (nazar). Premise 3 states that reflection may contain error.
These are considered axiomatic in the tradition.
3
Connect premises to conclusion
Since we rely on reflection to gain new knowledge (Premise 2) and reflection can err (Premise 3), we need a law to avoid error. This law is logic.
The conclusion is implicit but stated as 'فهذه المقدمات الثلاث تفيد احتياج الناس... إلى قانون'.
4
Extract the definition of logic
From the conclusion, the author defines logic as 'a law whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thought'. Write this definition and identify its genus (law) and differentia (safeguarding from error).
The definition is given at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فقد ثبت احتياج الناس إلى المنطق في العصمة عن الخطأ في الفكر بثلاث مقدمات: الأولى: أن العلم إما تصور وإما تصديق. والثانية: أن كلا منهما إما أن يحصل بلا نظر أو حصل بالنظر. والثالثة: أن النظر قد يقع فيه الخطا. فهذه المقدمات الثلاث تفيد احتياج الناس في التحرز عن الخطاء في الفكر إلى قانون وذلك هو المنطق وعلم من هذا تعريف المنطق أيضاً بأنه: قانون تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطاء في الفكر.
Thus the need of people for logic for protection against error in thought has been established through three premises: First: knowledge is either conceptual or assent. Second: each of them is either obtained without reflection or obtained by reflection. Third: reflection may contain error. So these three premises indicate the need of people for a law to guard against error in thought, and that is logic. And from this the definition of logic is also known: it is a law whose observance safeguards the mind from error in thought.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is an epistemological proof for the necessity of logic. The author presents three premises that together show why we need a law to avoid error. Premise 1 divides knowledge (ilm) into two categories: conceptual knowledge (tasawwur, e.g., understanding the meaning of 'world') and assent (tasdiq, e.g., affirming that the world is created). Premise 2 says each of these can be acquired either without reflection (badihi, self-evident) or through reflection (nazar, reasoning). Premise 3 states that reflection can lead to error. The conclusion: since we use reflection to move from known to unknown, and reflection can err, we need a law (qanun) to keep our reflection on the right path. That law is logic. The passage then defines logic as a law (qanun) whose observance ('tura'atuha' – careful attention to it) safeguards the mind from error in thought. This definition is standard in the tradition, tying logic directly to the prevention of intellectual mistakes.

The Subject of Logic: Conceptual Known and Proof
Author clarifies that the subject of logic is the conceptual known and the proof, and explains 'essential accident'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define 'essential accident' (al-arad al-dhati) and identify the two ways it attaches to a subject, as well as state the subject matter of logic.

Core Idea
Every science, including logic, investigates the essential accidents of its subject.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies what the 'subject' of a science means and explains the crucial concept of 'essential accident'. He then specifies that logic's subject is both the definer (conceptual knowledge) and the proof, examined not in themselves but as tools for attaining unknown knowledge. Understanding this sets the foundation for all logical study.

Guided Path
1
Understand 'subject of a science' (mawdu' al-ilm).
The passage defines mawdu' al-ilm as that in which the science investigates its essential accidents (arad dhatiyya). Every science has a topic and studies properties that necessarily follow from that topic's essence. Locate the phrase in the Arabic and note the emphasis on 'essential accidents'.
This definition is standard across Islamic sciences.
2
Grasp the two types of essential accident (arad dhati).
The author gives two types: (1) those that occur primarily and essentially (awwalan wa bi al-dhat), like wonder (ta'ajjub) for humans; (2) those that occur through something coextensive (bi wasitat amr musawin), like laughter (dahk) for humans, which actually belongs to wonder. Identify the example and understand the distinction.
Laughter is an essential accident of humans only indirectly, because it is an essential accident of wonder, which is itself essential to humans.
3
Identify the subject of logic: definer and proof.
The passage states that the subject (mawdu') of logic is 'the definer (al-mu'arraf) and the proof (al-hujja)'. The definer corresponds to conceptual knowledge (al-malum al-tasawwuri), but not absolutely; it is studied insofar as it leads to something else. The proof is the means of attaining assent. Recognize that logic studies these in their instrumental role.
The passage is cut off but the idea is clear: logic's subject is tools for reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فهيهنا علم أمران من الأمور الثلاثة التي وضعت المقدمة لبيانها، بقي الكلام في الأمر الثالث وهو تحقيق أن موضوع المنطق ماذا؟ فأشار إليه بقوله: وموضوعه الخ. قوله «وموضوعه»: موضوع العلم ما يبحث فيه عن عوارضه الذاتية والعرض الذاتي ما يعرض الشيء إما أولاً وبالذات كالتعجب اللاحق للإنسان من حيث أنه إنسان وإما بواسطة أمر مساو لذلك الشيء كالضحك الذي يعرض حقيقة للتعجب ثم ينسب عروضه إلى الإنسان بالعرض والمجاز فافهم. قوله «المعلوم التصوري»: اعلم أن موضوع المنطق هو المعرف والحجة أما المعرف فهو عبارة عن المعلوم التصوري ولكن لا مطلقاً بل من حيث أنه يوصل إلى...
So here we know two of the three matters that the premises were set to explain; the remaining is the third matter, which is to investigate what the subject of logic is. He pointed to it by his saying: 'And its subject...' His saying: 'And its subject': the subject of a science is that in which it investigates its essential accidents. And the essential accident is that which occurs to a thing either primarily and essentially, like wonder occurring to human being insofar as he is human, or through something coextensive with that thing, like laughter which occurs truly to wonder and then its occurrence is attributed to human being metaphorically and figuratively—understand. His saying: 'the known conceptual': Know that the subject of logic is the definer and the proof. As for the definer, it is the conceptual known, but not absolutely, rather insofar as it leads to...
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is definitional, explaining the subject (mawdu') of logic. The author first notes that three matters were intended by the earlier premises; two are now clear, and the third is the subject of logic. He quotes the matn: 'And its subject...' then explains what 'subject of a science' means: it is the topic about which the science investigates its essential accidents (arad dhatiyya). He defines 'essential accident' (al-arad al-dhati) as an attribute that belongs to a thing either primarily and essentially (like wonder in humans, since humans are naturally capable of wonder) or through something coextensive with the thing (like laughter, which actually belongs to wonder but is attributed to humans metaphorically because humans are the wonderers). This is standard Aristotelian terminology. Then he specifies that the subject of logic is 'the definer' (al-mu'arraf, which is the conceptual known) and 'the proof' (al-hujja, which relates to assent). However, the conceptual known is not studied absolutely, but insofar as it leads to something else—i.e., as a means to acquire unknown knowledge. The passage cuts off, but the idea is clear: logic studies conceptual knowledge and proofs in their role as tools for reasoning.

Key Takeaway
The subject of any science, including logic, is defined by the essential accidents it studies; logic specifically studies the definer (conceptual knowledge) and the proof as tools for discovery.

The Definition of Logic as a Protective Law
Author defines logic as a protective law.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the definition of logic as a law that protects the mind from error and distinguish it from mere reasoning tools.

Core Idea
Logic is not just reasoning; it is a law that shields the mind from error.

In this passage, the author defines logic as a law (qanun) whose observance protects the mind from error in thought. This is a precise and functional definition. Understanding this definition helps you see why logic is considered essential in the Islamic sciences—it is not an optional tool but a necessary guardian against error.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition in the text
Find the sentence in the Arabic that explicitly defines logic. It occurs after the three premises: 'وعلم من هذا تعريف المنطق ايضاً بانه : قانون تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطاء في الفكر.'
The definition is presented as a conclusion from the preceding argument.
2
Identify the key components
Break down the definition: 1) 'qanun' (law/rule) – it is systematic, not ad hoc. 2) 'ta'sim' (protects) – it prevents error. 3) 'al-dhihn' (the mind) – the subject. 4) 'al-khata' fi al-fikr' (error in thought) – what it guards against.
3
Compare with alternative definitions
Some define logic as a 'tool' (ala) for knowledge. How is 'law' (qanun) different? A law is prescriptive and must be followed; a tool is optional. The author emphasizes necessity.
In Islamic sciences, laws like those of usul al-fiqh are also called qawanin.
4
Apply to an Islamic science example
In usul al-fiqh, the rules of qiyas (analogical reasoning) function as a law to protect the jurist from error in deriving rulings. Just as logic protects thought generally, usul al-fiqh protects legal reasoning.
This shows the practical necessity of such a law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلابد من قاعدة كلية لو روعيت لم يقع الخطأ فى الفكر و هو المنطق. فقد ثبت احتياج الناس الى المنطق فى العصمة عن الخطأ فى الفكر بثلاث مقدمات: الاولى : ان العلم اما تصور واما تصديق . الثانية : ان كلا منهما اما ان يحصل بلا نظر او حصل بالنظر. الثالثة : ان النظر قد يقع فيه الخطأ. فهذه المقدمات الثلاث تفيد احتياج الناس فى التحرز عن الخطأ في الفكر الى قانون وذلك هو المنطق. وعلم من هذا تعريف المنطق ايضاً بانه : قانون تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطاء في الفكر.
So there must be a universal rule, if observed, error does not occur in thought, and that is logic. It has been established that people are in need of logic for protection from error in thought by three premises: the first: that knowledge is either a concept or an assent. The second: that each of them is either obtained without reflection or obtained by reflection. The third: that reflection may contain error. So these three premises indicate people's need, in guarding against error in thought, for a law, and that is logic. And it is known from this the definition of logic as well: it is a law, the observance of which protects the mind from error in thought.

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of logic. The author first asserts that a universal rule is needed to avoid error in thought, and identifies that rule as logic. He then provides three premises that collectively demonstrate humanity's need for such a rule. The first premise divides knowledge into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq); the second says each may be acquired without reflection (bi-la nazar) or through reflection; the third warns that reflection can be erroneous. From these, it follows that a law (qanun) is necessary to prevent error, and that law is logic. The author then restates the definition of logic as 'a law whose observance protects the mind from error in thought.' Note the argument is compound: it is a syllogism with three premises leading to a conclusion, but the author treats them as a straightforward indication.

The Three-Premise Proof for the Necessity of Logic
Author presents a three-premise proof for the need for logic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the three-premise argument for the need for logic and evaluate its logical structure.

Core Idea
Because reflection can err, humanity needs a law to guide it – and that law is logic.

The author provides a concise proof for the necessity of logic using three premises. Understanding this argument helps you appreciate why logic is not an optional extra but a fundamental discipline in the Islamic sciences. The argument shows that any thoughtful person who reflects (nazar) needs a rule to avoid falling into error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three premises
Find and list the three premises from the text. P1: Knowledge is either concept (tasawwur) or assent (tasdiq). P2: Each of these is either obtained without reflection (bi-la nazar) or by reflection (bi al-nazar). P3: Reflection may contain error (khata').
The premises are clearly marked by 'the first', 'the second', 'the third'.
2
See how the premises combine
P1 and P2 together imply that all knowledge (except that which is immediate) is acquired through reflection. P3 warns that reflection can be erroneous. Therefore, to avoid error when acquiring knowledge through reflection, a law is needed. The author concludes that this law is logic.
The argument assumes that immediate knowledge (fitri) is error-free, but no rule is needed for it.
3
Check the validity
Is the inference valid? If we accept the premises, the conclusion follows: reflection can lead to error, so a rule to stay on the right path is necessary. The premises are not questioned by the author; they are taken as axiomatic.
Later thinkers might question whether all knowledge requires reflection, but that is not in view here.
4
Apply to Islamic science reasoning
In kalam, theology uses reflection to prove Allah's existence. Without logical rules, such reflection can fall into fallacy. For instance, the argument from temporality (huduth) relies on valid syllogistic form.
This shows the practical import of the proof.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلابد من قاعدة كلية لو روعيت لم يقع الخطأ فى الفكر و هو المنطق. فقد ثبت احتياج الناس الى المنطق فى العصمة عن الخطأ فى الفكر بثلاث مقدمات: الاولى : ان العلم اما تصور واما تصديق . الثانية : ان كلا منهما اما ان يحصل بلا نظر او حصل بالنظر. الثالثة : ان النظر قد يقع فيه الخطأ. فهذه المقدمات الثلاث تفيد احتياج الناس فى التحرز عن الخطأ في الفكر الى قانون وذلك هو المنطق. وعلم من هذا تعريف المنطق ايضاً بانه : قانون تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطاء في الفكر.
So there must be a universal rule, if observed, error does not occur in thought, and that is logic. It has been established that people are in need of logic for protection from error in thought by three premises: the first: that knowledge is either a concept or an assent. The second: that each of them is either obtained without reflection or obtained by reflection. The third: that reflection may contain error. So these three premises indicate people's need, in guarding against error in thought, for a law, and that is logic. And it is known from this the definition of logic as well: it is a law, the observance of which protects the mind from error in thought.
Source pages p. 13

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of logic. The author first asserts that a universal rule is needed to avoid error in thought, and identifies that rule as logic. He then provides three premises that collectively demonstrate humanity's need for such a rule. The first premise divides knowledge into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq); the second says each may be acquired without reflection (bi-la nazar) or through reflection; the third warns that reflection can be erroneous. From these, it follows that a law (qanun) is necessary to prevent error, and that law is logic. The author then restates the definition of logic as 'a law whose observance protects the mind from error in thought.' Note the argument is compound: it is a syllogism with three premises leading to a conclusion, but the author treats them as a straightforward indication.

What Is Mantiq?

The Binary of Knowledge: Tasawwur and Tasdiq
First premise divides knowledge into concept and assent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between tasawwur (concept) and tasdiq (assent) and give examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
All knowledge is either a concept or a judgment – these are the two fundamental mental acts.

The first premise of the argument states that knowledge (al-'ilm) is either tasawwur (concept) or tasdiq (assent). This binary is foundational in Islamic logic. Understanding this distinction helps you see that logic deals with both forming concepts and affirming propositions. In Islamic sciences, such as tafsir, you need both: understanding the meaning of words (tasawwur) and affirming the truth of statements (tasdiq).

Guided Path
1
Find the first premise in the text
Identify the first premise: 'that knowledge is either a concept or an assent.' This is the division of knowledge into two categories.
The author treats this as self-evident.
2
Define tasawwur and tasdiq
Tasawwur is simple apprehension of an object without judgment, e.g., the concept of 'human'. Tasdiq is assent to a proposition, e.g., 'human is rational'. Tasdiq always involves a judgment (hukm); tasawwur does not.
Some later logicians add a third category, but the classical tradition holds to this binary.
3
Give examples from Islamic sciences
In tafsir, understanding the lexical meaning of a Qur'anic word is tasawwur; affirming that a particular interpretation is correct is tasdiq. In fiqh, knowing the definition of 'salah' is tasawwur; saying 'salah is obligatory' is tasdiq.
Logic provides rules for both: definition for tasawwur, argumentation for tasdiq.
4
Understand why this division matters
The division sets the scope of logic: logic is not only about arguments (tasdiq) but also about definitions and concepts (tasawwur). The three-premise argument for logic's necessity covers both, since both can be obtained through reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فلابد من قاعدة كلية لو روعيت لم يقع الخطأ فى الفكر و هو المنطق. فقد ثبت احتياج الناس الى المنطق فى العصمة عن الخطأ فى الفكر بثلاث مقدمات: الاولى : ان العلم اما تصور واما تصديق . الثانية : ان كلا منهما اما ان يحصل بلا نظر او حصل بالنظر. الثالثة : ان النظر قد يقع فيه الخطأ. فهذه المقدمات الثلاث تفيد احتياج الناس فى التحرز عن الخطأ في الفكر الى قانون وذلك هو المنطق. وعلم من هذا تعريف المنطق ايضاً بانه : قانون تعصم مراعاتها الذهن عن الخطاء في الفكر.
So there must be a universal rule, if observed, error does not occur in thought, and that is logic. It has been established that people are in need of logic for protection from error in thought by three premises: the first: that knowledge is either a concept or an assent. The second: that each of them is either obtained without reflection or obtained by reflection. The third: that reflection may contain error. So these three premises indicate people's need, in guarding against error in thought, for a law, and that is logic. And it is known from this the definition of logic as well: it is a law, the observance of which protects the mind from error in thought.

Commentary
This passage is a proof for the necessity of logic. The author first asserts that a universal rule is needed to avoid error in thought, and identifies that rule as logic. He then provides three premises that collectively demonstrate humanity's need for such a rule. The first premise divides knowledge into concept (tasawwur) and assent (tasdiq); the second says each may be acquired without reflection (bi-la nazar) or through reflection; the third warns that reflection can be erroneous. From these, it follows that a law (qanun) is necessary to prevent error, and that law is logic. The author then restates the definition of logic as 'a law whose observance protects the mind from error in thought.' Note the argument is compound: it is a syllogism with three premises leading to a conclusion, but the author treats them as a straightforward indication.

Definition of al-Ma'rif and al-Hujja
Author defines ma'rif and hujja as tools leading to unknown conceptual or assertoric ends.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish al-ma'rif from al-hujja and determine whether a given statement qualifies as a logical tool based on its function of leading to an unknown.

Core Idea
Not every known thing is a logical tool; only those that lead to an unknown count as ma'rif or hujja.

In this passage, the author defines two fundamental tools of logic: al-ma'rif (definition) for conceptual ends and al-hujja (proof) for assertoric ends. The key condition is that these tools must lead from a known to an unknown. We will examine how to apply this condition and distinguish valid tools from mere known facts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two logical tools
Locate the terms al-ma'rif and al-hujja in the Arabic text. Note that both are introduced as 'known matters' (ma'lum) that serve a specific function: leading to an unknown (majhul).
2
Understand the condition for each tool
For al-ma'rif: it must be a known conceptual matter that leads to the conception of an unknown essence (e.g., 'rational animal' leads to 'human'). For al-hujja: it must be a known assertoric matter that leads to assent of an unknown claim (e.g., the syllogism leads to 'the world is temporally originated').
Passage explicitly states: 'but not absolutely either; rather insofar as it leads to the unknown assertoric [thing]'.
3
Apply the condition to examples
Read the examples given: 'Zayd' and 'Amr' are known but do not lead to an unknown conceptual matter, so they are not ma'rif. 'Fire is hot' is known but does not lead to an unknown assertoric, so it is not hujja. Contrast with 'rational animal' and the syllogism.
4
Summarize the logician's focus
The logician does not study known things that do not lead to unknowns. Instead, logic investigates how to properly arrange ma'rif and hujja to reach the unknown.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حيث انه يوصل الى مطلوب تصوري فيسمى معرفاً (ه) او مطلوب تصديقى فيسمى حجة (ه) المجهول التصورى كالحيوان الناطق الموصل الى تصور الانسان و اما المعلوم التصوري الذى لا يوصل الى المجهول التصورى فلايسمى معرفاً و المنطق لا يبحث عنه (۷۹) كالامور الجزئية المعلومة نحو زيد و عمرو و اما الحجة فهى عبارة عن المعلوم التصديقى لكن لا مطلقا ايضاً بل من حيث انه يوصل الى المجهول التصديق كقولنا : العالم متغير و كل متغير حادث الموصل الى التصديق بقولنا: العالم حادث و اما ما لا يوصل كقولنا النار حارة مثلا فليس بحجة والمنطقى لا ينظر فيه بل يبحث عن المعرف و الحجة من حيث انهما كيف ينبغي ان يترتبا حتى يوصلا الى المجهول ( ۸۰ )
Since it leads to a conceptual desired [thing], it is called a ma'rif; or to an assertoric desired [thing], it is called a hujja. The unknown conceptual [thing] is like 'rational animal' which leads to the conception of 'human'. As for the known conceptual [thing] that does not lead to the unknown conceptual [thing], it is not called a ma'rif, and logic does not discuss it, like particular known matters such as Zayd and 'Amr. As for the hujja, it is the assertoric known [thing], but not absolutely either; rather insofar as it leads to the unknown assertoric [thing], like our statement: 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated' which leads to the assertion of our statement: 'The world is temporally originated'. As for what does not lead, like our statement 'Fire is hot' for example, it is not a hujja, and the logician does not examine it. Rather, he investigates the ma'rif and hujja in terms of how they should be arranged so that they lead to the unknown.
Source pages p. 14

Commentary
This passage defines two key terms in logic: al-ma'rif (definition) and al-hujja (proof). The author explains that a conceptual tool is called a ma'rif only if it leads to a conceptual unknown. For example, the phrase 'rational animal' leads to the concept of 'human'. If it does not lead to an unknown, like particular names 'Zayd' and 'Amr', it is not a ma'rif and logic does not study it. Similarly, an assertoric tool is called a hujja only if it leads to an assertoric unknown. The author gives the example of the syllogism: 'The world is changing, everything changing is temporally originated' which leads to the conclusion 'The world is temporally originated'. In contrast, a statement like 'Fire is hot' does not lead to an unknown, so it is not a hujja. The logician studies how to arrange these tools to reach the unknown.

Key Terms
المعرف (al-Ma'rif): A known conceptual matter that leads to the conception of an unknown essence, e.g., 'rational animal' for 'human'. [Note: No significant shift.]
الحجة (al-Hujja): A known assertoric matter that leads to the assent of an unknown claim, e.g., a syllogism proving the world's temporality. [Note: In some later texts, hujja is restricted to certain types of argument; here it is broad.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) 'Fire is hot.'
C) 'Every intoxicant is forbidden, every nabidh is an intoxicant, therefore nabidh is forbidden.'
D) 'The world is changing, and everything changing is temporally originated.'

Key Takeaway
A logical tool (ma'rif or hujja) is defined not merely by being known, but by its function of leading from a known to an unknown.

What is Indication? The Three Types of Dalaala
Author defines dalaala and divides it into wad'iyya, tabi'iyya, 'aqliyya.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three types of dalaala (wad'iyya, tabi'iyya, 'aqliyya) and classify examples from Islamic sciences.

Core Idea
Indication is the relation where one thing leads to knowledge of another; its types depend on the cause: placement, nature, or reason.

Before studying arguments, we must understand how words convey meaning. The Islamic logical tradition starts with dalaala – the relation between a sign and what it signifies. This lesson covers the general definition of dalaala and its threefold division based on the cause of indication. You will learn to distinguish each type and understand why logic focuses on conventional verbal indication.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition
Indication (dalaala) is defined as 'a thing such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of something else.' This is a relational concept: the indicator (dal) and the indicated (madlool).
The definition is from the passage: 'كون الشيء بحيث يلزم من العلم به العلم بشيء آخر'.
2
Identify the cause of indication
The division of dalaala into three types is based on what establishes the relation: human placement (wad'), natural concomitance (tab'), or rational inference ('aql).
The passage lists examples for each: words for wad'iyya, sigh for tabi'iyya, smoke for 'aqliyya.
3
Apply to Islamic examples
Given an indication, classify it: e.g., the word 'Zayd' indicating its owner is wad'iyya; a yawn indicating sleepiness is tabi'iyya; footprints indicating a person is 'aqliyya. Practice with examples from the Qur'an and hadith.
Use real examples: the word 'Allah' (wad'iyya), the sound of crying (tabi'iyya), the design of the universe indicating a Creator ('aqliyya).
4
Understand the focus of logic
The passage states that the investigation in mantiq is restricted to conventional verbal indication (al-dalaala al-lafdhiyya al-wad'iyya) because it is the basis of communication and reasoning.
Natural and rational indications are studied in other sciences like medicine or theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

In indications: conformity and on its part inclusion and on the external entailment; and there must be in it [entailment] mental or customary entailment; and it entails conformity even if by estimation. And benefit and acquisition [of knowledge] are only in utterances through indication, therefore the mention of indication was begun. And indication is: a thing being such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of something else; the first is the indicator and the second is the indicated. And if the indicator is a word, then the indication is verbal, otherwise non-verbal. And each of them, if it is due to the placement of the positor and his setting the first for the second, then it is conventional, like the indication of the word Zayd on its essence and the indication of the four terms on their meanings. And if it is due to the requirement of nature, like the occurrence of the indicator upon the occurrence of the indicated, then it is natural, like the indication of 'ah ah' on chest pain and the indication of rapid pulse on fever. And if it is due to something other than placement and nature, then it is mental, like the indication of the sound heard from behind the wall on the existence of the speaker, like the indication of smoke on fire. So the divisions of indication are six. And what is intended by investigation here from them is the verbal conventional indication, for upon it are based benefit and acquisition. And it is divided into conformity, inclusion, and entailment, because the indication of a word due to the positor's placement is either on the entirety of what it is placed for, or on its part, or on what is external to it and necessary for it. And his statement: 'and there must be in it' i.e., in indication of entailment, the entailment indication. His statement: 'of entailment' i.e., the external matter being such that it is impossible to conceive the subject matter without it; whether this mental entailment is rational like sight in relation to blindness, or customary like generosity in relation to Hatim. His statement: 'and it entails conformity, even if by estimation': for there is no doubt that conventional indication on... and no reverse. And the subject, if by part of it the indication of part of the meaning is intended, then it is compound; the part of the named and its necessary are branches of the indication of the named, whether the indication of the named is realized by the utterance being spoken and the named intended and the part or necessary understood as subordinate, or estimated as when the word becomes famous for the part or necessary, so the indication of the subject matter, even if it is not actually realized there, yet it is realized by estimation, meaning that this word has a meaning that if it were intended from the word, its indication upon it would be conformity, and to this he pointed by his statement 'even if by estimation'. His statement: 'and no reverse': because it is permissible for a word to have a simple meaning that has no part and no necessary, so then conformity is realized without inclusion and entailment; and if it has a compound meaning with no necessary, inclusion is realized without entailment; and if it has a simple meaning with no mental necessary like the sun, entailment is realized without inclusion; so the mutual entailment does not occur in any of the two sides.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage from the Yazdi Hashiya is a commentary explaining the division of indication (dalaala). It opens by stating that a word's indication can be by conformity (mutabaqa) to the whole meaning, by inclusion (tadammun) of a part, or by entailment (iltizam) of something external. Entailment requires a mental or customary link, not just any connection. The commentator clarifies that both inclusion and entailment presuppose conformity, even if only in a hypothetical sense: if the word had been used for the whole, it would have indicated it by conformity. The passage then defines dalaala generally as a relation where knowledge of one thing leads to knowledge of another. The indicator can be verbal or non-verbal. The cause of the relation divides dalaala into three: conventional (wad'iyya) due to human placement, natural (tabi'iyya) due to a natural concomitance like a sigh indicating pain, and rational ('aqliyya) due to a mental inference like smoke indicating fire. The focus of logic is only on the conventional verbal indication, which is further divided into the three types mentioned. The commentary then elaborates on the condition of luzum for entailment: it must be rational (e.g., blindness necessarily involves absence of sight) or customary (e.g., Hatim's name entails generosity). Finally, it explains that conformity is always present in inclusion and entailment, either explicitly or implicitly. The 'no reverse' statement confirms that conformity can exist without inclusion or entailment when the meaning is simple with no parts or necessary concomitants, as with the word 'sun'.

Key Terms
الدلالة
الدلالة الوضعية
الدلالة الطبيعية
الدلالة العقلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The definition of dalaala and its three-way division into conventional, natural, and rational provide the foundational framework for understanding how signs convey meaning in logic.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
10 min
The Three Types of Conventional Indication: Mutabaqa, Tadammun, Iltizam
Author divides conventional verbal indication into conformity, inclusion, entailment, and explains condition of luzum.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam in any conventional verbal indication and test the condition of luzum for iltizam.

Core Idea
A word indicates its whole meaning (mutabaqa), a part (tadammun), or an external necessary (iltizam); the latter requires a rational or customary link.

In the previous lesson, we learned that conventional verbal indication is the main concern of logic. This lesson dives into its three subdivisions: mutabaqa (conformity), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (entailment). These categories allow us to analyze exactly how a word relates to its meaning. You will learn to identify each type and understand the crucial condition of luzum for entailment.

Guided Path
1
Recall the definition of conventional indication
Conventional indication is due to human placement (wad'). The word is assigned to a meaning by convention.
This is the basis for the three types.
2
Distinguish the three directions
The word's indication can be: (a) on the entire meaning it was assigned for (mutabaqa), (b) on a part of that meaning (tadammun), or (c) on something external that is necessarily connected to that meaning (iltizam).
Examples: 'insan' (human) mutabaqa = animal + rational; tadammun = animal alone; iltizam = capacity for knowledge.
3
Apply the condition of luzum for iltizam
The external thing must be such that it is impossible to conceive the original meaning without it. This luzum can be rational (e.g., blindness implies absence of sight) or customary (e.g., Hatim implies generosity).
Not every external association qualifies; there must be necessity in the mind or custom.
4
Practice with Islamic examples
Take the word 'القرآن' (Qur'an): mutabaqa = the revealed book; tadammun = its words; iltizam = divine origin (rational) or guidance (customary).
Use examples from tafsir: when a word is used figuratively for a part, the full meaning is still implied (conformity is estimated).

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

In indications: conformity and on its part inclusion and on the external entailment; and there must be in it [entailment] mental or customary entailment; and it entails conformity even if by estimation. And benefit and acquisition [of knowledge] are only in utterances through indication, therefore the mention of indication was begun. And indication is: a thing being such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of something else; the first is the indicator and the second is the indicated. And if the indicator is a word, then the indication is verbal, otherwise non-verbal. And each of them, if it is due to the placement of the positor and his setting the first for the second, then it is conventional, like the indication of the word Zayd on its essence and the indication of the four terms on their meanings. And if it is due to the requirement of nature, like the occurrence of the indicator upon the occurrence of the indicated, then it is natural, like the indication of 'ah ah' on chest pain and the indication of rapid pulse on fever. And if it is due to something other than placement and nature, then it is mental, like the indication of the sound heard from behind the wall on the existence of the speaker, like the indication of smoke on fire. So the divisions of indication are six. And what is intended by investigation here from them is the verbal conventional indication, for upon it are based benefit and acquisition. And it is divided into conformity, inclusion, and entailment, because the indication of a word due to the positor's placement is either on the entirety of what it is placed for, or on its part, or on what is external to it and necessary for it. And his statement: 'and there must be in it' i.e., in indication of entailment, the entailment indication. His statement: 'of entailment' i.e., the external matter being such that it is impossible to conceive the subject matter without it; whether this mental entailment is rational like sight in relation to blindness, or customary like generosity in relation to Hatim. His statement: 'and it entails conformity, even if by estimation': for there is no doubt that conventional indication on... and no reverse. And the subject, if by part of it the indication of part of the meaning is intended, then it is compound; the part of the named and its necessary are branches of the indication of the named, whether the indication of the named is realized by the utterance being spoken and the named intended and the part or necessary understood as subordinate, or estimated as when the word becomes famous for the part or necessary, so the indication of the subject matter, even if it is not actually realized there, yet it is realized by estimation, meaning that this word has a meaning that if it were intended from the word, its indication upon it would be conformity, and to this he pointed by his statement 'even if by estimation'. His statement: 'and no reverse': because it is permissible for a word to have a simple meaning that has no part and no necessary, so then conformity is realized without inclusion and entailment; and if it has a compound meaning with no necessary, inclusion is realized without entailment; and if it has a simple meaning with no mental necessary like the sun, entailment is realized without inclusion; so the mutual entailment does not occur in any of the two sides.
Source pages p. 18

Commentary
This passage from the Yazdi Hashiya is a commentary explaining the division of indication (dalaala). It opens by stating that a word's indication can be by conformity (mutabaqa) to the whole meaning, by inclusion (tadammun) of a part, or by entailment (iltizam) of something external. Entailment requires a mental or customary link, not just any connection. The commentator clarifies that both inclusion and entailment presuppose conformity, even if only in a hypothetical sense: if the word had been used for the whole, it would have indicated it by conformity. The passage then defines dalaala generally as a relation where knowledge of one thing leads to knowledge of another. The indicator can be verbal or non-verbal. The cause of the relation divides dalaala into three: conventional (wad'iyya) due to human placement, natural (tabi'iyya) due to a natural concomitance like a sigh indicating pain, and rational ('aqliyya) due to a mental inference like smoke indicating fire. The focus of logic is only on the conventional verbal indication, which is further divided into the three types mentioned. The commentary then elaborates on the condition of luzum for entailment: it must be rational (e.g., blindness necessarily involves absence of sight) or customary (e.g., Hatim's name entails generosity). Finally, it explains that conformity is always present in inclusion and entailment, either explicitly or implicitly. The 'no reverse' statement confirms that conformity can exist without inclusion or entailment when the meaning is simple with no parts or necessary concomitants, as with the word 'sun'.

التضمن
الالتزام
اللزوم العقلي
اللزوم العرفي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what condition is necessary for iltizam (entailment) to be valid?
A) The external matter must be mentioned in the same sentence.
B) The external matter must be impossible to conceive without the subject.
C) The external matter must be a part of the meaning.
D) The external matter must be a natural occurrence.

Key Takeaway
The three types of conventional indication – mutabaqa, tadammun, iltizam – provide a precise analytical tool for understanding how a word relates to its meaning, with iltizam requiring a necessary mental or customary link.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
For the Arabic word 'صلاة' (prayer), identify examples of mutabaqa, tadammun, and iltizam. (Hint: mutabaqa = the act of worship; tadammun = the specific movements; iltizam = submission to Allah or prohibition of indecency, as implied in the Quran.)
?
Test the condition of luzum: Is the connection between 'fire' and 'heat' rational or customary? Is the connection between 'Kaba' and 'qibla' rational or customary? Refer to the passage's criteria.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
The Priority of Conformity: Why Tadammun and Iltizam Depend on Mutabaqa
Author explains that inclusion and entailment entail conformity even if by estimation, and that conformity can stand alone.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why both tadammun and iltizam presuppose mutabaqa, even if only in a hypothetical sense.

Core Idea
Inclusion and entailment are parasitic on conformity – the full meaning is always the benchmark, even when a part or necessary is intended.

In the previous lesson, we saw that the three divisions of conventional indication are distinct. However, the commentator makes a crucial point: both tadammun and iltizam always entail mutabaqa, even if only 'by estimation'. This lesson explores that relationship and its consequences. Understanding this helps us see how language works: when we use a word for a part or something associated, the full meaning remains in the background as a reference.

Guided Path
1
Recall the three types
We already know mutabaqa (whole), tadammun (part), iltizam (external necessary).
2
Understand the claim 'يلزمها المطابقة ولو تقديرا'
The commentator asserts that both tadammun and iltizam require conformity to the full meaning, even if only in a hypothetical sense. This means that the full meaning is always assumed as the basis for the part or necessary.
For example, when we say 'human' to mean 'rational animal', the full meaning is present. If we use 'human' to mean just 'animal' (tadammun), we still presuppose the full meaning.
3
Test the 'no reverse' rule
Conformity can exist without inclusion or entailment (if the meaning is simple, with no parts and no necessary external). But inclusion and entailment cannot exist without conformity. This is the 'لا عكس' statement.
Example: 'moon' has a simple meaning – no part meaning, and its necessary (e.g., reflected light) may not be a necessary entailment? The passage gives 'sun' as an example of simple with no mental necessary? We'll use 'sun' as simple.
4
Apply to Islamic text analysis
When interpreting a Quranic verse, if a word is used metaphorically for a part of its meaning (e.g., 'hand' meaning 'power'), the full meaning ('hand' as limb) is still the assumed reference point, even if not explicitly intended.
This is why figurative language only works when the original meaning is understood as the standard.

What Is Mantiq?

Types of Transferred Terms: Shar'i, 'Urfi, Istilahi
Defines the three categories of transferred terms based on the type of transmitter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given transferred term as shar'i, 'urfi, or istilahi based on the transmitter.

Core Idea
A term's meaning can be transferred by Shari'ah, common custom, or technical convention, defining three types.

In this passage, the author explains how technical terms in the Islamic sciences acquire new meanings through transfer (naql). The transfer is always effected by a transmitter (nāqil), which can be either the Shari'ah, the common people ('urf 'āmm), or a specialized group ('urf khāṣṣ). Understanding this classification helps you trace the origin of key terms in fiqh, kalām, and grammar.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term as manqūl
Confirm that the term is a transferred term, i.e., its meaning has been shifted from its original linguistic sense to a new one.
2
Determine the transmitter (nāqil)
Ask: Who performed the transfer? The Shari'ah (Lawgiver), the common people at large, or a specialized group (e.g., grammarians, jurists)?
3
Apply the corresponding label
If the transmitter is the Shari'ah, the term is shar'ī; if common custom, 'urfī; if a technical convention, istilāhī.
4
Test with Islamic examples
For instance, 'ṣalāh' (prayer) was transferred by the Shari'ah → shar'ī. 'Dābbah' (riding animal) was transferred by common custom → 'urfī. 'Fi'l' (verb) was transferred by grammarian convention → istilāhī.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في المفرد والمركب واقسامها المنقول إليه فهذا الناقل إما أهل الشرع أو أهل العرف العام أو أهل العرف الخاص واصطلاح خاص كالنحوي مثلاً فعلى الأول يسمى منقولاً شرعياً وعلى الثاني عرفياً وعلى الثالث اصطلاحياً وإلى هذا أشار بقوله: «ينسب إلى الناقل».
On the simple and the compound and their divisions. The transferred-to: so this transmitter is either the people of the Shari'ah, or the people of common custom, or the people of special custom and a particular technical convention, like the grammarian for example. So according to the first it is called a Shari'ah-transferred term, according to the second a customary-transferred, and according to the third a technical-transferred. And to this he alludes by his saying: 'It is attributed to the transmitter.'
Source pages p. 24

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of 'manqul' (transferred term) and its three divisions based on the transmitter. The transmitter is either the people of the Shari'ah (i.e., the Lawgiver), the people of common custom, or the people of a specialised technical convention such as grammarians. Accordingly, the term is called shar'ī, 'urfī, or istilāhī. This classification is important in understanding the technical vocabulary in the Islamic sciences, as many terms have been transferred from their original linguistic meanings to new specialized meanings. The author notes that the original text alludes to this by the statement 'it is attributed to the transmitter'.

Key Terms
المنقول (al-manqūl): A term whose meaning has been transferred from its original linguistic meaning to a new meaning due to the practice of the Shari'ah, common custom, or a technical convention. [Note: This term is stable across the tradition.]
الناقل (al-nāqil): The authority or group that effects the transfer of a term's meaning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Shar'i
B) 'Urfi
C) Istilahi
D) Manqul

Key Takeaway
Transferred terms are classified based on the transmitter: Shari'ah gives shar'ī, common custom gives 'urfī, and technical convention gives istilāhī.

What Is Mantiq?

Three Aspects of a Single Meaning: Mafhum, Ma'na, Madlul
Author defines concept as what is understood, and distinguishes concept, meaning, and denoted.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-mafhūm, al-maʿnā, and al-madlūl for any given utterance, and explain the different considerations that produce these three aspects.

Core Idea
Concept, meaning, and denoted are three facets of one mental content, viewed from understanding, intention, and indication.

This lesson introduces a foundational distinction in Islamic logic: the same mental content derived from an utterance can be considered from three perspectives. When we hear a statement, we understand something (concept), recognize that the speaker intends that something (meaning), and note that the words point to it (denoted). Recognizing these aspects helps us analyze definitions and arguments with precision.

Guided Path
1
Identify the utterance and its understood content
Take any meaningful Arabic utterance, for example 'إن الله على كل شيء قدير'. What mental content is obtained in the intellect when you hear it? That is the mafhum.
The mafhum is the raw comprehension, before considering intention.
2
Consider the utterance as intended by the speaker
Now shift perspective: the speaker chose this utterance to convey a specific purpose. The same content, now viewed as intended, is called ma'na. Ask: what does the speaker mean to communicate?
Ma'na adds the dimension of speaker's intent.
3
Consider the utterance as indicating a thing
Finally, note that the utterance is a sign (dalil) pointing to that content. The content under this consideration is the madlul. The utterance indicates it; the relationship is that of dalaala.
Madlul emphasizes the signifier-signified link.
4
Apply the three terms to an Islamic example
Use the Shahada: 'لا إله إلا الله'. The mafhum is the meaning 'There is no deity but Allah' as understood. The ma'na is that same meaning as intended by the speaker to affirm tawhid. The madlul is that meaning as indicated by the words of the Shahada.
All three refer to the same mental content but highlight different relations.

قوله « المفهوم» ای ما حصل عند العقل. و اعلم ان ما يستفاد من اللفظ باعتبار انه يفهم منه يسمى مفهوما، وباعتبار ان هذا المستفاد من اللفظ مقصود منه يسمى معنى (من عناه اذا قصده)، وباعتبار ان اللفظ دال عليه يسمى مدلولا.
His saying 'المفهوم' i.e. what is obtained in the intellect. Know that what is derived from an utterance in consideration of being understood from it is called concept (مفهوم), and in consideration of this derived thing being intended from it is called meaning (معنى), and in consideration of the utterance indicating it is called denoted (مدلول).
Source pages p. 25

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'concept' (المفهوم) as what is obtained in the intellect. The author then explains that from an utterance we can consider three aspects: first, what is understood from it is called 'concept' (مفهوم); second, the understood thing insofar as it is intended by the speaker is called 'meaning' (معنى); third, the utterance's indication of it is called 'denoted' (مدلول). This is a foundational classification in Islamic logic, showing that the same mental content can be viewed from different perspectives: as understood, as intended, and as indicated. All three are aspects of the same content but highlight different relations to the utterance and the speaker.

Key Terms
المفهوم
المعنى
المدلول

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) المفهوم
B) المعنى
C) المدلول
D) اللفظ

Key Takeaway
Concept, meaning, and denoted are three aspects of the same mental content corresponding to understanding, intention, and indication.

What Is Mantiq?
intermediate
8 min
Conditions for Contradiction
Author states the three required differences and the eight unities for contradiction.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three required differences (quality, quantity, modality) and the eight unities for a valid contradiction between two categorical propositions.

Core Idea
Contradiction requires difference in quality, quantity, and modality, but unity in everything else.

Before you can find the contradictory of any proposition, you must understand what makes two propositions contradictory in the first place. This lesson unpacks the classical conditions for tanāquḍ: the two propositions must differ in quality (affirmative/negative), quantity (universal/particular), and modality (necessary/possible, etc.), while being identical in all other respects. These conditions ensure that the pair cannot be both true or both false.

Guided Path
1
Identify quality difference
Check that one proposition is affirmative and the other negative. If both are affirmative or both negative, they are not contradictories – they could both be true or both false.
2
Identify quantity difference (for quantified propositions)
If the propositions are quantified (universal or particular), they must differ in quantity. For example, 'Every S is P' and 'Some S is not P' differ in quantity (universal vs. particular) and quality.
3
Identify modality difference (for modal propositions)
If the propositions are modal (necessary, possible, perpetual, etc.), they must differ in modality. Two necessary propositions may both be false, and two possible propositions may both be true.
4
Verify unity in all other aspects
Ensure the two propositions share the same subject, predicate, time, place, condition, part/whole relation, potentiality/actuality, and addition. These are the eight unities mentioned in the verse.
The eight unities are: condition, addition, part and whole, subject, predicate, place, potentiality and actuality, and time.
