On contradiction. And it is necessary that there be difference in quantity, quality, and modality, and unity in other than these. His saying 'and unity in other than these' means: and it is required in contradiction that the two propositions be united in other than the three mentioned matters, namely quantity, quality, and modality. And the negation of every necessity is precisely the possibility of the opposite side. So the contradictory of the necessity of affirmation is the possibility of negation (14), and the contradictory of the necessity of negation is the possibility of affirmation. And the contradictory of perpetualness is the negation of perpetualness, and you already know that it necessitates the actuality of the opposite side. So the removal of perpetualness of affirmation necessitates the actuality of negation, and the removal of perpetualness of negation necessitates the actuality of affirmation. Thus the general possible is the explicit contradictory of the absolute necessary (15), and the general absolute is a necessary concomitant of the contradictory of the perpetual absolute. And since its explicit contradictory, which is non-perpetualness, is not a well-established concept considered among common propositions, they said: the contradictory of the perpetual absolute is the general absolute (16). Then know that the ratio of the possible temporal to the general conditioned is like the ratio of the general possible to the necessary (17). So this is the meaning of the absolute temporal that differs from the conventional proposition in quality. Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions for contradiction (tanāquḍ) in categorical propositions and the determination of contradictories for various modal and composite propositions. The author begins by stating the general rule: contradiction requires difference (ikhtilāf) in quantity (kamm), quality (kayf), and modality (jiha), and unity (ittiḥād) in all other aspects. He then expands on each condition. The condition of difference in quality means one must be affirmative and the other negative, because two affirmatives or two negatives can both be true or both false. For quantified propositions, they must also differ in quantity (e.g., universal vs. particular). For modalized propositions, they must differ in modality (e.g., necessary vs. possible), since two necessary propositions can both be false (e.g., 'Every human is a writer necessarily' and 'No human is a writer necessarily' are both false), and two possible propositions can both be true (e.g., 'Every human is a writer possible' and 'No human is a writer possible' are both true). The unity condition is codified in eight matters, presented in a Persian verse: unity of condition, addition, part and whole, subject, predicate, place, potentiality and actuality, and time. Next, the author explains how to find the contradictory of modal propositions. The contradictory of any proposition is its removal (raf'). For a necessary proposition, the contradictory is a proposition that negates that necessity, which is equivalent to the possibility of the opposite. Thus, the contradictory of 'necessarily A is B' is 'possibly A is not B'. For a perpetual proposition (dā'ima), its contradictory is the negation of perpetualness, which entails the actuality of the opposite, but since 'non-perpetual' is not a standard modal term, the contradictory is taken as the absolute proposition (muṭlaqa). The same logic applies to conditional (sharṭiyya) and temporal (ḥīniyya) propositions. For composite propositions (murakkaba), which combine two modalities, the contradictory is a disjunctive proposition that asserts the negation of one component without specifying which (but not both, i.e., exclusive disjunction). For example, the contradictory of 'necessarily A is B as long as it is C, but not perpetually' is 'either possibly A is not B when it is C, or A is B perpetually'. The author ends by noting that the contradictory of a particular composite cannot be a simple disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, because both may be false while the original is false, as shown with an example from animal/human.

Key Takeaway
For two propositions to be contradictories, they must differ in quality, quantity, and modality (if quantified and modal), while being united in subject, predicate, time, place, condition, part/whole, potentiality/actuality, and addition.

What Is Mantiq?
advanced

Contradictories of Modal Propositions
Author explains how to derive contradictories for necessary, perpetual, conditioned, and temporal propositions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the correct contradictory of any simple modal proposition (necessary, perpetual, conditioned, temporal) using the principle of removal (raf').

Core Idea
The contradictory of a modal proposition negates its modality, not just its quality, yielding the possibility of the opposite.

Now that we know the conditions for contradiction, we need to apply them to modal propositions. The key insight is that the contradictory of a proposition is its removal (raf'). For modal propositions, removing the modality leads to a proposition of the opposite possibility or a simple absolute, depending on the type. This lesson walks through the rules for necessary, perpetual, conditioned, and temporal propositions, as explained in the passage.

Guided Path
1
Recall the principle: nāqīḍ kulli shay'in raf'uhu
The contradictory of something is its removal. For a proposition, removal means negating the judgment while preserving unity conditions.
2
Find the contradictory of a necessary proposition
The contradictory of 'necessarily S is P' is 'possibly S is not P'. This negates the necessity, which is equivalent to affirming the possibility of the opposite.
Example: 'Every human is a writer necessarily' -> 'Some human is not a writer possibly'.
3
Find the contradictory of a perpetual proposition
The contradictory of 'always S is P' is not 'not always S is P' (that is not a standard modal form). Instead, it is the absolute proposition 'S is P at some time' (general absolute), which is the necessary concomitant of negating perpetualness.
Example: 'Every human is an animal perpetually' -> 'Some human is not an animal at some time' (absolute).
4
Find the contradictory of a conditioned proposition
The contradictory of 'as long as S is described by D, S is P necessarily' is the possible temporal: 'some S, when described by D, is not P possibly'.
Example: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer' -> 'Some writer does not move his fingers when he is a writer, possibly'.

(۱۶) ثم اعلم ان نسبة الحينية الممكنة الى المشروطة العامة كنسبة الممكنة العامة الى الضرورية (۱۷) فان الحينية الممكنة هي التي حكم فيها بسلب الضرورة الوصفية اى الضرورة مادام الوصف عن الجانب المخالف فتكون الحينية الممكنة و للعرفية العامة، الحينية المطلقة و للمركبة، المفهوم المردد بين نقيضي للجزئين و لكن فى الجزئية بالنسبة الى كل فرد فرد. نقيضاً صريحاً لما حكم فيها بضرورة الجانب الموافق بحسب الوصف فقولنا بالضرورة كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع مادام كاتباً، نقيضه : ليس بعض الكاتب بمتحرك الاصابع حين هو كاتب بالامكان و نسبة الحينية المطلقة وهى قضية حكم فيها بفعلية النسبة حين اتصالات ذات الموضوع بالوصف العنوانى الى العرفية العامة كنسبة المطلقة العامة الى الدائمة (۱۸) و ذلك لان الحكم فى العرفية العامة بدوام النسبة مادام ذات الموضوع متصفاً بالوصف العنواني فنقيضها الصريح (۱۹) هو سلب ذلك الدوام و يلزمه وقوع الطرف المقابل فى اوقات الوصف العنوانى فهذا معنى الحينية المطلقة المخالفة للقضية العرفية فى الكيف فنقيض قولنا بالدوام كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع مادام كاتباً، قولنا : ليس بعض الكاتب بمتحرك الاصابع حين هو كاتب بالفعل. والمصنف لم يتعرض لبيان نقيضى الوقتية والمنتشرة المطلقتين من البسايط اذ لا يتعلق بذلك (٢٠) غرض (۲۱) فيما سيأتى من مباحث العكوس والأقيسة بخلاف باقى البسايط فتأمل . (۲۲) كلا الخلو قوله « وللمركبة»: قد علمت ان نقيض كل شيء رفعه فاعلم ان رفع المركب انما يكون برفع احد جزئيه (۲۳) لا على التعيين (۲۴) بل على سبيل منع اذ يجوز (۲۵) ان يكون برفع جزئيه فنقيض القضية المركبة نقيض احد جزئيه (٢٦) على سبيل منع الخلو فنقيض قولنا : كل كاتب متحرك الاصابع بالضرورة مادام كاتباً لا دائماً اى لاشيء من الكاتب بمتحرك الاصابع بالفعل، قضية منفصلة مانعة الخلو (۲۷) و هى قولنا اما بعض الكاتب ليس بمتحرك الاصابع بالامكان حين هو كاتب و اما بعض الكاتب متحرك الاصابع دائماً. و انت بعد اطلاعك على حقايق المركبات ونقائض البسائط تتمكن من استخراج التفاصيل . (۲۸) قوله « ولكن فى الجزئية بالنسبة الى كل فرد «فرد» یعنى لا يكفي في اخذ نقيض القضية المركبة الجزئية الترديد بين نقيضى جزئيها و هما الكليتان، اذ قد يكذب وهما المركبة الجزئية كقوننا بعض الحيوان انسان بالفعل لا دائماً (۲۹) و يكذب كلا نقیضی جزئيها (۳۰) ايضاً و هما قولنا: لاشيء من الحيوان بانسان دائماً وقولنا كل
On contradiction. And it is necessary that there be difference in quantity, quality, and modality, and unity in other than these. And the contradictory of the necessary, the general possible, and of the perpetual, the general absolute, and of the general conditioned in quantity (4) as the author will clarify. His saying 'And it is necessary that there be difference' means: it is required in contradiction that one of the two contradictories be affirmative and the other negative, necessarily because two affirmatives and likewise two negatives (the two negatives? error) may both be true or both false (5). Then if the two propositions are quantified, their difference in quantity is also required (7) as previously, then if they are modalized, their difference in modality is also required (1). For two necessary propositions may both be false (10) as when we say: 'Every human is a writer necessarily' and 'No human is a writer necessarily'; and two possible propositions may both be true (11) as when we say: 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'No human is a writer with general possibility'. His saying 'and unity in other than these' means: and it is required in contradiction that the two propositions be united in other than the three mentioned matters, namely quantity, quality, and modality. And they have codified this unity within the unity in eight matters (12). One of them said in verse: 'In contradiction, know eight unities as condition: unity of condition and addition, part and whole, unity of subject and predicate and place, potentiality and actuality, and at the end time.' His saying 'and the contradictory of the necessary': Know that the contradictory of everything is its removal (13). So the contradictory of the proposition in which the necessity of affirmation or negation is judged is a proposition in which the negation of that necessity is judged. And the negation of every necessity is precisely the possibility of the opposite side. So the contradictory of the necessity of affirmation is the possibility of negation (14), and the contradictory of the necessity of negation is the possibility of affirmation. And the contradictory of perpetualness is the negation of perpetualness, and you already know that it necessitates the actuality of the opposite side. So the removal of perpetualness of affirmation necessitates the actuality of negation, and the removal of perpetualness of negation necessitates the actuality of affirmation. Thus the general possible is the explicit contradictory of the absolute necessary (15), and the general absolute is a necessary concomitant of the contradictory of the perpetual absolute. And since its explicit contradictory, which is non-perpetualness, is not a well-established concept considered among common propositions, they said: the contradictory of the perpetual absolute is the general absolute (16). Then know that the ratio of the possible temporal to the general conditioned is like the ratio of the general possible to the necessary (17). For the possible temporal is that in which the negation of the description-necessity is judged, i.e., the necessity as long as the description is from the opposite side. So the possible temporal and for the general conventional, the absolute temporal, and for the composite, the notion that is disjunct between the contradictories of the two parts, but in the particularity with respect to each individual. It is an explicit contradictory of that in which the necessity of the agreeing side according to the description is judged. So our saying 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer', its contradictory is: 'Some writer does not move his fingers when he is a writer, with possibility.' And the ratio of the absolute temporal, which is a proposition in which the actuality of the relation is judged at the time of the subject's being described by the titular description, to the general conventional is like the ratio of the general absolute to the perpetual (18). That is because the judgment in the general conventional is of the perpetualness of the relation as long as the subject is described by the titular description, so its explicit contradictory (19) is the negation of that perpetualness, and it necessitates the occurrence of the opposite side at times of the titular description. So this is the meaning of the absolute temporal that differs from the conventional proposition in quality. So the contradictory of our saying 'Perpetually, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' is our saying: 'Some writer does not move his fingers when he is a writer, actually.' And the author did not address the exposition of the contradictories of the two absolute temporary and dispersed propositions from among the simple ones, since no purpose (20) relates to that (21) in what will come of the topics of conversion and syllogisms, unlike the rest of the simple ones, so contemplate (22). Both? The exclusivity? His saying 'and for the composite': You already know that the contradictory of everything is its removal. Know that the removal of the composite is only by the removal of one of its two parts (23), not determinately (24), but in the manner of prevention, for it is possible (25) that it be by the removal of both its parts. So the contradictory of the composite proposition is the contradictory of one of its two parts (26) in the manner of prevention of absence. So the contradictory of our saying: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not perpetually' i.e., 'No writer moves his fingers actually' is a disjunctive proposition that prevents absence (27), and it is our saying: 'Either some writer does not move his fingers with possibility when he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers perpetually.' And after your acquaintance with the realities of composites and the contradictories of simples, you will be able to extract the details (28). His saying 'but in the particularity with respect to each individual' means: it is not sufficient in taking the contradictory of a particular composite proposition to disjunct between the contradictories of its two parts, which are universal, because both may be false with the composite particular being false, like our saying: 'Some animal is a man actually not perpetually' (29), and both contradictories of its two parts (30) are also false, and they are our saying: 'No animal is a man perpetually' and our saying: 'Every

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions for contradiction (tanāquḍ) in categorical propositions and the determination of contradictories for various modal and composite propositions. The author begins by stating the general rule: contradiction requires difference (ikhtilāf) in quantity (kamm), quality (kayf), and modality (jiha), and unity (ittiḥād) in all other aspects. He then expands on each condition. The condition of difference in quality means one must be affirmative and the other negative, because two affirmatives or two negatives can both be true or both false. For quantified propositions, they must also differ in quantity (e.g., universal vs. particular). For modalized propositions, they must differ in modality (e.g., necessary vs. possible), since two necessary propositions can both be false (e.g., 'Every human is a writer necessarily' and 'No human is a writer necessarily' are both false), and two possible propositions can both be true (e.g., 'Every human is a writer possible' and 'No human is a writer possible' are both true). The unity condition is codified in eight matters, presented in a Persian verse: unity of condition, addition, part and whole, subject, predicate, place, potentiality and actuality, and time. Next, the author explains how to find the contradictory of modal propositions. The contradictory of any proposition is its removal (raf'). For a necessary proposition, the contradictory is a proposition that negates that necessity, which is equivalent to the possibility of the opposite. Thus, the contradictory of 'necessarily A is B' is 'possibly A is not B'. For a perpetual proposition (dā'ima), its contradictory is the negation of perpetualness, which entails the actuality of the opposite, but since 'non-perpetual' is not a standard modal term, the contradictory is taken as the absolute proposition (muṭlaqa). The same logic applies to conditional (sharṭiyya) and temporal (ḥīniyya) propositions. For composite propositions (murakkaba), which combine two modalities, the contradictory is a disjunctive proposition that asserts the negation of one component without specifying which (but not both, i.e., exclusive disjunction). For example, the contradictory of 'necessarily A is B as long as it is C, but not perpetually' is 'either possibly A is not B when it is C, or A is B perpetually'. The author ends by noting that the contradictory of a particular composite cannot be a simple disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, because both may be false while the original is false, as shown with an example from animal/human.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the contradictory of 'Every human is an animal necessarily'?
A) Every human is not an animal necessarily.
B) Some human is not an animal possibly.
C) Some human is an animal possibly.
D) No human is an animal necessarily.

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a modal proposition is obtained by removing its modality, which often converts necessity into possibility or perpetualness into absoluteness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Given the proposition 'Every human is an animal necessarily', write its contradictory in the correct modal form and explain why.
?
The proposition 'No writer moves his fingers always (perpetually)' is given. Determine its contradictory using the rule for perpetual propositions.

What Is Mantiq?
advanced
10 min
Contradictories of Composite Propositions
Author explains the contradictory of composite propositions using a disjunctive form.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct the contradictory of a composite proposition (e.g., necessary under condition but not perpetual) as a disjunctive proposition that prevents absence.

Core Idea
The contradictory of a composite proposition is a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, in an exclusive sense (man'at al-khuluw).

Composite propositions combine two modal conditions, such as 'necessarily as long as the description holds, but not perpetually'. Their contradictories are not simple because removing a composite requires removing at least one part, but not both. This lesson explains how to form the contradictory as a disjunctive proposition that excludes the possibility of both parts being false, using the author's example.

Guided Path
1
Understand the structure of a composite proposition
A composite proposition has two parts conjoined, typically by 'but' or 'and', combining two modal conditions. Example: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not perpetually.'
2
Recall the principle of removal for composites
The removal (raf') of a composite is the removal of one of its parts, not determinately, but in the manner of prevention of absence (man'at al-khuluw). That is, it is a disjunction such that at least one part is removed, but not necessarily both.
3
Form the contradictory as a disjunction
Take the contradictories of each part separately, then combine them with an exclusive disjunction (man'at al-khuluw: exactly one is true). For the example, the contradictory is: 'Either some writer does not move his fingers with possibility when he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers perpetually.'
The two parts are: (a) necessity under description, (b) negation of perpetualness. Their contradictories are: (a') possible temporal negative, (b') perpetual affirmative.
4
Handle the particular case
If the composite is particular (e.g., 'some animal is a man actually not perpetually'), the contradictory cannot simply be a disjunction of the contradictories of its parts (which are universal) because both could be false while the original is false. Instead, the contradictory must be constructed with respect to each individual.
The passage warns about this subtlety.

On contradiction. And it is necessary that there be difference in quantity, quality, and modality, and unity in other than these. And the contradictory of the necessary, the general possible, and of the perpetual, the general absolute, and of the general conditioned in quantity (4) as the author will clarify. His saying 'And it is necessary that there be difference' means: it is required in contradiction that one of the two contradictories be affirmative and the other negative, necessarily because two affirmatives and likewise two negatives (the two negatives? error) may both be true or both false (5). Then if the two propositions are quantified, their difference in quantity is also required (7) as previously, then if they are modalized, their difference in modality is also required (1). For two necessary propositions may both be false (10) as when we say: 'Every human is a writer necessarily' and 'No human is a writer necessarily'; and two possible propositions may both be true (11) as when we say: 'Every human is a writer with general possibility' and 'No human is a writer with general possibility'. His saying 'and unity in other than these' means: and it is required in contradiction that the two propositions be united in other than the three mentioned matters, namely quantity, quality, and modality. And they have codified this unity within the unity in eight matters (12). One of them said in verse: 'In contradiction, know eight unities as condition: unity of condition and addition, part and whole, unity of subject and predicate and place, potentiality and actuality, and at the end time.' His saying 'and the contradictory of the necessary': Know that the contradictory of everything is its removal (13). So the contradictory of the proposition in which the necessity of affirmation or negation is judged is a proposition in which the negation of that necessity is judged. And the negation of every necessity is precisely the possibility of the opposite side. So the contradictory of the necessity of affirmation is the possibility of negation (14), and the contradictory of the necessity of negation is the possibility of affirmation. And the contradictory of perpetualness is the negation of perpetualness, and you already know that it necessitates the actuality of the opposite side. So the removal of perpetualness of affirmation necessitates the actuality of negation, and the removal of perpetualness of negation necessitates the actuality of affirmation. Thus the general possible is the explicit contradictory of the absolute necessary (15), and the general absolute is a necessary concomitant of the contradictory of the perpetual absolute. And since its explicit contradictory, which is non-perpetualness, is not a well-established concept considered among common propositions, they said: the contradictory of the perpetual absolute is the general absolute (16). Then know that the ratio of the possible temporal to the general conditioned is like the ratio of the general possible to the necessary (17). For the possible temporal is that in which the negation of the description-necessity is judged, i.e., the necessity as long as the description is from the opposite side. So the possible temporal and for the general conventional, the absolute temporal, and for the composite, the notion that is disjunct between the contradictories of the two parts, but in the particularity with respect to each individual. It is an explicit contradictory of that in which the necessity of the agreeing side according to the description is judged. So our saying 'Necessarily, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer', its contradictory is: 'Some writer does not move his fingers when he is a writer, with possibility.' And the ratio of the absolute temporal, which is a proposition in which the actuality of the relation is judged at the time of the subject's being described by the titular description, to the general conventional is like the ratio of the general absolute to the perpetual (18). That is because the judgment in the general conventional is of the perpetualness of the relation as long as the subject is described by the titular description, so its explicit contradictory (19) is the negation of that perpetualness, and it necessitates the occurrence of the opposite side at times of the titular description. So this is the meaning of the absolute temporal that differs from the conventional proposition in quality. So the contradictory of our saying 'Perpetually, every writer moves his fingers as long as he is a writer' is our saying: 'Some writer does not move his fingers when he is a writer, actually.' And the author did not address the exposition of the contradictories of the two absolute temporary and dispersed propositions from among the simple ones, since no purpose (20) relates to that (21) in what will come of the topics of conversion and syllogisms, unlike the rest of the simple ones, so contemplate (22). Both? The exclusivity? His saying 'and for the composite': You already know that the contradictory of everything is its removal. Know that the removal of the composite is only by the removal of one of its two parts (23), not determinately (24), but in the manner of prevention, for it is possible (25) that it be by the removal of both its parts. So the contradictory of the composite proposition is the contradictory of one of its two parts (26) in the manner of prevention of absence. So the contradictory of our saying: 'Every writer moves his fingers necessarily as long as he is a writer, but not perpetually' i.e., 'No writer moves his fingers actually' is a disjunctive proposition that prevents absence (27), and it is our saying: 'Either some writer does not move his fingers with possibility when he is a writer, or some writer moves his fingers perpetually.' And after your acquaintance with the realities of composites and the contradictories of simples, you will be able to extract the details (28). His saying 'but in the particularity with respect to each individual' means: it is not sufficient in taking the contradictory of a particular composite proposition to disjunct between the contradictories of its two parts, which are universal, because both may be false with the composite particular being false, like our saying: 'Some animal is a man actually not perpetually' (29), and both contradictories of its two parts (30) are also false, and they are our saying: 'No animal is a man perpetually' and our saying: 'Every
Source pages p. 66

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the conditions for contradiction (tanāquḍ) in categorical propositions and the determination of contradictories for various modal and composite propositions. The author begins by stating the general rule: contradiction requires difference (ikhtilāf) in quantity (kamm), quality (kayf), and modality (jiha), and unity (ittiḥād) in all other aspects. He then expands on each condition. The condition of difference in quality means one must be affirmative and the other negative, because two affirmatives or two negatives can both be true or both false. For quantified propositions, they must also differ in quantity (e.g., universal vs. particular). For modalized propositions, they must differ in modality (e.g., necessary vs. possible), since two necessary propositions can both be false (e.g., 'Every human is a writer necessarily' and 'No human is a writer necessarily' are both false), and two possible propositions can both be true (e.g., 'Every human is a writer possible' and 'No human is a writer possible' are both true). The unity condition is codified in eight matters, presented in a Persian verse: unity of condition, addition, part and whole, subject, predicate, place, potentiality and actuality, and time. Next, the author explains how to find the contradictory of modal propositions. The contradictory of any proposition is its removal (raf'). For a necessary proposition, the contradictory is a proposition that negates that necessity, which is equivalent to the possibility of the opposite. Thus, the contradictory of 'necessarily A is B' is 'possibly A is not B'. For a perpetual proposition (dā'ima), its contradictory is the negation of perpetualness, which entails the actuality of the opposite, but since 'non-perpetual' is not a standard modal term, the contradictory is taken as the absolute proposition (muṭlaqa). The same logic applies to conditional (sharṭiyya) and temporal (ḥīniyya) propositions. For composite propositions (murakkaba), which combine two modalities, the contradictory is a disjunctive proposition that asserts the negation of one component without specifying which (but not both, i.e., exclusive disjunction). For example, the contradictory of 'necessarily A is B as long as it is C, but not perpetually' is 'either possibly A is not B when it is C, or A is B perpetually'. The author ends by noting that the contradictory of a particular composite cannot be a simple disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, because both may be false while the original is false, as shown with an example from animal/human.

Key Terms
مركبة
منفصلة مانعة الخلو
رفع

Key Takeaway
The contradictory of a composite proposition is an exclusive disjunction of the contradictories of its parts, ensuring that at least one part is removed without specifying which.

Contrapositive: Definition and Rules
Author defines contrapositive and explains its rules for different proposition types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to compute the contrapositive of any categorical proposition and determine whether the ancients or later scholars' rule applies.

Core Idea
Contrapositive swaps negated terms; ancients keep quality, later scholars flip it.

In this lesson, we explore the logical operation of contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ), a form of immediate inference that transforms propositions by negating and swapping terms. The author contrasts two historical approaches: the ancients preserve the quality of the proposition, while the later scholars reverse it. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly applying contrapositive in Islamic logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject (S) and predicate (P) of the original proposition.
For example, in 'Every J is B', S=J, P=B. Write the proposition in standard form.
2
Negate both terms individually.
Form 'not-S' and 'not-P'. For J, 'not-J'; for B, 'not-B'.
3
Swap the negated terms to form the contrapositive frame.
The subject becomes 'not-P' and predicate becomes 'not-S'.
4
Apply the quality rule based on the school.
Ancients: preserve the original quality (affirmative/negative). Later scholars: reverse the quality.
If the original is affirmative, the contrapositive is negative for later scholars.
5
Construct the final proposition with the appropriate quantifier.
For a universal affirmative like 'Every J is B', ancients give 'Every not-B is not-J'; later scholars give 'No not-B is J'.
Check truth preservation: if original true, contrapositive is also true.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: عكس النقيض: تبديل نقيضي الطرفين مع بقاء الصدق والكيف. عكس النقيض. قوله تبديل نقيضي الطرفين أي جعل نقيض الجزء الأول من الأصل جزءاً ثانياً من العكس ونقيض الجزء الثاني جزءاً أولاً. وقوله مع بقاء الصدق أي إن كان الأصل صادقاً كان العكس صادقاً بقاء. والكيف أي إن كان الأصل موجباً كان العكس موجباً وإن كان سالباً كان العكس سالباً. مثلاً قولنا كل ج ب ينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى قولنا: كل ما ليس ب ليس ج. وهذه طريقة القدماء. وأما المتأخرون فقالوا: عكس النقيض هو جعل نقيض الجزء الثاني أولاً وعين الجزء الأول ثانياً مع مخالفة الكيف أي إن كان الأصل موجباً كان العكس سالباً وبالعكس ويعتبر بقاء الصدق كما مر. فقولنا كل ج ب ينعكس إلى قولنا: لا شيء مما ليس ب ب ج. والمصنف لم يصرح بقولهم وعين الأول ثانياً للعلم به ضمناً ولا باعتبار بقاء الصدق في التعريف الثاني لذكره سابقاً فحيث لم يخالفه في هذا التعريف علم اعتباره ههنا أيضاً. ثم إنه بين المصنف أحكام عكس النقيض على طريقة القدماء إذ فيه غنية. ثم قال: وحكم الموجبات ههنا حكم السوالب في المستوى وبالعكس والبيان هو البيان. قوله ههنا أي في عكس النقيض. قوله في المستوى أي كما أن السالبة الكلية تنعكس في عكس المستوى يعني كنفسها والجزئية لا تنعكس أصلاً كذلك الموجبة الكلية في عكس النقيض تنعكس كنفسها والجزئية لا تنعكس أصلاً لصدق قولنا بعض الحيوان لا إنسان وكذب قولنا بعض الإنسان لا حيوان. وكذلك التسع من الموجهات أعني الوقتيتين المطلقتين والوقتيتين والوجوديتين والممكنتين والمطلقة العامة لا تنعكس. والباقي تنعكس على ما سبق في السوالب في العكس المستوى. قوله وبالعكس أي حكم السوالب ههنا حكم الموجبات في المستوى فكما أن الموجبة في المستوى لا تنعكس إلا الجزئية كذلك السالبة ههنا لا تنعكس إلا الجزئية. الجواز أن يكون نقيض المحمول في السالبة أعم من الموضوع ولا يجوز سلب نقيض الأخص عن عين الأعم كلياً. مثلاً يصح: لا شيء من الإنسان بلا حيوان ولا يصح: لا شيء من الحيوان بلا إنسان لصدق نقيضه بعض الحيوان لا إنسان كالفرس. وكذلك بحسب الجهة الدائمتان والعامتان تنعكس حينية مطلقة والخاصتان تنعكسان حينية مطلقة لا دائمة والوقتيتان والوجوديتان والمطلقة العامة مطلقة عامة ولا عكس للممكنتين على قياس الموجبات في المستوى. قوله والبيان هو البيان يعني كما أن المطالب المذكورة في العكس.
Chapter: Contrapositive: Substituting the two negated terms while preserving truth and quality. Contrapositive: His statement 'substituting the two negated terms' means making the negation of the first part of the original a second part of the converse, and the negation of the second part a first part. And his statement 'while preserving truth' means that if the original is true, the converse is true, preserving truth. And [preserving] quality means that if the original is affirmative, the converse is affirmative, and if negative, the converse is negative. Example: our statement 'Every J is B' converts by contrapositive to our statement: 'Every not-B is not-J'. This is the method of the ancients. As for the later scholars, they said: Contrapositive is making the negation of the second part first, and the same of the first part second, while opposing the quality, i.e., if the original is affirmative, the converse is negative, and vice versa, and preservation of truth is considered as before. So our statement 'Every J is B' converts to our statement: 'Nothing not-B is J'. The author did not explicitly mention their statement 'and the same of the first part second' because it is implicitly known, nor did he mention preservation of truth in the second definition because he mentioned it earlier, so since he did not contradict it in this definition, its consideration is known here as well. Then the author explained the rules of contrapositive according to the method of the ancients, for it is sufficient. Then he said: 'And the rule of affirmatives here is the rule of negatives in the direct converse, and vice versa, and the explanation is the same.' His statement 'here' means in contrapositive. His statement 'in the direct converse' means that just as the universal negative converts in direct converse as is, and the particular does not convert at all, likewise the universal affirmative in contrapositive converts as is, and the particular does not convert at all, because our statement 'Some animal is not man' is true, and our statement 'Some man is not animal' is false. And likewise the nine modal propositions, namely the two absolute temporal, the two temporal, the two existential, the two possible, and the general absolute, do not convert. And the remainder convert as previously in the negatives in direct converse. His statement 'and vice versa' means that the rule of negatives here is the rule of affirmatives in direct converse, so just as the affirmative in direct converse does not convert except the particular, likewise the negative here does not convert except the particular. It is permissible that the negation of the predicate in the negative is more general than the subject, and it is not permissible to universally negate the negation of the more specific from the same of the more general. For example, it is correct: 'Nothing from man is without animal' but not correct: 'Nothing from animal is without man' because its contradictory 'Some animal is not man' is true, like the horse. And likewise according to modality: the two perpetuals and the two generals convert to absolute temporal; the two specials convert to absolute temporal not perpetual; the two temporal, the two existential, and the general absolute convert to general absolute; and the two possibles have no conversion, analogously to the affirmatives in direct converse. His statement 'and the explanation is the same' means that the demands mentioned in the converse are the same.
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage defines the logical operation of contrapositive (ʿaks al-naqīḍ) and then explains its rules for different types of propositions. The author first gives the definition from the method of the ancients (al-qudamāʾ): substituting the two negated terms (i.e., making the negation of the first part the second part and the negation of the second part the first part) while preserving truth and quality. An example: 'Every J is B' becomes 'Every not-B is not-J'. Then he contrasts the method of the later scholars (al-mutaʾakhkhirūn), who define it as making the negation of the second part first and the same of the first part second, but with opposite quality. So 'Every J is B' becomes 'Nothing not-B is J'. The author notes that the later definition implicitly includes preservation of truth because it was mentioned earlier. The author then explains the rules for contrapositive following the method of the ancients. He states that in contrapositive, universal affirmative propositions convert as is (i.e., to universal affirmative), while particular affirmatives do not convert. This mirrors the rule for negatives in direct converse (al-mustawī). Similarly, negatives in contrapositive behave like affirmatives in direct converse: universal negatives convert, particulars do not. The author gives a counterexample: 'Some animal is not man' is true, but 'Some man is not animal' is false. He then extends the discussion to modal propositions, listing nine types that do not convert and the remainder that do, following patterns from direct converse. The passage concludes by clarifying that the rules for possibilities and other modalities follow the same pattern as in direct converse. The commentary here (the ḥāshiya) refines the matn by providing these detailed rules and examples, showing the precision required in logical conversion.

Key Terms
عكس النقيض (ʿaks al-naqīḍ): The logical operation of contrapositive: swapping the subject and predicate after negating both, while preserving truth and quality (according to the ancients) or while changing quality (according to the later scholars). [Note: The ancients preserved quality, later scholars reversed it; this distinction is important for understanding historical developments.]
القدماء (al-qudamāʾ): Earlier logicians who defined contrapositive with preservation of quality. [Note: In later texts, especially after al-Rāzī, the term often refers to the earlier Peripatetic tradition.]
المتأخرون (al-mutaʾakhkhirūn): Later logicians who defined contrapositive with change of quality. [Note: This group includes post-Avicennan logicians like al-Ṭūsī and al-Rāzī.]
الكيف (al-kayf): Quality of the proposition: affirmative or negative. [Note: Standard term across logical tradition.]
المستوى (al-mustawī): Direct converse (ʿaks mustawī): swapping subject and predicate without negation. [Note: Also called ʿaks basīṭ; distinguished from ʿaks al-naqīḍ.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Every forbidden is intoxicant
B) Every not-forbidden is not-intoxicant
C) No not-forbidden is intoxicant
D) Some not-forbidden is not-intoxicant

Key Takeaway
The contrapositive operation differs between the ancient and later logicians in whether quality is preserved or reversed; this historical distinction affects how one applies the rule.

The Definition of Qiyas: Composite Statement with Proportionality
Author defines qiyas as a composite statement and explains its components.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two key components of the definition of qiyas and explain why 'mu'allaf' is more specific than 'murakkab'.

Core Idea
Qiyas is more than just a composite statement; its parts must harmonize proportionally.

The logicians define qiyas as a statement that not only is composed of propositions but also entails another statement by itself. In this lesson, we will dissect the definition, paying special attention to the terms 'qawl', 'mu'allaf', and 'murakkab', and understand why the commentator insists on the nuance of proportionality.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition of qiyas
Locate the full definition in the matn: 'القياس قول مؤلّف من قضايا يلزمه لذاته قول آخر'. Note each term.
This is the base claim being expanded.
2
Distinguish between 'murakkab' and 'mu'allaf'
The commentary explains that 'qawl' means 'murakkab' (composite) in general, but 'mu'allaf' is more specific because it implies proportionality (ulfa) between parts.
Proportionality ensures the parts fit together logically.
3
Recognize the commentary's method
The commentator notes that mentioning 'mu'allaf' after 'qawl' is an instance of mentioning the specific after the general, a common technique in definitions.
This technique narrows the meaning.
4
Reflect on the formal part
The commentary states that considering 'ta'lif' after 'tarkib' points to the formal part of the proof (al-juz' al-suri fi al-hujja). Understand what that means for the structure of the syllogism.
The formal part concerns the arrangement and logical connection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القياس قول مؤلّف من قضايا يلزمه لذاته قول آخر. قوله «القياس قول»: ای مرکب (۱) و هو اعم من المؤلّف (۲) اذ قد اعتبر فى المؤلف المناسبة بين أجزائه لانه مأخوذ من الالفة صرح بذلك الشريف المحقق في حاشية الكشاف وحينئذ فذكر المؤلف بعد القول من قبيل ذكر الخاص بعد العام و هو متعارف فى التعريفات و فى اعتبار التأليف بعد التركيب اشارة الى اعتبار الجزء الصوري في الحجة (۳) و القول يشمل المركبات التامة وغيرها كلها (۴) و بقوله :
The syllogism is a statement composed of propositions which entails by itself another statement. His statement: 'The syllogism is a statement' means it is composite (1), and it is more general than 'composed' (2), because in 'composed' the proportionality between its parts is considered, since it is derived from 'harmony'. This was stated by al-Sharif al-Muhaqqiq in his Hashiya on al-Kashshaf. So mentioning 'composed' after 'statement' is of the type of mentioning the specific after the general, which is customary in definitions. And considering 'composition' after 'combination' indicates the consideration of the formal part in the proof (3). And 'statement' includes complete compounds and all others (4). And by his statement:
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of qiyas (syllogism) as given in the matn. The author first quotes the matn: 'The syllogism is a statement composed of propositions which entails by itself another statement.' Then he explains each part. First, he notes that 'statement' here means a composite (murakkab), which is more general than 'composed' (mu'allaf). The reason is that 'mu'allaf' implies a proportionality or harmony (ulfa) between its parts, as al-Sharif al-Jurjani mentioned in his Hashiya on al-Kashshaf. So by using 'mu'allaf' after 'qawl' (statement), the matn author is mentioning a specific type of composite after the general term, which is a common practice in definitions to narrow down the meaning. The addition of 'composition' (ta'lif) after 'combination' (tarkib) points to the formal aspect of the proof (al-juz' al-suri fi al-hujja). Finally, 'statement' encompasses all types of compounds, both complete and incomplete. This fine distinction shows the precision required in logical definitions.

Key Takeaway
The precision of the definition of qiyas lies in the distinction between mere composition (tarkib) and proportional composition (ta'lif), which ensures the rational coherence of the syllogism.

The Three Parts of Any Science: Subject, Problems, and Principles
Author enumerates the three essential parts of any science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject, problems, and principles of any Islamic science and distinguish between conceptual and confirmatory principles.

Core Idea
Every science has three pillars: subject, problems, and principles – know them to understand any discipline.

Have you ever wondered what makes a science a science? The classical logicians did not just study arguments; they also analysed the structure of knowledge itself. In this passage from the conclusion of a section, the author lays out the three indispensable components of any codified science (al-ʿulūm al-mudawwana). By internalising this framework, you will be able to instantly see how disciplines like ʿilm al-kalām, uṣūl al-fiqh, or ʿilm al-ḥadīth are organised — and why they are rigorous bodies of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three parts mentioned
Scan the Arabic text for the three items the author declares essential for any science. They are: al-mawḍūʿ (subject), al-masāʾil (problems), and al-mabādiʾ (principles). Note that the principles are further subdivided.
The author uses the phrase 'اجزاء العلوم ثلاثة' to introduce the tripartite structure.
2
Understand the role of the subject (al-mawḍūʿ)
The subject is what the science is about; the entire investigation returns to it. For example, in ʿilm al-kalām, the subject is Allāh and His attributes. The author mentions that the science investigates its 'essential attributes' (aʿrāḍ dhātiyya) which are the effects and characteristics sought.
The subject defines the scope of the science.
3
Distinguish the problems (al-masāʾil) from principles
The problems are the propositions (qaḍāyā) that are investigated. They are mostly theoretical (naẓariyya) but can sometimes be evident (badīhiyya). Principles, on the other hand, are the foundations on which the problems are built. The author subdivides principles into conceptual (taṣawwuriyya) and confirmatory (taṣdīqiyya).
The passage notes a variant reading about 'by demonstration' (al-burhān) which the author clarifies.
4
Apply the framework to a real Islamic science
Choose a science you know, such as uṣūl al-fiqh, and map its subject (e.g., the proofs of fiqh), problems (e.g., does amr imply wujūb?), conceptual principles (definitions of amr, nahy), and confirmatory principles (the premise that the Qurʾān is a proof).
This step solidifies the abstract division into a concrete instance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خاتمة: أجزاء العلوم ثلاثة، رات کا مال اساری

اجزاء العلوم

:الثالث ما يبنى عليه المسائل مما يفيد تصورات اطرافها (۷) او التصديقات بالقضاياء المأخوذة فى دلائلها (۸) فالاولى هى المبادى التصورية و الثانية هي المبادى التصديقية.
Conclusion: The parts of the sciences are three. [Garbled] three. Parts of the sciences. His statement: 'The parts of the sciences': Every science among the recorded sciences must have matters: One of them is that in which its characteristics and the sought effects are investigated, i.e., to which all of the science returns, and that is the subject. And those effects are the essential attributes. Second: The propositions in which this investigation occurs, and they are the problems. They are mostly theoretical, and sometimes they may be evident needing proof, as they have stated. And his statement: 'required in the science' covers both types. And as for what is found in some copies of the specification by his statement: 'by demonstration' — that is from the scribe's addition, and it can be justified as based on the majority or that by 'demonstration' is meant what includes alerting, so be alert. Third: That on which the problems are based, which provides the conceptualizations of their terms or the confirmations of the propositions taken in their proofs. The first are the conceptual principles, and the second are the confirmatory principles.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage is the conclusion (khatima) of a section, presenting the three essential parts of any science. The author explains that every codified science necessarily has: (1) the subject (mawdu'), which is the thing whose characteristics and effects are investigated, and those effects are the 'accidental essential' (a'rad dhatiyya) of that subject; (2) the problems (masa'il), which are the propositions investigated; these are mostly theoretical but sometimes evident though still needing clarification; and (3) the principles (mabadi'), which are the foundations upon which the problems are built. The principles are of two kinds: conceptual (tasawwuri), which provide concepts of the terms used in the problems, and confirmatory (tasdiqi), which provide the propositions used as premises in proofs. The author also notes a variant reading in some manuscripts where the phrase 'by demonstration' is added after 'required in the science', but he explains it can be reconciled as referring to the predominant mode (demonstration) or that 'demonstration' here includes any clarification. This is a definitional passage enumerating the structure of any science, crucial for understanding how disciplines like logic, jurisprudence, or theology are organized.

المسائل
المبادى التصورية
المبادى التصديقية

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Every codified Islamic science is built on three components: a subject matter, problems to investigate, and principles (both conceptual and confirmatory) that support the investigation.

Distinguishing Principles from Premises
Author presents an alternate definition of principles and premises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'principles' (mabadi') and 'premises' (muqaddimat) in the context of an Islamic science, using the alternative definition that premises require expertise and strong desire.

Core Idea
Principles are your starting point; premises are what your start depends on with expertise.

This passage from a hashia on logic introduces a refined distinction between two key terms in the organization of a science: principles (mabadi') and premises (muqaddimat). The author explains that principles are the preliminary matters you begin with before reaching the main goal, while premises are the foundational items upon which the actual commencement of the science rests—and this dependence is strong, requiring both expertise (khibra) and intense desire (fart al-raghba). Understanding this distinction helps you see how a science like usul al-fiqh is structured and what counts as truly foundational.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms being defined
Find 'المبادى' (al-mabadi') and 'المقدمات' (al-muqaddimat) in the Arabic text. Notice that the author is giving an alternative definition for both.
These terms are often used interchangeably, but here they are distinguished.
2
Extract the genus for each term
Principles are defined as 'ما يبدء به قبل المقصود' (what one begins with before the intended goal). Premises are defined as 'ما يتوقف عليه الشروع' (what the commencement depends upon). Compare the two: one is about what you start with, the other about what the start relies on.
3
Note the special condition for premises
Premises have an extra qualification: 'على وجه الخبرة و فرط الرغبة' (in the manner of expertise and extreme desire). This means not any dependency qualifies; it must be a dependency that requires deep knowledge and strong eagerness. Why would the author add this? Because premises are the critical foundations that the science cannot do without, and they demand mastery.
This condition is the key difference from principles.
4
Apply the definition to the given example
The author gives examples: 'تعريف العلم وبيان غايته وموضوعه' (defining the science, stating its purpose and subject). Are these principles or premises? According to the definition, they are principles because they are what you begin with before the main discussion. They are not premises because the commencement of the science does not depend on them in the sense of expertise and desire—they are introductory, not foundational.
This clarifies how to categorize introductory material vs. foundational premises.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و قد يقال المبادى لما يبدء به قبل المقصود والمقدمات لما يتوقف عليه الشروع على وجه الخبرة و فرط الرغبة كتعريف العلم وبيان غايته وموضوعه.
And it could be said: Principles are what one begins with before the intended goal, and premises are what the commencement depends upon, in the manner of expertise and extreme desire, such as defining the science, stating its purpose and subject.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage introduces another terminological usage for 'principles' and 'premises' in the sciences. The author states that according to some, principles are the things that one starts with before reaching the main goal of a science, and premises are those things upon which the commencement of the science depends. The phrase 'on the way of expertise and extreme desire' qualifies the dependency: it is not any dependency, but one that requires deep knowledge and intense eagerness. Examples include defining the science, explaining its purpose, and stating its subject matter. This definition is different from the earlier one (not quoted here) which defines principles as the fundamental concepts and premises as the starting points. The point is to delimit what counts as a premise in a science.

Key Takeaway
Principles are the starting points, premises are the critical dependencies with expertise; a science's structure becomes clearer when you separate them.

The Naming of Logic: From Nutq to Mantiq
Author explains why the science is called mantiq, deriving it from nutq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the science of logic is called mantiq by identifying the two meanings of nutq and the two morphological derivations of mantiq from nutq.

Core Idea
Logic is named mantiq because it perfects both speech and reason (nutq).

The science of logic is called mantiq in Arabic, a name derived from the word nutq. But nutq itself has two meanings: external articulate speech and the internal rational faculty that perceives universals. In this passage, the commentator explains how logic relates to both, and offers two possible grammatical derivations for the name mantiq—as a verbal noun meaning 'perfect nutq' or as a noun of place meaning 'the locus of nutq.' Understanding this etymology reveals the foundational role logic plays in perfecting human thought and expression.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of nutq
In the Arabic, locate the phrase 'يطلق على الظاهرى و هو التكلم وعلى الباطني و هو ادراك الكليات'. This tells us that nutq refers to both external speech and internal perception of universals.
Note that the internal meaning is the rational faculty, not just any thinking.
2
Understand how logic serves each meaning
The text says logic 'يقوى الأول' (strengthens the first) and 'يسلك بالثانى مسلك السداد' (leads the second on the path of rectitude). So logic improves both speech and reasoning.
This dual role is key to understanding why the science is named after nutq.
3
Analyze the two morphological derivations of mantiq
The author presents two possibilities: mantiq is either a masdar mimi (verbal noun) meaning nutq itself, used hyperbolically to show logic's centrality; or an ism makan (noun of place) meaning the locus where nutq is manifested.
Both derivations emphasize the close connection between logic and nutq.
4
Synthesize the purpose of the naming
The naming indicates that logic is so integral to perfecting nutq that it is as if it were nutq itself, or that logic is the place where proper reasoning occurs.
This gives a concise indication of the goals of the science.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سمی
الإشارة الى وجه تسمية العلم (۴۸) كما يقال : انما المنطق منطقاً (۴۹) لان النطق يطلق على الظاهرى و هو التكلم وعلى الباطني و هوادراك الكليات و هذا العلم يقوى الأول ويسلك بالثانى مسلك السداد (۵۰) فاشتق له اسم من النطق، فالمنطق اما مصدر ميمى بمعنی النطق اطلق على العلم المذكور مبالغة فى مدخليته في تكميل النطق حتى كانه هو و اما آسم مكان كان هذا العلم محل النطق و مظهره و في ذكر وجه التسمية اشارة اجمالية الى ما يفضله العلم من المقاصد.
It was named: The indication of the aspect of naming the science, as it is said: It is only called mantiq because nutq is applied to the external, which is speech, and to the internal, which is the perception of universals. And this science strengthens the first and leads the second along the path of rectitude. So a name was derived for it from nutq. Thus mantiq is either a verbal noun (maṣdar mīmī) meaning nutq, applied to the mentioned science as an exaggeration of its role in perfecting nutq, to the point that it is as if it is nutq itself; or it is a noun of place, meaning that this science is the locus and manifestation of nutq. And in mentioning the aspect of naming there is an overall indication of the goals that the science aims for.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage explains the etymology of the term 'mantiq' (logic). The author states that the science is named mantiq because it relates to 'nutq', which has two meanings: external nutq (speech) and internal nutq (the intellectual perception of universals, i.e., reason). Logic serves to strengthen external speech (by providing correct argument forms) and to guide internal reasoning along the right path (by ensuring valid inference). Hence, the name mantiq is derived from nutq. The author then gives two possible morphological derivations: First, mantiq as a masdar mīmī (verbal noun) meaning 'nutq' itself, used hyperbolically to indicate that logic is so instrumental in perfecting nutq that it is as if it were nutq itself. Second, mantiq as a noun of place (ism makān), meaning that logic is the place where nutq (reason) is manifested and exercised. This dual explanation highlights the intimate connection between logic and rational speech. The mention of the naming aspect provides a concise indication of the objectives of the science: to perfect both speech and thought.

God as the Ultimate Putter of Names
Author argues that God may be the original putter of names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the author's critique against the claim that only humans place names, and identify the theological alternative of divine wad‘.

Core Idea
The non-human origin of a name does not prove it has no origin—God can place it.

This passage from Yazdi's Hashiya addresses a subtle point in the theory of wad‘ (linguistic assignment). While some held that only humans can assign words to meanings, the author counters that even if a name is not placed by humans, it could have been placed by God through revelation or inspiration. This lesson unpacks the logical structure of that argument and its implications for the intersection of logic and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's claim
The opponent asserts that if a specific name (like a proper name) was not placed by humans, then it has no conventional origin at all.
The opponent assumes that only humans can perform wad‘.
2
Grant the opponent's premise for argument
The author says 'even if we grant' that humans did not place this particular name as a sign (bi-wad‘ al-bashar).
This is a dialectical concession to test the consequence.
3
Extract the logical consequence
From that grant, it follows that humans did not place it (lam yada‘hu al-bashar).
This consequence is more specific (akhas) than the opponent's original claim.
4
Observe the scope mismatch
The opponent's claim was that the name has no placement at all; the author shows that only human placement is negated, not any placement.
The logical move: non placitum a humano non sequitur nullum placitum.
5
Introduce the alternative of divine placement
The author presents that God could have placed the name for His essence (li-dhatihi), making it known via inspiration or revelation.
This aligns with the position of the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) that God is the ultimate putter.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عن جميع ما عداه كما هو ظاهر لمن لاحظ وضع الاعلام فان كثيرها من هذا القبيل ولو سلم فنقول : اللازم من هذا ان لا يضعه البشر علماً و هواخص من المدعى اذ لا يلزم من عدم وضعه عدم الوضع مطلقاً الجواز ان يضع الله سبحانه علماً لذاته فيعلم غيره بالالهام أو الوحى او خلق الاصوات على ما ذهب اليه المحققون من ان الواضع للالفاظ مطلقا أو واضع هذا الاسم بخصوصه هو الله تعالى هذا.
From all besides it, as is apparent to one who observes the placement of proper names, for many of them are of this kind. And even if we grant, we say: The necessary consequence is that humans did not place it as a sign, which is more specific than the claim, for it does not follow from its non-placement that there is no placement at all, because it is possible that God placed a sign for His essence, so He makes others know it by inspiration or revelation or creation of sounds, according to what the verifiers have held: that the putter of words absolutely, or the putter of this name specifically, is God Almighty.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement responding to an implied objection about naming. The author argues that even if one concedes that humans did not place a particular name, it does not entail that the name was never placed; rather, God could have placed it directly through revelation or inspiration. The author aligns with the position of the verifiers (al-muhaqqiqun) that God is the ultimate putter of all words or at least this specific name. This shows the interconnection between logic (wad') and theology (kalam), as the issue of naming touches on divine attributes and the origin of language. The argument is dialectical: the opponent's claim is too broad, and the author narrows the scope to show a possible divine action.

Key Takeaway

Two Meanings of Dhat and Its Grammatical Gender
Commentator clarifies two meanings of 'dhāt' and its grammatical gender.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of 'dhāt' (ḥaqīqa vs. opposite of waṣf) and explain its grammatical flexibility using the Quranic example.

Core Idea
Dhat shifts meaning from 'reality' to 'that which is not description' depending on context.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies a crucial term in logic: 'dhāt' (essence/self). The word has two distinct uses—one referring to the true reality of a thing, another referring to the entity itself as opposed to its attributes. Understanding which is meant is key to precise logical definitions. The commentator also draws on Arabic grammar and a Quranic verse to show that 'dhāt' can be treated as feminine or masculine.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under discussion
The passage begins with 'قوله للذات' – 'His saying: for the essence'. This signals that the base text used the word 'dhāt', and the commentator is explaining its meaning.
2
Recognize the two usages of dhāt
The commentator lists two usages: (1) the true reality (ḥaqīqat al-shay'), and (2) that which is opposite of description (mā yuqābil al-waṣf). The second is the intended meaning here. Note that this distinction is pragmatic: in logic, 'dhāt' often means the subject of attributes, not the attributes themselves.
3
Compare with similar words
The commentator notes that 'dhāt' is used like 'nafs' (self) and 'shay'' (thing). This shows that 'dhāt' is a flexible term that can refer to the core identity of something.
4
Understand the grammatical gender flexibility
The commentator explains that 'dhāt' can be feminized or masculinized. He cites al-Akhfash's comment on Q 49:10: 'wa aṣliḥū dhāta baynikum'. In that verse, 'dhāt' is treated as feminine because the word itself is sometimes assigned a feminine gender, just as 'dār' is feminine and 'ḥā'iṭ' is masculine.
This shows that logical terminology is rooted in Arabic linguistic conventions.
5
Apply the two meanings to a real text
Consider a definition from logic: 'al-insānu dhātun nāṭiqah' (Man is a rational essence). Here 'dhāt' likely means the reality (ḥaqīqa) of man, not the opposite of description. Check which meaning fits based on context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله للذات اعلم ان الذات قد يطلق و يراد بها حقيقة الشيء و قد يطلق و يراد بها ما يقابل الوصف وهو المراد هنا و هو يستعمل استعمال النفس واستعمال الشيء ولذا يجوز تأنيثه وتذكيره. هذا. وقال الأخفش في قوله تعالى: واصلحواذات بينكم: و انما انثواذات، لان بعض الاشياء قد وضع له اسم مؤنث و لبعضها اسم مذكر كما قالوا دار وحائط انتوا الدار و ذكروا الحائط. (محمد علی)
His saying: 'for the essence (li-l-dhāt)'. Know that 'dhāt' may be used to mean the reality of a thing, and may be used to mean that which is opposite of description, and that is what is meant here. And it is used like the use of 'self' (nafs) and like the use of 'thing' (shay'), and therefore it may be feminized or masculinized. This. And al-Akhfash said regarding His saying, exalted: 'And make peace between yourselves (wa aṣliḥū dhāta baynikum)': They only feminized 'dhāt' because some things have been given a feminine name and some a masculine name, as they said 'dār' (house) and 'ḥā'iṭ' (wall); they feminized 'dār' and masculinized 'ḥā'iṭ'.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a definitional expansion explaining the term 'dhāt' (essence/self) as used in the original text. The author notes that 'dhāt' has two usages: one referring to the reality (ḥaqīqa) of a thing, and another referring to what is opposite of description (al-waṣf). Here the intended meaning is the opposite of description. The author also explains the grammatical gender of 'dhāt': it can be feminine or masculine depending on context, citing al-Akhfash's comment on the Quranic verse about reconciliation, where 'dhāt' is feminized because the word is used as a noun like 'dār' (feminine) or 'ḥā'iṭ' (masculine). This shows the intersection of logic (definition of terms) and Arabic grammar.

The Uniqueness of 'Wajib': Refuting the Need for 'Li-dhatihi'
Author criticizes a commentator's need to add 'li-dhatihi' to Wajib al-Wujud.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the term 'Wajib' exclusively refers to the Necessary by Itself and identify the error in adding qualifications like 'by itself'.

Core Idea
Wajib means by itself; no explanatory addition is needed or valid.

This passage shows a key logical principle in Islamic metaphysics: the term 'wajib' (necessary) when used absolutely only applies to God, the Necessary Existence. A commentator suggested we must add 'by itself' to clarify. The hashya author firmly refutes this, arguing that adding such qualification is unnecessary and conceptually flawed. Understanding this helps you grasp the precision of Islamic theological language and avoid redundant definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the commentator's claim
The verifiers among the commentators added 'li-dhatihi' to 'Wajib al-Wujud', claiming it is the immediate understanding and that 'wajib bi-l-ghayr' pertains to possibles.
2
Find the author's objection
The hashya author says there is no need for estimation or claiming 'immediate understanding'. Why? Because 'wajib' absolutely only means the Necessary by Itself; a possible cannot be described as necessary at all.
3
Analyze the logical basis
The author's basis is that it is impermissible for a possible to be described as necessary even with qualification 'by another'. So the term 'wajib' is exclusive to the Necessary by Itself.
This relies on the metaphysical principle that modal attributes are exclusive to their subject.
4
Evaluate the critique's validity
Consider whether the author's argument is sound. In Islamic metaphysics, necessity is a modal property exclusive to God. Possibles are contingent, so describing them as necessary in any sense is impossible. Thus the critique holds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : الواجب الوجود قال بعض المحققين من المحشين: «اى لذاته لانه المتبادر عند الاطلاق، ولان الواجب بالغير شأن الممكن والممكن لايسمى بالله ولا يكون مستحقاً للحمد والمقصود اثبات ذلك . انتهى . ولا يخفى ما فيه، فان الواجب لا يراد منه حيث يطلق الا الواجب لذاته لعدم جواز اتصاف الممكن بالواجب حتى يقيد بقولنا بالغير» كما تقرر فى موضعه فلا يحتاج الى التقدير وادعاء التبادر. (محمد علی)
His saying: 'the Necessary Existence'. Some of the verifiers among the commentators said: 'i.e., by itself (li-dhatihi), because it is the first understood when unqualified, and because the necessary by another is the quality of the possible, and the possible is not named 'Allah' nor is it deserving of praise, and the purpose is to establish that.' End. And it is not hidden what is in it, for 'the Necessary' when used absolutely is not intended except for the Necessary by itself, because it is impermissible for the possible to be described as necessary even if qualified by 'by another', as established in its place, so there is no need for the estimation and claiming of being the first understood.
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a commentator's suggestion. The commentator claimed that when 'Wajib al-Wujud' is mentioned, one should interpret it as 'by itself' because it is the immediate understanding and because 'wajib bi-l-ghayr' pertains to the possible. The author of the hashya (Muhammad Ali) refutes this, arguing that the term 'wajib' when unqualified absolutely means 'wajib bi-dhatihi' by definition, since a possible cannot truly be described as necessary at all, even with the qualification 'by another'. Therefore, no explanatory addition (taqdir) is needed nor is there need to claim 'immediate understanding' (tabādur). This is a standard logical point in Islamic metaphysics: the term 'wajib' is exclusively for the self-necessary, and 'possible' cannot share that description in any genuine sense.

The Logical Meaning of 'Alhamdu lillah' – Scope of the Definite Article
Commentators expand on the logical meaning of 'Alhamdu lillah' and its definite article.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two interpretations of 'al-' in 'al-hamd' as lām al-jins (genus) or istighrāq (comprehensiveness) and explain how each affects the restriction of praise to God.

Core Idea
The definite article 'al-' can indicate the genus or comprehensiveness – a logical distinction with theological implications.

In this passage, commentators unpack the meaning of 'Alhamdu lillah' by analyzing the definite article 'al-' in 'al-hamd'. They show that the phrase carries a logical nuance: it can refer to the essence of praise (genus) or to every individual instance of praise (comprehensiveness). Understanding this distinction is key to grasping why praise is exclusively for God.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two interpretations of 'al-hamd muṭlaqan'
The text says 'al-hamd muṭlaqan' can mean either 'absolute praise' (al-hamd al-muṭlaq) or 'praise in all its individuals' (al-hamd bi-jamī‘ afrādih). Note the two possibilities.
These correspond to different functions of the definite article.
2
Understand the first interpretation: lām al-jins
If 'al-' is for the genus, then 'al-hamd' refers to the reality/essence of praise – praise as a kind. This makes the phrase mean 'absolute praise' (the maximum intensity of praise).
Lām al-jins points to the nature of the thing, not its individual instances.
3
Understand the second interpretation: lām al-istighrāq
If 'al-' is for comprehensiveness (isti‘ghrāq), then 'al-hamd' covers every individual instance of praise – all acts of praise. This gives the meaning 'all praise'.
This is the more common understanding in the phrase 'Alhamdu lillah'.
4
Relate the interpretations to the restriction of praise to God
Both interpretations lead to the conclusion that absolute praise (or all praise) is confined to God, because He alone possesses all perfections. The commentator argues that this restriction is based on God's attribute of gathering all perfections, not just on His essence.
The restriction is thus rationally justified by the perfection of God.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الاستاذ الشيخ محمد الكرمي): قوله « ولدلالته على هذا الاستجماع» أى ولدلالة لفظ «الله» على ذلك صار قولنا: «الحمد لله» في قوة أن يقال: «الحمد المطلق باطلاقه منحصر في حق من هو مستجمع الجميع صفات الكمالات من حيث هو مستجمع الجميع صفات الكمالات فالشارح يريد ان هذه الحيثية هى العلة الناطقة بالحصار الحمد المطلق فى حق الذات الواجب الوجود لا الذات بما هي ذات وهذه الحيثية هى البيئة والبرهان على ادعاء ان الحمد المطلق منحصر فى الله و ان ما سواه ممن يستحق الحمد انما يستحق منه لوناً او الواناً خاصة.   (التقريب ص 6). قوله فى قوة ان يقال : الحمد مطلقا يحتمل ان يكون المراد من ذلك الحمد المطلق و مطلق الحمد و يحتمل ان يكون المراد: الحمد بجميع افراده، فعلى الاول يكون اشارة الى جعل اللام اى اللام الذي في لفظ «الحمد» للجنس والحقيقة وعلى الثانى اشارة الى جعله للاستغراق (محمد على)
And the master Sheikh Muhammad al-Karmi said: His saying: 'and because of its indication of this gathering' i.e., and because the word 'Allah' indicates that, our saying 'Alhamdu lillah' becomes equivalent to saying: 'Absolute praise in its absoluteness is confined to the right of one who is gatherer of all attributes of perfections, inasmuch as he is the gatherer of all attributes of perfections.' So the commentator intends that this consideration is the speaking cause for the restriction of absolute praise to the essence of the Necessary Existence, not to the essence as mere essence. And this consideration is the evidence and proof for the claim that absolute praise is confined to God, and that others who deserve praise only deserve a particular hue or particular hues.   (al-Taqrib p. 6). His saying: 'it becomes as if one said: praise absolutely' – it is possible that what is meant by that is 'absolute praise' and 'absolute praise' (different interpretation), and it is possible that what is meant is 'praise in all its individuals'. On the first, it is an indication of making the lam (i.e., the lam in the word 'al-hamd') for the genus and reality; on the second, it is an indication of making it for comprehensiveness (isti‘ghrāq). (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 129

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the meaning of 'Alhamdu lillah' as used in the original text. The commentator (Muhammad al-Karmi) explains that the word 'Allah' indicates the gathering of all perfect attributes, and therefore the phrase 'Alhamdu lillah' is equivalent to saying that absolute praise (al-hamd al-muṭlaq) is confined to the one who possesses all perfections. The purpose is to show that the restriction is not due to the essence alone but due to the consideration of perfections. Then Muhammad Ali adds that the phrase 'al-hamd muṭlaqan' can be interpreted in two ways: as 'absolute praise' (al-hamd al-muṭlaq) or 'praise in all its individuals' (al-hamd bi-jamī‘ afrādih). The first interpretation treats 'al-' as for the genus/reality, the second treats 'al-' as for comprehensiveness (isti‘ghrāq). This distinction is important in Usul al-Fiqh and logic for determining the scope of general terms.

Key Takeaway
