Two Definitions of Hidayat and Their Quranic Refutations
Author discusses two definitions of 'hidayah' and their refutations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two definitions of hidayah and identify the Quranic counterexamples used to refute each.

Core Idea
Hidayat is defined either as showing the way or ensuring arrival; the Quran uses both meanings.

This passage from a hashia tackles a critical ambiguity in the term 'hidayah' (guidance). Two definitions are proposed, each facing a Quranic objection. Your task is to parse these definitions and see how the grammatical usage supports a resolution. The discussion is essential for understanding divine guidance in theology and tafsir.

Guided Path
1
Extract the two definitions
Read the Arabic: 'قيل: الهداية الدلالة على ما يوصل. وقيل: بل الدلالة الموصلة الى المطلوب'. Identify the key difference: the first defines hidayah as 'indication to that which leads' (showing the way), the second as 'indication that leads to the desired end' (causing arrival).
Note that the first is about indicating a path, the second about actually reaching the goal.
2
Identify the counterexamples
The passage cites two Quranic verses. For the second definition (indication that leads to goal), the verse about Thamud (41:17) shows that guidance was given but they chose blindness, so it did not lead to the goal. For the first definition (indication of what leads), the verse 'You cannot guide whom you love' (28:56) shows that even showing the way may not be effective.
Both definitions face a counterexample from revelation, suggesting the need for metaphor.
3
Consider the metaphorical possibility
The author notes 'و احتمال التجوز مشترك' meaning that both definitions can be interpreted metaphorically to avoid contradiction. This is a common logical move in definitional disputes.
Metaphorical interpretation is a tool to reconcile revelation with a definition.
4
Apply the grammatical distinction from Hashiya al-Kashshaf
The hashia clarifies that the verb 'hada' can be transitive by itself (meaning 'to make reach') or with 'ila' or 'lam' (meaning 'to show the way'). This grammatical rule resolves the apparent conflict: both meanings are valid but correspond to different transitive patterns.
This shows how grammar can refine a definition and explain Quranic usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قيل: الهداية الدلالة على ما يوصل. وقيل: بل الدلالة الموصلة الى المطلوب ورجح الأول ونسب الثانى الى النقض و نقض بقوله تعالى ايضاً: «و اما ثمود فهديناهم .... و الاول منقوض بقوله تعالى ايضاً انك لاتهدى من احببت» و احتمال التجوز مشترك. و للمناقشة في امتناع حمله على هذا المعنى مجال فتأمل. قال في حاشية الكشاف ما حاصله انها يتعدى بنفسها او بالى او باللام وعلى معناها الاول الايصال و على الثانيين ارائة الطريق فافهم ملاجلال الدين
It was said: Guidance (hidāyah) is the indication to that which leads. And it was said: Rather, it is the indication that leads to the desired end. The first was favored, and the second was attributed to being refuted. And it was refuted by His saying, exalted: 'And as for Thamud, we guided them...' (41:17) but they preferred blindness over guidance. And the first is refuted by His saying, exalted: 'Indeed, you cannot guide whom you love' (28:56). And the possibility of metaphorical interpretation is common. And there is room for discussion on the impossibility of carrying it (the first meaning) on this meaning, so reflect. He said in the Hashiya of al-Kashshaf, its gist: that it (the verb) is transitive by itself or by 'ilā' or by 'lām'. On the first meaning, it is 'to make reach' (īṣāl); on the other two, 'to show the way' (irā'at al-ṭarīq). So understand, Mulla Jalal al-Din.
Source pages p. 130

Commentary
This passage is a parsing exercise on the definition of 'hidāyah' (guidance). Two definitions are given: (1) The indication of that which leads (al-dalāla ‘alā mā yūṣil), i.e., showing a path that can reach the goal; (2) The indication that actually reaches the desired end (al-dalāla al-mūṣila ilā al-maṭlūb). The first is preferred, but both face Quranic counterexamples. For the second, the verse about Thamud (they were guided but chose blindness) shows that guidance does not always result in reaching the goal. For the first, the verse 'You cannot guide whom you love' indicates that even showing the path may not be effective for some. The author notes that metaphorical interpretation is possible for both. Then a grammatical point is added from the Hashiya of al-Kashshaf: the verb 'hada' can be transitive by itself (meaning to make reach) or take 'ilā' or 'lām' (meaning to show the way). This discussion is crucial for understanding the nature of divine guidance in Islamic theology and its logical implications.

Homonymy of Al-Hidaya and Resolving Objections in Quranic Interpretation
Author establishes hidaya as homonym and repels two objections

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify homonymy (ishtirak) in key Quranic terms and use it to resolve apparent contradictions between verses.

Core Idea
Homonymy is not ambiguity; it is the key to coherent interpretation of revelation.

In this lesson, we examine how the term 'al-hidaya' (guidance) carries two distinct meanings in the Quran. Recognizing this homonymy allows us to resolve two classical objections that arise if one insists on a single meaning. This skill is essential for anyone engaged in tafsir or usul al-fiqh, where precise lexical analysis prevents false contradictions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of hidaya
From the passage, hidaya is defined as 'indication with gentleness' which is a genus covering two specific meanings: conveying directly to the goal (īṣāl) and showing the way (irātha). Recognize that both are valid interpretations depending on context.
2
Apply each meaning to the two problematic verses
Assign one meaning to each verse: In the first verse (e.g., 'Ihdina al-sirāṭ al-mustaqīm'), hidaya means 'showing the way' (irātha) with an implied preposition. In the second verse (e.g., 'Inna hādhā al-qur'āna yahdī li-llatī hiya aqwam'), it means 'conveying' (īṣāl) without a preposition. This resolves the apparent contradiction.
3
Understand why homonymy is necessary
If hidaya had only one meaning, the two verses would contradict each other. The author argues that because it is a homonym, both verses are coherent and the objections are repelled.
4
Generalize the principle to other Islamic terms
This method is not limited to hidaya. Many terms in the Quran and Sunnah (e.g., wali, hukm) are homonyms. Recognizing this avoids false contradictions and deepens understanding of revelation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فالحق أن الهداية هى الدلالة بلطف مطلقا سواء كانت دلالة موصلة الى المطلوب ام دلالة موصلة على ما يوصل الى المطلوب وسواء كانت متعدية إلى المفعول الثاني بنفسه ام بغيره كما يدل عليه السابقة منا ومن المحشى وقد نص على ذلك جمع من المحققين فاحفظ (محمد على) (۳۲) اى وحين اذ كان الهداية لفظاً مشتركاً بين المعنيين المذكورين الايصال والاراثة، يظهر اندفاع النقضين لأنه يقال انها في الآية الأولى للاراثة والمفعول الثانى المحذوف مقدر مع الى او اللام وفي الآية الثانية للايصال والمفعول الثاني مقدر بدونهما. ثم الفرق بين الدفع والرفع هو: ان الاول يقال لاعدام الشيء قبل مجيئه والثاني لاعدامه بعد مجيئه عكس الوضع. (محمد علی)
The truth is that guidance (al-hidaya) is indication with gentleness absolutely, whether it is an indication that leads to the sought object or an indication that leads to what leads to the sought object, and whether it is transitive to the second object by itself or by something else, as indicated by what preceded from us and from the commentator. A group of verificators have stated this, so memorize it (Muhammad 'Ali). (32) That is, when hidaya is a homonym between the two mentioned meanings — conveyance (al-īṣāl) and showing the way (al-irātha) — the repelling of the two objections becomes apparent, because it is said that in the first verse it is for showing the way, and the omitted second object is estimated with 'to' (ilā) or 'for' (lām), and in the second verse it is for conveyance, and the second object is estimated without them. Then the difference between repelling (daf') and removal (raf') is: the first is said for the abolition of something before its coming, and the second for its abolition after its coming, opposite of the situation. (Muhammad 'Ali)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'al-hidaya' and clarifies its usage in the Quran. The author states that the correct view is that 'hidaya' means 'indication with gentleness' in an absolute sense. It can be either an indication that directly leads to the sought object, or an indication that leads to something that leads to the sought object. It can be transitive to the second object by itself or with the help of a preposition. This definition is supported by earlier statements from the author and the commentator (al-muḥashshī), and a group of scholars have explicitly stated this. The student is urged to memorize this. Then the text explains that because 'hidaya' is a homonym between the meanings of 'conveyance' (īṣāl) and 'showing the way' (irātha), the two objections (naqḍayn) are repelled. In the first verse (presumably 'Ihdina al-sirāṭ al-mustaqīm'), the meaning is 'showing the way', and the implied second object is estimated with a preposition. In the second verse (e.g., 'Inna hādhā al-qur'āna yahdī li-llatī hiya aqwam'), it means 'conveyance', and the second object is estimated without prepositions. The passage then gives a terminological distinction between 'daf'' (repelling) and 'raf'' (removal): daf' is used when something is eliminated before it arrives, while raf' is used after it arrives — this is the opposite of the usual order. This distinction is useful in logical and theological discussions.

Key Terms

الدفع
الرفع
النقضان

Key Takeaway

The Derivation and Significance of Al-Sirat Al-Mustaqim
Author explains the derivation and significance of 'al-sirat al-mustaqim'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the lexical derivation of 'sirat' and its theological significance as the true religion leading to Paradise.

Core Idea
The word 'sirat' is chosen to remind us of the bridge over Hell.

In this lesson, we unpack the opening line of the Fatihah commentary. The author, Shaykh 'Abd al-Rahim, explains not only the pronunciation and etymology of 'sirat', but also why it is the perfect word to describe the path to salvation. We will see how a single word can carry both linguistic and eschatological weight.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word being analyzed
The word is 'al-sirat' in the phrase 'ihdina al-sirat al-mustaqim'. Note its two possible pronunciations: with sin (سراط) and with sad (صراط).
2
Understand the etymology
The root 'sarata' means 'to swallow'. The path is called 'sirat' because it 'swallows' travellers as they pass along it.
Similar to 'luqam' (morsel) which 'takes in' wayfarers.
3
Determine the theological definition
The straight path is the true religion (al-din al-haqq) which Allah accepts. It leads its follower to Paradise.
4
Explore the deeper significance
The choice of 'sirat' over 'tariq' or 'sabil' is to remind us of the actual bridge (sirat) over Hell that must be crossed.
5
Connect to a broader principle
Word choice in the Quran and its commentaries is not arbitrary; it conveys layers of meaning that affect both theology and daily practice.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله نحو اهدنا الصراط المستقيم: السراط بالسين: الجادة من سرط الشيء اذا ابتلعه لانه يسترط المارة اذا سلكوه كما ستى لقماً لانه يلتقم السابلة وبالصاد من قلب السين صاداً لاجل الطاء وهي اللغة الفصيحة. والصّراط المستقيم هو الدين الحق الذى لا يقبل الله تعالى غيره من العباد و انما الدين صراطاً، لانه يؤدى من يسلكه الى الجنة كما ان الصراط يؤدى من يسلكه الى مقصده. و في اختيار الصراط على الطريق والسبيل تذكرة للصراط الذى هو جسر محدود بين طرف جهنم. سهل الله علينا وروده و عبوره شیخ عبدالرحیم)
His saying: 'Guide us to the straight path' (ihdinā al-ṣirāṭ al-mustaqīm): al-sirāṭ with sin (sīn): the main road, from sarata al-shay' (he swallowed it) because it swallows up the passersby when they travel on it, just as it is called luqam (morsel) because it seizes the travellers. And with ṣād (from turning sīn into ṣād because of the letter ṭā') is the eloquent language. And the straight path (al-ṣirāṭ al-mustaqīm) is the true religion (al-dīn al-ḥaqq) which Allah Almighty does not accept any other from His servants. It is called a path (ṣirāṭ) because it leads the one who follows it to Paradise, just as the path leads the one who follows it to his destination. And in choosing the word ṣirāṭ over ṭarīq and sabīl is a reminder of the ṣirāṭ which is a limited bridge between the two sides of Hell. May Allah make easy for us its arrival and crossing. (Shaykh 'Abd al-Raḥīm)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage is an extensive commentary on the phrase 'ihdinā al-ṣirāṭ al-mustaqīm' from Surah al-Fatiha. The author begins by discussing the word 'ṣirāṭ' (path) — it can be pronounced with sīn or with ṣād, the latter being the more eloquent due to the influence of the emphatic ṭā' in the following word. Etymologically, 'sarata' means 'to swallow', and the path is called ṣirāṭ because it 'swallows' the travellers as they pass along it, similar to the word 'luqam' (morsel) because it 'takes in' the wayfarers. Then the meaning is clarified: the straight path is the true religion (al-dīn al-ḥaqq) which Allah accepts from no one else. It is called a 'ṣirāṭ' because it leads its follower to Paradise, just as any physical path leads to its destination. The choice of the word 'ṣirāṭ' over 'ṭarīq' (road) or 'sabīl' (way) is intentional: it serves as a reminder of the actual ṣirāṭ (the bridge) over Hell, which is a narrow bridge stretching between the two sides of Hell. The commentator ends with a prayer for ease in reaching and crossing that bridge.

Key Terms
الصراط المستقيم
السراط/الصراط

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, why is the word 'sirat' used instead of 'tariq' in Surah al-Fatiha?
A) Because 'sirat' is a broader term.
B) To remind of the bridge over Hell.
C) Because 'sirat' is Arabic and 'tariq' is Aramaic.
D) No particular reason.

Key Takeaway
The word 'sirat' is chosen in the Fatihah not only for its meaning of 'path' but also to evoke the eschatological bridge over Hell.

The Lexical Meaning of 'Sawa' al-Tariq' and Its Concomitants
Author defines 'sawa' al-tariq' as the middle of the road and critiques an expansion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the lexical meaning of 'sawa' al-tariq' and its concomitant properties.

Core Idea
Lexical meaning is precise; concomitants are attached but not part of the definition.

In this passage, the author clarifies that 'sawa' al-tariq' simply means 'middle of the road'—nothing more. The idea that it leads to the goal is a concomitant, not part of its lexical meaning. This distinction is crucial for precise definitions in Islamic sciences such as Tafsir and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Locate the phrase 'sawa' al-tariq' in the Arabic passage. What does the author say it means?
The author explicitly says 'ay wasṭuhu' (i.e., its middle).
2
Extract the lexical meaning from the source
Al-Raghib's definition is quoted: 'sawa'' means middle, and it is used as a description and adverb. The author confirms this is the core lexical meaning.
No mention of reaching a goal in the lexical definition.
3
Spot the commentator's addition
The commentator (shāriḥ) added: 'which leads its traveller to the sought object definitively.' Identify where this appears.
This is the expansion being critiqued.
4
Distinguish lexical meaning from concomitant
The author argues that the commentator's addition is not part of the lexical meaning (laysa maʿnan lughawiyyan) but rather a concomitant (min muqāranātihi). Understand the difference: lexical meaning is inherent; concomitants are associated but not part of the definition.
This is the key logical operation the author performs.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله سواء الطريق اى :وسطه قال الراغب فى المفردات و مكان سوى وسواء وسط و يقال سواء و سوی و سوی ای پستوی طرفاه ويستعمل ذلك وصفاً وظرفاً واصل ذلك مصدر وقال في سواء الجحيم و سواء السبيل قال والاستقامة تقال فى الطريق الذى يكون على خط مستر و به شبه طريق المحق نحو اهدنا الصراط المستقيم». و ان هذا صراطى مستقيماً». «على صراط مستقيم». وقول الشارح أى وسطه الذى يفضى سالكه الى المطلوب البتة، ليس معنى لغوياً لسواء الطريق بل الوسط من حيث هو لا يتعدى معناه اللغوى وليس فى معناه اللغوى الافضاء بسا لكه الى المطلوب وانما هو من مقارناته (پايان) (۳۷) قوله يفضى سالكه الى المطلوب اى يأمنه من تغليط الغير. و لفظ «البتة» مصدر لا يستعمله العرب الا بالالف واللام فان حذفهما خطاء عندهم. وقد يجيء بلا تعريف اى)) بدون الالف واللام كما انشد اليزيدى فى مناظرته مع «الكسائي»: او خيرهم بنة ابو بكر. فان من خيرهم و اكرمهم و يفهم من كلام الجوهرى ان ال » غير لازم له (شيخ عبد الرحيم ) قال الجوهري : ويقال : لا افعله «بتة» ولا افعله ( البتة لكل أمر لا رجعة فيه. ونصبه على المصدر و قيل انه اى ان لفظ ال لا يحذف الا فى الضرورة. ثم انهم اختلفوا فى ان الصيغة هل هي متصرفة او غير منصرفة للتأنيث والعلمية فانه علم لقطع خاص فى اى مكان يقع فعدم دخول التنوين على الاول لاجل اللام و على الثانى للمنع من الصرف (میرزا محمد علی)
His saying: 'sawa' al-tariq' i.e., its middle (wasṭuhu). Al-Raghib said in al-Mufradat: 'A place that is suwan or sawā' means middle, and it is said sawā' and suwā and siwā meaning its two ends are equal, and it is used as a description and an adverb, and its origin is a verbal noun.' And he said regarding 'sawā' al-jaḥīm' and 'sawā' al-sabīl'. He said: 'Al-istiqāma (straightness) is said of a path that is on a straight line, and by it the path of truth is likened, as in "Guide us to the straight path", "And this is my straight path", "On a straight path".' The commentator's statement: 'i.e., its middle which leads its traveller to the sought object definitively (al-batta)' is not a lexical meaning of sawā' al-tariq; rather the middle as such does not go beyond its lexical meaning, and in its lexical meaning there is no leading of its traveller to the sought object; rather that is one of its concomitants. (End) (37) His saying: 'it leads its traveller to the sought object' meaning it protects him from being misled by others. And the word 'al-batta' is a verbal noun which the Arabs do not use except with the definite article (al-); deleting it is a mistake according to them. Sometimes it comes without definition, i.e., without the al, as al-Yazīdī recited in his debate with al-Kisā'ī: 'aw khayruhum bannata abū bakr' (or the best of them, the benevolence of Abu Bakr). For he is the best and most generous of them. And it is understood from the words of al-Jawharī that the 'al' is not necessary for it. (Shaykh 'Abd al-Raḥīm) Al-Jawharī said: 'It is said: lā afʿaluhu batta (I will not do it at all) and lā afʿaluhu al-batta for every matter that has no return.' It is declined as a verbal noun (mansūb ʿalā al-maṣdar). And it is said that the word al is not deleted except in necessity. Then they differed as to whether the form is declinable (mutaṣarrifa) or non-declinable (ghayr munṣarifa) due to femininity and proper-noun status, because it is a proper name for a specific cutting off in whatever place it occurs. So the absence of tanwīn in the first is because of the lām, and in the second because of prevention from declension (man' al-ṣarf). (Mīrzā Muḥammad 'Alī)
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage comments on the phrase 'sawa' al-tariq' meaning 'the middle of the road'. It quotes al-Raghib al-Isfahani's lexical explanation that 'sawa'' means middle, and that 'istiqama' (straightness) is applied to a path that is straight, and the path of truth is likened to it. The author then critiques the commentator (shāriḥ) who said that the middle leads the traveller to the goal definitively. He argues that this is not a lexical meaning of 'sawa' al-tariq', but rather a concomitant property: the middle as such, in its lexical sense, does not guarantee reaching the goal; that property is merely associated with it. Then, a separate commentary on the phrase 'yuḍī sālikuhu ilā al-maṭlūb' (it leads its traveller to the sought object) explains that it means protection from being misled by others. The rest of the passage is a detailed grammatical and lexicographical discussion of the word 'al-batta', an adverb meaning 'definitely, absolutely'. It notes that it is normally used with the definite article, though exceptions exist, as in a line of poetry. Al-Jawhari's dictionary is cited, along with a discussion of its declension: some consider it non-declinable due to being a feminine proper noun for a specific absolute cutting off. The passage thus provides both semantic and grammatical clarification.

Key Terms
سواء الطريق
مقارنات

A) Yes, that is its lexical meaning.
B) No, it only means 'middle of the road'; reaching the goal is a concomitant.
C) It means both.
D) The text does not address this.

Key Takeaway
The lexical meaning of 'sawa' al-tariq' is simply 'middle'; 'leading to the goal' is a concomitant, not part of the definition.

The Word 'Al-Batta': Usage and Grammatical Peculiarities
Author explains the word 'al-batta' and its usage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the word 'al-batta', understand its usage with or without the definite article, and recognise its declension.

Core Idea
'Al-batta' is a unique adverb of negation that normally requires the definite article.

The word 'al-batta' (definitely, absolutely) is used in oaths and negations. This passage discusses its origin as a verbal noun, its fixed usage with 'al', exceptions in poetry, and the grammatical debate on its declension. Understanding such lexical details is essential for precise language in Islamic legal and theological texts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term and its basic meaning
Locate 'al-batta' in the Arabic passage. It is a verbal noun meaning absolute cutting off, used as an adverb meaning 'definitely, at all'.
Notice that the author says it is a masdar (verbal noun).
2
Understand the rule for the definite article
The text states that the Arabs do not use 'batta' without the definite article except in poetry. Deleting 'al' is considered a mistake. Find the line of poetry cited as an exception.
The poetry: 'aw khayruhum bannata abū bakr' (or the best of them, the benevolence of Abu Bakr).
3
Recognise the grammatical debate on declension
The passage mentions a difference of opinion: some consider 'al-batta' declinable (mutaṣarrif) with tanwin, others non-declinable (ghayr munṣarif) due to femininity and proper-noun status. Identify the reasons given.
The absence of tanwin is either because of the 'al' or because of prevention from declension.
4
Synthesise the usage
Summarise: 'al-batta' is normally used with 'al', is a verbal noun acting as an adverb, and is considered a proper noun for absolute cutting off. Its declension is debated.
This shows the richness of Arabic lexicography and its impact on interpretation.

What Is Mantiq?

Kinaya: Definition and Mutual Implication
Definition of kinaya and its application to mutual implication

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two definitions of kināya and determine whether a given expression qualifies as kināya by checking mutual implication between its terms.

Core Idea
Kināya is not a fixed one-way relation; it depends on the direction of implication chosen.

This passage from a logical commentary defines the concept of kināya (allusion) and applies it to a specific linguistic pair. Understanding kināya is essential for analyzing figurative language in Islamic sciences, especially in balāgha and tafsīr. The author presents two technical definitions and shows how a phrase like 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' (middle of the road) can serve as a kināya for 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' (the straight road) based on mutual implication.

Guided Path
1
Identify the linguistic definition
The passage first gives the linguistic meaning: kināya is derived from 'kanaytu' meaning 'I alluded without explicit mention'. Note that in language it simply means indirect expression.
2
Distinguish the two technical meanings
According to al-Sakkākī, kināya has two meanings: (1) the act of mentioning the concomitant (lāzim) while intending the concomitant thing (malzūm), with the possibility of also intending the concomitant; (2) the word itself. According to others, it is the reverse: mentioning the malzūm while intending the lāzim.
The second meaning (the word) is often the technical term used in logic and rhetoric.
3
Apply to the example pair
The author applies this to 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' and 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī'. Each is a concomitant (lāzim) and a concomitant thing (malzūm) with respect to the other. Therefore, depending on which definition you adopt, one can be a kināya for the other.
Mutual implication (talāzum) means each implies the other logically.
4
Explain the commentator's note
The commentator (al-muḥashshī) points out that the pair are 'mutalāzimān' (mutually implicative), which cements the fact that either direction of kināya is acceptable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: «الكناية في اللغة مصدر قولك: كنيت بكذا عن كذا و كنوت اذا تركت التصريح به وهى في الاصطلاح يطلق على معنيين احدهما: المعنى المصدرى الذي هو فعل المتكلم اعنى ذكر اللازم وارادة الملزوم مع جواز ارادة اللازم ايضاً. فاللفظ مكنى به والمعنى مكني عنه، والثاني نفس اللفظ » . انتهى واعلم : ان هذا بناء على ظاهر مذهب السكاكي» واما على مذهب غيره فهى ذكر الملزوم وارادة اللازم مع جواز ارادة الملزوم ايضاً وما نحن فيه صالح لكل منهما اذ كل من سواء الطريق والطريق المستوى لازم وملزوم بالنسبة الى الاخر والى هذا المعنى اشار المحشى بقوله: «اذ هما متلازمان فان التلازم من الطرفين. (محمد علی)
His saying: 'Kināya in language is a verbal noun from your saying: kanaytu bi-kadhā 'an kadhā (I alluded by such-and-such to such-and-such) and kanawtu, when you leave explicit mention of it. In terminology, it is applied to two meanings: one is the verbal-noun meaning which is the act of the speaker, i.e., mentioning the concomitant (lāzim) and intending the concomitant thing (malzūm) while also allowing the intention of the concomitant itself. So the word is the kināya bih (the instrument of allusion), and the meaning is the kināya 'anhu (the object of allusion). The second meaning is the word itself.' End. Know that this is based on the apparent doctrine of al-Sakkākī. As for the doctrine of others, it is mentioning the malzūm and intending the lāzim, while also allowing the intention of the malzūm itself. And what we are dealing with (i.e., sawā' al-ṭarīq and al-ṭarīq al-mustawī) is suitable for each of these two definitions, because each of sawā' al-ṭarīq and al-ṭarīq al-mustawī is a concomitant (lāzim) and a concomitant thing (malzūm) with respect to the other. And to this meaning the commentator (al-muḥashshī) alluded by his saying: 'for they are mutually implicative (mutalāzimān), since the mutual implication (al-talāzum) is from both sides.' (Muḥammad 'Alī)
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'kināya' (allusion/indirection). It begins with the linguistic meaning: it is the verbal noun from 'kanaytu' meaning 'I alluded' without explicit mention. Then the technical meaning according to al-Sakkākī: it has two meanings: (1) the act of the speaker, which is mentioning the necessary concomitant (lāzim) while intending the thing of which it is a concomitant (malzūm), with the possibility that the speaker also intends the concomitant itself (i.e., the phrase could be used literally as well). The word is called 'maknī bih' and the meaning is 'maknī 'anhu'. (2) The second technical meaning is the word itself (i.e., the expression that serves as the kināya). The author then notes that this is the view of al-Sakkākī. According to others (like the theologians?), kināya is the opposite: mentioning the malzūm while intending the lāzim, again with the possibility of intending the malzūm itself. The author then applies this to the case at hand: the phrase 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' (middle of the road) and 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' (the straight road) are mutually implicative (mutalāzimān). Therefore, each can be a kināya for the other according to either definition: if one takes 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' as the lāzim for 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' (since the middle is a property of the straight road), then mentioning 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' and intending 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' fits al-Sakkākī's definition; conversely, if one takes 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' as the lāzim for 'sawā' al-ṭarīq', then the reverse fits the other definition. The commentator (al-muḥashshī) indicated this by saying 'for they are mutually implicative'.

Key Terms
الكناية
اللازم والملزوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the relationship between 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' and 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī'?
A) They are synonyms.
B) They are mutually implicative (mutalāzimān).
C) One is general, the other specific.
D) They have no relation.

Key Takeaway
Kināya is defined by the relationship of implication between two terms, which can be mutual, allowing flexible figurative usage.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Defending a Kinaya Interpretation: Al-Dawani's Sawa' al-Tariq
Author defends al-Dawani's interpretation of 'sawa' al-tariq' as 'al-tariq al-mustawi' by explaining it as a kinaya, not a lexical claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a lexical definition and a kinaya-based interpretation in scholarly commentary, and evaluate an objection against a commentator's reading using the concept of 'urf (convention).

Core Idea
A commentator can explain a term's intended meaning via kinaya without claiming it is the literal definition.

In this passage, the author defends al-Dawani against critics who accused him of misinterpreting the phrase 'sawa' al-tariq'. The defense hinges on the difference between lexical meaning and allusive (kinaya) meaning. Understanding this distinction is key to reading scholarly commentary in the Islamic tradition, where commentators often explain the import of a phrase without altering its literal sense.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find the specific criticism against al-Dawani. The critics claimed he made 'sawa'' mean 'istiwā' (straightness) and then used it as the adjective 'mustawī', forcing the phrase into an unnatural grammatical construction (iḍāfat al-ṣifa ilā al-mawṣūf).
2
Understand the conventional meaning
Recognize that 'sawa' al-ṭarīq' conventionally means 'the middle of the road' (wasṭ al-ṭarīq), as the author states. This is supported by the parallel phrase 'sawa' al-jaḥīm' (middle of Hell).
3
Recognize the kinaya mechanism
Grasp that al-Dawani was not providing a lexical equivalent but explaining the intended meaning (ḥāṣil al-ma'nā al-maqṣūd) of 'wasṭ al-ṭarīq' as a kinaya for 'the straight road'. The middle of the road alludes to the straight path.
Kinaya is a figurative expression where the literal meaning is not the intended one.
4
Evaluate the defense
The author argues that the kinaya reading is stronger because it accords with conventional usage and yields a more appropriate praise (ḥamd) for guiding to the middle of the road rather than merely to a straight road.
The defense shows that al-Dawani's interpretation is consistent and not a linguistic error.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله كناية عن الطريق المستوى وذلك لانه لو كان المراد بوسط الطريق معناه الظاهرى لما كان معنى الكلام متعارفاً كما لا يخفى وقوله : وهذا مراد من فسره» اشارة الى دفع ما اورد على الفاضل الدوانى حيث فسره فى حاشيته على تلك الرسالة سواء الطريق بالطريق المستوى والصراط المستقيم فاعترضوا عليه بانه جعل السواء بمعنى الاستواء ثم استعمله بمعنى المستوى، فجعل كلام المصنف من قبيل اضافة الصفة الى الموصوف كجرد قطيفة وهذا تكلف اذا المتعارف بين اهل العرف هو ان سواء الطريق بمعنى وسطه كما ان سواء الجحيم بمعنى وسطه وايضاً لوكان من باب اضافة الصفة الى الموصوف كان المقصود اظهار الحمد على ايصاله إلى الطريق المستقيم و اذا كان بمعنى الوسط فيكون المقصود: الحمد على ايصاله إلى وسط الطريق وهذا اقوى من الأول فالحمد عليه اولى. و حاصل الدفع ان ليس غرضه من ذلك التفسير ان معنى السواء ذلك، بل غرضه بیان حاصل المعنى المقصود من وسط الطريق كناية فلذا فسره بالطريق المستوى والصراط المستقيم . (عبد الرحيم)
His saying: 'as a kināya (allusion) for the straight road (al-ṭarīq al-mustawī)' — and that is because if the intended meaning of 'middle of the road' (wasṭ al-ṭarīq) were its apparent meaning, then the meaning of the speech would not be conventional (mut'āraf), as is not hidden. And his saying: 'This is what was intended by the one who interpreted it' is an indication of repelling the objection raised against al-Fāḍil al-Dawānī, where he interpreted in his gloss on that treatise 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' as 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' and 'al-ṣirāṭ al-mustaqīm'. They objected to him that he made 'sawā'' to mean 'al-istiwā' (straightness) and then used it in the sense of 'al-mustawī' (the straight one), thus making the author's statement an instance of adding the adjective to the described (iḍāfat al-ṣifa ilā al-mawṣūf) like 'jard quṭayfa'. This is forced, because the conventional understanding among people is that 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' means its middle, just as 'sawā' al-jaḥīm' means its middle. Also, if it were from the category of adding the adjective to the described, the intended meaning would be to show praise for guiding to the straight path, but if it means 'middle', then the intended meaning is praise for guiding to the middle of the road. This is stronger than the first, so praising for that is more appropriate. The gist of the defense is that his purpose in that interpretation was not that the meaning of 'sawā'' is 'istiwā', rather his purpose was to express the intended meaning of 'middle of the road' as a kināya. Therefore he interpreted it as 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' and 'al-ṣirāṭ al-mustaqīm'. ('Abd al-Raḥīm)
Source pages p. 134

Commentary
This passage defends the interpretation of al-Fāḍil al-Dawānī, who in his gloss explained 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' as 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' (the straight road) and 'al-ṣirāṭ al-mustaqīm' (the straight path). Critics objected that al-Dawānī was treating 'sawā' as a synonym of 'istiwā' (straightness) and then using it as the adjective 'mustawī', making it a case of 'iḍāfat al-ṣifa ilā al-mawṣūf' (addition of adjective to described), which is unnatural. The author rebuts this criticism. First, he explains that 'sawā' al-ṭarīq' conventionally means 'wasṭ al-ṭarīq' (the middle of the road), just as 'sawā' al-jaḥīm' means the middle of Hell. So the literal meaning is 'middle', not 'straight'. Second, if one insisted on the 'adjective + described' reading, the praise (ḥamd) would be for guiding to the straight path. But if it means 'middle', the praise is for guiding to the middle of the road. The author argues that the latter interpretation is stronger and more appropriate. The core of the defense is that al-Dawānī was not giving a lexical definition; rather, he was explaining the intended meaning (ḥāṣil al-ma'nā al-maqṣūd) of 'wasṭ al-ṭarīq' as a kināya. That is, the middle of the road is an allusion to the straight path. Therefore, al-Dawānī's interpretation is consistent and not a linguistic error.

Key Terms
سواء الطريق (sawā' al-ṭarīq): Conventionally means 'the middle of the road' (wasṭ al-ṭarīq). It is used as a kināya for 'al-ṭarīq al-mustawī' (the straight road).
الإيراد (al-īrād): An objection or criticism raised against an interpretation.
كناية (kināya): A rhetorical device where a phrase is used to allude to something else indirectly, based on conventional association.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He misunderstood the word 'sawā'.
B) He made 'sawā' mean 'istiwā' and then used it as 'mustawī' in an unnatural grammatical construction.
C) He used a non-Arabic word.
D) He ignored the Quranic context.

Key Takeaway
The defense of al-Dawānī shows that a commentator can use kināya to convey the intended meaning without altering the literal sense, and understanding this distinction prevents misreading of scholarly commentary.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. In al-Baydawi's tafsir, he interprets 'al-raḥmān' as both 'the merciful' and 'the one who bestows mercy'. Explain whether this is a kināya or lexical claim, using the concepts from this lesson.
?

The Imamiyya Definition of 'عترة' as the Twelve Imams
Defines 'عترة' as offspring and cites a hadith specifying the twelve Imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the specific referent of 'عترة' in the Imamiyya tradition and contrast it with alternative interpretations.

Core Idea
In Imamiyya, 'عترة' means the twelve Imams, not all relatives.

The term 'عترة' appears in the famous hadith of the two weighty things. While some scholars interpret it broadly as 'followers' or 'clan', the Imamiyya view specifies it as the Prophet's household including the twelve Imams. This lesson unpacks that definition and its basis in a hadith from Imam Ali.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term under definition
Locate 'عترة' in the passage. Note its linguistic range: offspring, clan, tribe.
The passage also defines 'آل' as a collective noun with no singular.
2
Contrast the two interpretations
The Imamiyya view: 'عترة' means offspring. Al-Nūrī and al-Azharī: it means 'followers'.
This contrast shows a critical refinement in meaning.
3
Analyse the hadith evidence
Read Imam Ali's response: he includes himself, al-Hasan, al-Husayn, and the nine Imams from al-Husayn's descendants. The last is the Mahdi.
The hadith provides the specific referent: the twelve Imams.
4
Understand the doctrinal significance
This definition ties the Imams to the Quran as equal 'weighty things', establishing their authority and inseparability from scripture.
Without this definition, the Imamiyya argument for succession loses its scriptural basis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله و آله عترته الآل اسم جمع لا واحد له من لفظه والعترة بالكسر نسل الرجل ورهطه : و عشيرته. ثم هذا المعنى الذى ذكره المحشى هو مذهب الامامية وذهب النورى والازهرى الى انه بمعنى الاتباع (عبد الرحيم). في حديث الصادق (ع) عن آبائه عن الحسن بن علی (ع) قال سئل امير المؤمنين (ع) عن معنى قول رسول الله (ص): «انى مخلف فيكم الثقلين كتاب الله و عترتى من العترة؟ فقال (ع): «انا والحسن و الحسين و الائمة التسعة من ولد الحسين تاسعهم مهديهم و قائمهم لا يفارقون كتاب الله ولا يفارقهم حتى يردوا على الرسول (ص) حوضه». (محمد علی)
His statement: 'and his family (āl) are his progeny (ʿitra)'; 'āl' is a collective noun with no singular from its own root; 'ʿitra' (with kasra) means a man's offspring, his clan, and his tribe. Then this meaning mentioned by the commentator is the view of the Imāmiyya. Al-Nūrī and al-Azharī held that it means 'followers' (ʿAbd al-Raḥīm). In a ḥadīth of al-Ṣādiq (a) from his fathers from al-Ḥasan b. ʿAlī (a): Amīr al-Mu'minīn (a) was asked about the meaning of the Prophet's (ṣ) statement: 'I am leaving among you two weighty things: the Book of God and my progeny (ʿitratī), who are my ʿitra?' He (a) said: 'I, al-Ḥasan, al-Ḥusayn, and the nine Imāms from the descendants of al-Ḥusayn, the ninth of whom is their Mahdī and Qā'im; they will not separate from the Book of God until they return to the Prophet (ṣ) at his pond.' (Muḥammad ʿAlī)
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a gloss clarifying the term 'عترة' (ʿitra) used in the text. The commentator notes that 'آل' is a collective noun with no singular, and 'عترة' means a man's offspring, clan, and tribe. The author of the gloss (ʿAbd al-Raḥīm) identifies this meaning as the Imāmiyya view, contrasting with al-Nūrī and al-Azharī who interpret it as 'followers'. A hadith is then cited from Imam al-Ṣādiq (a) through his fathers, where Imam ʿAlī (a) answers a question about the Prophet's statement about the two weighty things. Imam ʿAlī specifies that 'عترتي' refers to himself, al-Ḥasan, al-Ḥusayn, and the nine Imāms from the descendants of al-Ḥusayn, the last being the Mahdī. This establishes the Imāmiyya doctrine that the 'عترة' are the Twelve Imāms, inseparable from the Quran. The lesson is a doctrinal definition embedded in a logical text.

Key Terms
عترة
آل
ثقلين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway

Plurals of 'صاحب' and Defining 'الصحابة'
Definition of الصحابة and morphological analysis of its plural forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a regular verbal plural and a semantic collective in Arabic morphology and identify the key views on who qualifies as a Companion.

Core Idea
'أصحاب' is not the plural of 'صاحب'—it is a semantic collective.

In this passage, the commentator first presents the common definitions of 'الصحابة' (the Companions) and then dives into a grammatical refinement: the word 'أصحاب' is not actually the regular broken plural of 'صاحب' but a collective noun. This may seem like a narrow linguistic point, but it affects how we understand classical texts that use these forms. We will walk through the definition and the grammatical argument step by step.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definitions of 'الصحابة'
Read the first few sentences. The commentator lists two views: one that the Companions are those who believed and accepted the Sharia, and another that they are those who accompanied the Prophet and narrated from him. The common view among the general public is also mentioned.
Notice that the definition includes a condition of 'seeing' the Prophet.
2
Extract the grammatical claim about 'أصحاب'
The commentator states that 'أصحاب' is a plural of 'صحب' (with a kasra), which is a shortened form of 'صاحب'. He supports this by analogy with 'نمر' and its many plurals. He then clarifies that 'أصحاب' is not a regular plural of 'صاحب' because the pattern 'فاعل' does not take the plural form 'أفعال'.
The key point: 'أصحاب' is a collective noun (اسم جمع), not a broken plural of 'صاحب'.
3
Understand the commentator's correction
The commentator critiques the original author (المصنف) who, in some of his works, said that 'أصحاب' is the plural of 'صاحب'. The commentator argues that if the author meant a verbal plural, he is wrong; rather, the author must have meant a semantic plural (جمع معنوي).
This is a critical refinement typical in commentaries.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله مع الأيمان أى مع الاعتقاد وقبول الشريعة وقيل هم الذين ادركوا صحبة النبي (ص) و رووا عنه (ص) ايضاً. و المشهور بين العامة ان الصحابة كل مسلم رأى الرسول (ص) و قيل: او رأه الرسول (ص). ثم الظاهر انه (اصحاب) جمع صحب بالكسر مخفف صاحب كنمر (بفتح النون وكسر الميم وبكسر التون و سكون الميم و بفتح النون و سكون الميم) و انمار (و ايضاً جاء جمعه على وزن أَنمُر وتُمْر ويمار و نمارة و نمر و نُمور و تُمورة وهو ضرب من السباع من عائلة السنور أصغر من الاسد المنجد) و جمع صحب بالسكون (اى سكون الحاء) اسم جمع كنهر و انهار لاجع صاحب اذالمشهور ان فاعلا لا يجمع على افعال كما ذكره المصنف و جمع الصاحب صحب بسكون الحاء كراكب و ركب و صحاب (بكسر الصاد كجایع و جياع و صحبان بضم الصاد و سكون الحاء وصحابة بالكسر والفتح و اصاحيب جمع اصحاب کشاب و شيبان اما ما قاله المصنف فى بعض تصانيفه انه جمع صاحب قلم يرد أنه جمع لفظى بل اراد به أنه جمع معنوی (شیخ عبد الرحيم)
His statement: 'with faith' i.e. with belief and acceptance of the Sharia. It is said, they are those who attained companionship of the Prophet (ṣ) and also narrated from him (ṣ). The common view among the general public is that the Companions are every Muslim who saw the Messenger of God (ṣ), and it is said: or whom the Messenger of God saw. Then it appears that 'aṣḥāb' is a plural of 'ṣaḥb' (with kasra) which is a shortened form of 'ṣāḥib', like 'namir' (with fatḥa nūn, kasra mīm, and also with kasra nūn, sukūn mīm, and with fatḥa nūn, sukūn mīm) and 'anmār' (also its plural on the pattern of 'anmur', 'tumr', 'yimār', 'nimāra', 'nimr', 'numūr', 'tumūra', which is a type of beast of prey from the feline family, smaller than the lion). And the plural of 'ṣaḥb' with sukūn (i.e., sukūn of the ḥā') is a collective noun like 'nahr' and 'anhār'; it is not a plural of 'ṣāḥib' because it is well-known that 'fāʿil' does not form a plural to 'afʿāl' as the author mentioned. And the plural of 'al-ṣāḥib' is 'ṣaḥb' with sukūn ḥā', like 'rākib' and 'rakb', and 'ṣiḥāb' (with kasr ṣād) like 'jāyiʿ' and 'jiyāʿ', and 'ṣuḥbān' (with ḍamm ṣād, sukūn ḥā'), and 'ṣaḥāba' (with kasr and fatḥa), and 'aṣāḥīb' is the plural of 'aṣḥāb' like 'shāb' and 'shībān'. As for what the author said in some of his works that it is the plural of 'ṣāḥib', it is not to be accepted as a verbal plural; rather, he meant it as a semantic plural. (Shaykh ʿAbd al-Raḥīm)
Source pages p. 142

Commentary
This passage is a gloss on the term 'الصحابة' (al-ṣaḥāba). It first defines it: according to one view, the Companions are those who believed and accepted the Sharia, and another view says they are those who attained the companionship of the Prophet and narrated from him. The common view among the general populace is that every Muslim who saw the Prophet is a Companion. Then the commentator delves into the grammatical plural forms of 'صاحب' (ṣāḥib). He argues that 'أصحاب' (aṣḥāb) is not actually a plural of 'صاحب' in the standard sense, but rather a collective noun derived from 'صحب' (ṣaḥb, with kasra), which is a shortened form. He lists many plural forms from Arabic morphology, drawing analogies with other words like 'نمر' (namir) and its numerous plurals. The point is that 'أصحاب' is a collective, not a broken plural of 'فاعل'. The commentator corrects the author's claim that 'أصحاب' is the plural of 'صاحب'; he says it is a semantic plural (meaning it serves the function but is not the regular plural). This is a grammatical refinement typical in studying Islamic texts.

Key Terms
صحابة
صاحب
جمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) 'أصحاب' is the regular broken plural of 'صاحب'.
B) 'أصحاب' is a semantic plural, not a regular verbal plural.
C) 'أصحاب' is the dual form.
D) 'أصحاب' is a singular noun.

Key Takeaway
The lesson shows that careful attention to morphology is essential in Islamic scholarship, as a single word like 'أصحاب' can carry deep implications for understanding the Companions of the Prophet.

Distinguishing Sidq from Haqq: Mutual Correspondence
Commentator explains mutual correspondence of sidq and haqq.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between sidq and haqq based on the direction of correspondence, and explain why this distinction matters for Islamic epistemology.

Core Idea
Sidq is statement-to-reality; Haqq is reality-to-statement.

In Islamic logic, truth is not one-dimensional. This passage from the commentary clarifies that sidq and haqq are two sides of the same coin: mutual correspondence. Understanding this distinction helps you avoid conflating the truth of a statement with the reality it describes, a precision essential in Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two terms
Locate 'sidq' and 'haqq' in the Arabic passage. Note that they are being contrasted.
2
Recognize mutual correspondence
The passage states that sidq and haqq involve mutual correspondence (mutabaqa) between statement/belief and reality. Each matches the other.
The term mutabaqa means mutual fitting.
3
Determine the direction for sidq
Sidq is when the statement or belief corresponds to reality. The statement is true because it matches how things are.
The commentator says: statement/belief insofar as they correspond to reality are called sidq.
4
Determine the direction for haqq
Haqq is when reality corresponds to the statement or belief. The reality is said to be haqq because it matches what is stated.
The commentator says: reality insofar as it corresponds to them is called haqq.
5
Understand the naming rationale
Sidq originally means truthful reporting, so it fits the statement-to-reality direction. Haqq means fixed/established, so it fits reality matching the statement.
This etymological reasoning helps remember which term applies to which direction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
و منه قوله تعالى شرعة ومنهاجاً ، يقال : طريق ناهجة اى واضحة (محمد على) (٧٤) الظاهر ان غرضه من هذا التحقيق مجرد بيان الفرق بين الصدق و الحق ليكون كلام المصنف عارية عن شائبة التكرار و صحة الاعادة في الجملة. ثم الظاهر ايضاً ان غرضه من زيادة قوله: «والاعتقاد» الايماء الى ان المطابقة وعدمها للاعتقاد والحكم اولاً وبالذات وللخبر ثانياً وبالعرض من حيث كونه حاكياً و دالاً. فتامل (محمد على) (٧٥) قوله فان المفاعلة من الطرفين يعنى اذا صدق ان هذا طابق ذاك ، فذاك ايضاً مطابق لهذا. فالخبر و الاعتقاد من حيث انهما مطابقان (بالكسر) للواقع يسميان صدقاً و هما ايضاً من حيث ان الواقع مطابق لهما يسميان حقاً وقد يطلق الصدق و الحق على المفهوم الانتزاعى اعنى المطابقية (بالكسر) و المطابقية (بالفتح ايضاً. (التقريب ص ١٠) (٧٦) قوله فمن حيث انه :مطابق فان :قلت فما وجه تخصيص التسمية بالصدق بالحيثية الاولى و التسمية بالحق بالحيثية الثانية؟ :قلت اما وجه الأول فظاهر قان الصدق فى الاصل هو الاخبار عن الشيء على ما هو عليه، ستى به الخبر، تسمية الشيء باسم مسببه ولا ريب أن هذا انما يناسب الخبر من الحيثية الاولى لما فيها من ملاحظة مطابقة الخبر للمواقع و اما الثانى فلان الخبر بتلك الحيثية اعنى الحيثية الثانية يجعل امراً ثابتاً محققاً و يعتبر مطابقة الواقع له : والحق ، الامر الثابت (میرزا محمد علی (ره) (٧٧) ای بكسر الباء في لفظ المطابق
And from this is His saying: 'a shari'ah and a minhaj'. It is said: 'a clear path' meaning obvious (Muhammad Ali). (74) Apparently his aim from this investigation is merely to clarify the difference between sidq and haqq so that the author's speech is free from the taint of repetition and the correctness of repetition in general. Then apparently also his aim in adding his statement 'and belief' is to indicate that correspondence and non-correspondence are for belief and judgment first and essentially, and for the statement second and accidentally insofar as it is reporting and indicating. So reflect (Muhammad Ali). (75) His statement: for mutual interaction is from both sides means if it is true that this corresponds to that, then that also corresponds to this. So the statement and belief insofar as they correspond (with kasra of the ba) to reality are called sidq, and they also insofar as reality corresponds to them are called haqq. And sidq and haqq may also be applied to the abstract concept, i.e., correspondence (with kasra) and correspondence (with fatha also). (76) His statement: for insofar as it corresponds. For if you say: what is the reason for specifying the naming with sidq for the first aspect and naming with haqq for the second aspect? I say: as for the first, it is obvious because sidq originally is reporting about a thing as it is, so the statement is named by it, naming a thing by the name of its cause. There is no doubt that this is only appropriate for the statement from the first aspect because in it there is observation of the correspondence of the statement to reality. As for the second, because the statement in that aspect, i.e., the second aspect, makes it a fixed, verified thing and considers the correspondence of reality to it; and haqq is the fixed thing. (77) i.e., with kasra of the ba in the word 'al-mutabiq'.
Source pages p. 143

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the distinction between sidq (truth) and haqq (reality). The commentator begins by quoting a Quranic verse to illustrate the meaning of 'clear path'. Then he explains that the author's goal in this section is to differentiate between sidq and haqq to avoid redundancy. The author adds 'belief' to indicate that correspondence primarily applies to belief and judgment, and secondarily to statements. The key point is that sidq and haqq involve mutual correspondence: a statement is sidq if it corresponds to reality; reality is haqq if it corresponds to the statement. The commentator then discusses why sidq is used for the first aspect and haqq for the second: sidq originally means reporting a thing as it is, so it suits the statement's correspondence to reality; haqq means a fixed thing, so it suits reality's correspondence to the statement. A note on pronunciation is added.

Key Terms
الصدق
الحق
المطابقة

Checkpoint

A) Because the statement itself is fixed and unchanging.
B) Because the statement corresponds to reality.
C) Because reality corresponds to the statement.
D) Because the statement is believed by many people.

Key Takeaway
Sidq and haqq are relational terms describing mutual correspondence between statement/belief and reality, with the direction of correspondence determining which term applies.

Distinguishing Verbal and Mental Speech
Definition of verbal and mental speech with example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between verbal and mental speech and identify which of the two is the intended subject of logic in the Islamic tradition.

Core Idea
Logic studies mental speech, not just spoken words.

In Islamic logic, speech is not limited to audible utterances. The author distinguishes two types: verbal and mental. This distinction is crucial because the objects of logical analysis — concepts and propositions — are mental, even though they are expressed verbally.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Find the definitions of verbal speech and mental speech in the Arabic text. Verbal speech is what a person utters; mental speech is what he conceives in the mind.
2
Extract the relationship between them
Notice that verbal speech is produced in accordance with mental speech. Mental speech is the meaning or mental form that the verbal expression matches.
The author uses the example 'Zaid is standing' to illustrate both.
3
Apply to a new example
Take the proposition 'The world is created'. Its verbal speech is the utterance or writing of these words. Its mental speech is the conceptual meaning in the mind: the judgment that the world has a creator.
This helps internalise the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۷) الكلام اللفظى ما يتلفظ به الانسان و الكلام النفسى ما يتصوره في الذهن و يأتي بالكلام اللفظى على طبقه، فزيد قائم مثلا كلام لفظی و معناه وهي: الصورة الحاصلة في الذهن المطابقة لهذا الكلام الخارجى - كلام نفسى ووجه التسمية ظاهر في كليهما. (محمد على)
(97) Verbal speech (al-kalam al-lafzi) is what a person utters, and mental speech (al-kalam al-nafsi) is what he conceives in the mind and then produces verbal speech in accordance with it. For example, 'Zaid is standing' is verbal speech, and its meaning — which is the mental form corresponding to this external speech — is mental speech. The reason for the naming is apparent in both cases. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
This passage provides a clear definition of two types of speech: verbal and mental. The definition is straightforward: verbal speech is the audible utterance, while mental speech is the conceptual meaning in the mind that the verbal speech matches. The example 'Zaid is standing' (زيد قائم) illustrates both: the uttered sentence is verbal speech, and the mental image or proposition that 'Zaid is standing' is mental speech. The commentator notes that the reason for calling them 'speech' is obvious for both: one is literally spoken, the other is an inner 'speech' of the soul. This distinction is crucial in logic and theology, especially in discussions of the eternity of the Quran and the nature of divine speech.

Key Terms
الكلام اللفظي
الكلام النفسي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sound of the adhan (call to prayer).
B) The concept that 'giving charity is good' understood in the mind.
C) The written letters of the Quran on a page.
D) The spoken words of a Friday sermon.

Key Takeaway
Mental speech is the meaning in the mind; verbal speech is the outward expression of that meaning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Unpacking the Definition of Logic: Instrument, Law, and Thought
Commentator defines logic as an instrument for safeguarding the intellect and explains why this is a description rather than a strict definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the components of the definition 'logic is an instrumental legal tool' and explain how each term (āla, qānūn, fikr, ʿiṣma) contributes to the definition.

Core Idea
The definition of logic is a description (rasm) because it uses accidental properties and purpose.

The commentator Mirza Muhammad Ali unpacks the widely cited definition of logic as an 'instrumental legal tool'. In this lesson, we examine each term and how they collectively form a description of logic. Understanding this will help you distinguish between a strict definition (ḥadd) and a description (rasm) in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Find what is being defined. In this passage, it is logic (al-mantiq). The commentary starts with 'His statement: And logic is an instrumental tool'.
2
Analyze the genus: āla (tool)
The commentator explains that a tool (āla) is an intermediary between agent and effect, like a pen for a writer. Logic is a tool because it mediates between the rational faculty and acquired knowledge.
The term 'tool' serves as the genus in this description.
3
Analyze the differentia: qānūniyya (legal)
The word 'legal' (qānūniyya) specifies that logic is a law, a universal proposition from which particular rulings are derived. It is a remote differentia that excludes particular tools of craftsmen.
The commentator notes that qānūn is a Greek or Syriac word.
4
Understand the final condition: safeguarding from error in discourse
The definition includes that observance of logic safeguards from error in discourse, not just in thought. This is a proximate differentia that excludes other legal sciences like grammar and morphology.
Grammar safeguards discourse but not thought; logic safeguards both.
5
Examine the exclusions (iḥtirāzāt)
The commentator outlines what the definition includes and excludes: 'tool' as genus includes all tools, but 'legal' excludes non-legal tools; the final condition excludes sciences that safeguard discourse but not thought.
This shows the definition is carefully constructed as a rasm with genus and differentiae.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والمنطق آلة قانونية الالة هى الواسطة بين الفاعل و منفعله في وصول اثره اليه كالقلم للكاتب قائه واسطة بينه وبين المكتوب في وصول اثره اليه. والقانون لفظ يونانى او سرياني موضوع في لغتهم لمسطر الكتابة وفى الاصطلاح قضية كلية تعرف منها احكام جزئيات موضوعها و سيأتى ذلك مفصلاً عند شرح قول المصنف: «فاحتيج إلى قانون» انشاء الله تعالى. والفكر هو ترتيب أمور معلومة لتحصيل امور مجهولة وسيأتي تفصيله انشاء الله تعالى. ثم انما كان المنطق آلة لكونها واسطة بين القوة العاقلة والمطالب الكسبية في الاكتساب، وإنما كان قانوناً، لان مسائله قوانين كلية تعرف منها أحكام موضوعاتها. كما اذا عرفنا مثلاً ان الموجبة تنعكس إلى الموجبة الجزئية، عرفنا ان :قولنا كل انسان حیوان تنعكس الى قولنا بعض الحيوان انسان» و هكذا. وانما نسب العصمة إلى مراعاتها، لان نفس المنطق ليس بعاصم عن الخطاء والا لوجب ان لا يصدر خطاء عن المنطقى مع انه ربما يعرض الخطاء له لاهماله وعدم مراعاته الألة و هو ظاهر. هذا مفهوم التعريف و اما احترازاته فالالة بمنزلة الجنس وقوله ،قانونية بمنزلة الفصل البعيد يخرج الآلات الجزئية لارباب الصنايع فى صنايعهم. والقيد الاخير بمنزلة الفصل القريب يخرج العلوم القانونية التي تعصم مراعاتها عن الخطاء في المقال لا في الفكر كالنحو والصرف وغيرهما من العلوم الادبية. ثم ان التعريف رسم للمنطق لاحد له فان الحد انما يكون بالذاتيات كما سيجيء، وكونه آلة ليس من الذاتيات بل من العوارض فان الذاتى للشيء يكون له فى نفسه والآلية للمنطق ليست له في نفسه بل بالقياس الى سائر العلوم وايضاً هو تعريف بالغاية اذ العصمة عن الخطاء انما هي غاية للمنطق وغاية الشيء تكون خارجة عنه والتعريف بالخارج رسم هذا. و ربما قيل على الوجه الاول انه ربما يحصل الآلتية للمنطق بالقياس الى نفسه لظهور أن بعض المسائل المنطقية آلة للبعض. واجيب بان حصول الآلتية انما هو بالقياس الى البعض الاخر لا الى نفسه و حصول الآلتية لنفسه انما يكون اذا كان كل مسألة من مسائله آلة بالقياس الى نفسه. ولا يخفى ان هذا الجواب انما هو من باب المجاراة والمماشاة مع الخصم والا فيمكن ان يقال: انه يكفى في كون الآلتية عرضياً حصولها بالقياس الى علم آخر و إن كان حاصلاً بالقياس الى نفسه ايضاً اذ الذاتي للشيء يجب ان يكون له فى نفسه فقط. فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'And logic is an instrumental tool' — the tool is the intermediary between the agent and its object in the transmission of its effect to it, like the pen for the writer, for it is an intermediary between him and the written in the transmission of his effect to it. And 'law' (qānūn) is a Greek or Syriac word coined in their language for the ruler of writing; in technical terminology it is a universal proposition from which are known the rulings of the particulars of its subject. This will come in detail when explaining the author's statement: 'So there was need for a law', God willing. And 'thought' (fikr) is the ordering of known matters to obtain unknown matters; its detail will come, God willing. Then logic is a tool only because it is an intermediary between the rational faculty and the acquired demands in acquisition. It is a law only because its issues are universal laws from which are known the rulings of their subjects. For example, if we know that the affirmative proposition converts to the particular affirmative, we know that our statement 'Every human is an animal' converts to our statement 'Some animal is a human', and so on. And 'infallibility' (ʿiṣma) is attributed to its observance, because logic itself is not a safeguard against error; otherwise it would be necessary that no error should occur from a logician, yet error may occur to him due to negligence and non-observance of the tool. This is apparent. This is the concept of the definition. As for its exclusions (iḥtirāzāt): 'tool' serves as a genus, and his statement 'legal' serves as a remote differentia, excluding the particular tools of the craftsmen in their crafts. And the last condition serves as a proximate differentia, excluding the legal sciences the observance of which safeguards against error in discourse but not in thought, such as grammar, morphology, and other literary sciences. Then the definition is a description (rasm) of logic, not a strict definition (ḥadd), for a strict definition is only by essential properties (dhātiyyāt) as will come. Its being a tool is not of the essential properties but of the accidents (aʿrāḍ), for the essential of a thing belongs to it in itself, and instrumentality for logic does not belong to it in itself but in relation to other sciences. Also, it is a definition by purpose (ghāya), because safeguarding from error is only the purpose of logic, and the purpose of a thing is external to it, and definition by what is external is a description. And it might be said on the first aspect that perhaps instrumentality arises for logic in relation to itself, because it is apparent that some logical issues are tools for others. And it was answered that the arising of instrumentality is only in relation to some other, not to itself; and the arising of instrumentality for itself would only be if every issue of it were a tool in relation to itself. And it is not hidden that this answer is only by way of going along and placating the opponent, otherwise it could be said: it suffices for instrumentality being accidental that it arises in relation to another science, even if it also arises in relation to itself, because the essential of a thing must belong to it only in itself. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of logic as 'an instrumental legal tool' (āla qānūniyya). The commentator unpacks each term: 'tool' (āla) is an intermediary between agent and effect, like a pen for a writer; 'law' (qānūn) is a universal principle; 'thought' (fikr) is the ordering of known to unknown. Logic is a tool because it mediates between the rational faculty and cognitive goals; it is a law because its principles are universal, e.g., the rule of conversion of affirmative propositions. The phrase 'infallibility' (ʿiṣma) is attributed to observance of logic, not to logic itself, since even a logician can err if negligent. Then the commentator explains the exclusions: 'tool' as genus excludes non-instruments; 'legal' as remote differentia excludes specific craft tools; the final condition (that its observance safeguards against error in discourse) excludes other legal sciences like grammar that safeguard discourse but not thought. He then argues that this definition is a rasm (description) not a ḥadd (strict definition) because instrumentality is accidental (ʿaraḍ), not essential (dhātī). The essential must inhere in the thing itself, but logic is an instrument only relative to other sciences. Moreover, it defines by purpose (ghāya), which is external. An objection is raised: perhaps some logical problems serve as tools for others, implying instrumentality is internal. The answer is that it is only relative to some other problems, not to itself, and even if it were, it suffices that instrumentality arises relative to another science for it to be accidental. The commentary follows the standard Mantiq tradition of distinguishing between essential and accidental, and between hadd and rasm.

Key Terms
آلة
قانون
فكر
عصمة
رسم
حد
ذاتي
عارض

Checkpoint

C) It is a Greek word.
D) It is essential to logic.

Key Takeaway
The definition of logic as an instrumental legal tool is a description (rasm) because it uses accidental properties and purpose, not essential properties.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Essential vs. Accidental: Why Logic's Definition is a Rasm
Commentator defines logic as an instrument for safeguarding the intellect and explains why this is a description rather than a strict definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between essential (dhātī) and accidental (ʿaraḍ) properties and evaluate why the definition of logic as a tool is a rasm rather than a hadd.

Core Idea
Instrumentality is accidental because it is relative to other sciences, not intrinsic to logic.

The commentator argues that the definition of logic is a rasm because instrumentality (āliyya) is accidental and the definition invokes the purpose (ghāya) of safeguarding from error. In this lesson, we examine the reasoning behind this claim and the subsequent objection and response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the claim
The commentator states: 'Then the definition is a description (rasm) of logic, not a strict definition (ḥadd).' This is the main claim we need to evaluate.
2
Find the first reason: instrumentality is accidental
The essential (dhātī) of a thing belongs to it in itself. Being a tool (āla) is not intrinsic to logic; it is a tool only in relation to other sciences. Thus, it is an accidental property (ʿaraḍ).
The commentator says: 'Its being a tool is not of the essential properties but of the accidents.'
3
Understand the second reason: definition by purpose
The definition includes safeguarding from error, which is the purpose (ghāya) of logic. Since the purpose of a thing is external to it, a definition that includes it is a rasm.
The commentator says: 'It is a definition by purpose... the purpose of a thing is external to it.'
4
Analyze the objection
An objector says: 'Perhaps instrumentality arises for logic in relation to itself, because some logical issues are tools for others.' This would make instrumentality internal.
The objection challenges the claim that instrumentality is only relative to other sciences.
5
Examine the response
The answer is that even if some issues are tools for others, instrumentality is still relative to those others, not to itself. True instrumentality in itself would require every issue to be a tool in relation to itself. The commentator notes this answer is concessive; in reality, it suffices that instrumentality arises relative to another science for it to be accidental.
The commentator adds: 'It suffices for instrumentality being accidental that it arises in relation to another science, even if it also arises in relation to itself.'

قوله والمنطق آلة قانونية الالة هى الواسطة بين الفاعل و منفعله في وصول اثره اليه كالقلم للكاتب قائه واسطة بينه وبين المكتوب في وصول اثره اليه. والقانون لفظ يونانى او سرياني موضوع في لغتهم لمسطر الكتابة وفى الاصطلاح قضية كلية تعرف منها احكام جزئيات موضوعها و سيأتى ذلك مفصلاً عند شرح قول المصنف: «فاحتيج إلى قانون» انشاء الله تعالى. والفكر هو ترتيب أمور معلومة لتحصيل امور مجهولة وسيأتي تفصيله انشاء الله تعالى. ثم انما كان المنطق آلة لكونها واسطة بين القوة العاقلة والمطالب الكسبية في الاكتساب، وإنما كان قانوناً، لان مسائله قوانين كلية تعرف منها أحكام موضوعاتها. كما اذا عرفنا مثلاً ان الموجبة تنعكس إلى الموجبة الجزئية، عرفنا ان :قولنا كل انسان حیوان تنعكس الى قولنا بعض الحيوان انسان» و هكذا. وانما نسب العصمة إلى مراعاتها، لان نفس المنطق ليس بعاصم عن الخطاء والا لوجب ان لا يصدر خطاء عن المنطقى مع انه ربما يعرض الخطاء له لاهماله وعدم مراعاته الألة و هو ظاهر. هذا مفهوم التعريف و اما احترازاته فالالة بمنزلة الجنس وقوله ،قانونية بمنزلة الفصل البعيد يخرج الآلات الجزئية لارباب الصنايع فى صنايعهم. والقيد الاخير بمنزلة الفصل القريب يخرج العلوم القانونية التي تعصم مراعاتها عن الخطاء في المقال لا في الفكر كالنحو والصرف وغيرهما من العلوم الادبية. ثم ان التعريف رسم للمنطق لاحد له فان الحد انما يكون بالذاتيات كما سيجيء، وكونه آلة ليس من الذاتيات بل من العوارض فان الذاتى للشيء يكون له فى نفسه والآلية للمنطق ليست له في نفسه بل بالقياس الى سائر العلوم وايضاً هو تعريف بالغاية اذ العصمة عن الخطاء انما هي غاية للمنطق وغاية الشيء تكون خارجة عنه والتعريف بالخارج رسم هذا. و ربما قيل على الوجه الاول انه ربما يحصل الآلتية للمنطق بالقياس الى نفسه لظهور أن بعض المسائل المنطقية آلة للبعض. واجيب بان حصول الآلتية انما هو بالقياس الى البعض الاخر لا الى نفسه و حصول الآلتية لنفسه انما يكون اذا كان كل مسألة من مسائله آلة بالقياس الى نفسه. ولا يخفى ان هذا الجواب انما هو من باب المجاراة والمماشاة مع الخصم والا فيمكن ان يقال: انه يكفى في كون الآلتية عرضياً حصولها بالقياس الى علم آخر و إن كان حاصلاً بالقياس الى نفسه ايضاً اذ الذاتي للشيء يجب ان يكون له فى نفسه فقط. فتأمل. (میرزا محمد علی)
His statement: 'And logic is an instrumental tool' — the tool is the intermediary between the agent and its object in the transmission of its effect to it, like the pen for the writer, for it is an intermediary between him and the written in the transmission of his effect to it. And 'law' (qānūn) is a Greek or Syriac word coined in their language for the ruler of writing; in technical terminology it is a universal proposition from which are known the rulings of the particulars of its subject. This will come in detail when explaining the author's statement: 'So there was need for a law', God willing. And 'thought' (fikr) is the ordering of known matters to obtain unknown matters; its detail will come, God willing. Then logic is a tool only because it is an intermediary between the rational faculty and the acquired demands in acquisition. It is a law only because its issues are universal laws from which are known the rulings of their subjects. For example, if we know that the affirmative proposition converts to the particular affirmative, we know that our statement 'Every human is an animal' converts to our statement 'Some animal is a human', and so on. And 'infallibility' (ʿiṣma) is attributed to its observance, because logic itself is not a safeguard against error; otherwise it would be necessary that no error should occur from a logician, yet error may occur to him due to negligence and non-observance of the tool. This is apparent. This is the concept of the definition. As for its exclusions (iḥtirāzāt): 'tool' serves as a genus, and his statement 'legal' serves as a remote differentia, excluding the particular tools of the craftsmen in their crafts. And the last condition serves as a proximate differentia, excluding the legal sciences the observance of which safeguards against error in discourse but not in thought, such as grammar, morphology, and other literary sciences. Then the definition is a description (rasm) of logic, not a strict definition (ḥadd), for a strict definition is only by essential properties (dhātiyyāt) as will come. Its being a tool is not of the essential properties but of the accidents (aʿrāḍ), for the essential of a thing belongs to it in itself, and instrumentality for logic does not belong to it in itself but in relation to other sciences. Also, it is a definition by purpose (ghāya), because safeguarding from error is only the purpose of logic, and the purpose of a thing is external to it, and definition by what is external is a description. And it might be said on the first aspect that perhaps instrumentality arises for logic in relation to itself, because it is apparent that some logical issues are tools for others. And it was answered that the arising of instrumentality is only in relation to some other, not to itself; and the arising of instrumentality for itself would only be if every issue of it were a tool in relation to itself. And it is not hidden that this answer is only by way of going along and placating the opponent, otherwise it could be said: it suffices for instrumentality being accidental that it arises in relation to another science, even if it also arises in relation to itself, because the essential of a thing must belong to it only in itself. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the definition of logic as 'an instrumental legal tool' (āla qānūniyya). The commentator unpacks each term: 'tool' (āla) is an intermediary between agent and effect, like a pen for a writer; 'law' (qānūn) is a universal principle; 'thought' (fikr) is the ordering of known to unknown. Logic is a tool because it mediates between the rational faculty and cognitive goals; it is a law because its principles are universal, e.g., the rule of conversion of affirmative propositions. The phrase 'infallibility' (ʿiṣma) is attributed to observance of logic, not to logic itself, since even a logician can err if negligent. Then the commentator explains the exclusions: 'tool' as genus excludes non-instruments; 'legal' as remote differentia excludes specific craft tools; the final condition (that its observance safeguards against error in discourse) excludes other legal sciences like grammar that safeguard discourse but not thought. He then argues that this definition is a rasm (description) not a ḥadd (strict definition) because instrumentality is accidental (ʿaraḍ), not essential (dhātī). The essential must inhere in the thing itself, but logic is an instrument only relative to other sciences. Moreover, it defines by purpose (ghāya), which is external. An objection is raised: perhaps some logical problems serve as tools for others, implying instrumentality is internal. The answer is that it is only relative to some other problems, not to itself, and even if it were, it suffices that instrumentality arises relative to another science for it to be accidental. The commentary follows the standard Mantiq tradition of distinguishing between essential and accidental, and between hadd and rasm.

Key Terms
ذاتي
عارض
غاية
حد
رسم

Checkpoint
Why does the commentator reject the objection that instrumentality is internal because some logical issues are tools for others?
A) Because instrumentality is relative, not absolute.
B) Because logical issues are not tools.
C) Because the definition is still useful.
D) Because the objection is irrelevant.

Key Takeaway
A definition that uses accidental properties or purpose is a rasm, not a hadd. The essential property of logic is not instrumentality but something belonging to it in itself.

Why the Definition of Mantiq is a Rasm, Not a Hadd
Author defines mantiq as a lawful tool and argues it is a rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify why the standard definition of mantiq (logic) is considered a rasm (description) rather than a hadd (strict definition) according to this hashia.

Core Idea
Tool-ness is accidental, not essential, so the definition of logic is a rasm.

In this hashia, the author explains the terms in the definition of mantiq and then argues that the definition is a rasm. He shows that 'tool' is an accidental attribute and that the definition mentions the goal of logic, which is external. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and shows how to distinguish hadd from rasm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and differentia in the definition
The definition uses 'tool' (آلة) as genus and 'lawful' (قانونية) as differentia. Note that this is a standard structure but the author challenges its status as a hadd.
2
Examine the accidentality of 'tool-ness'
The author argues that being a tool is not essential to logic because it is relational (to other sciences). Essential properties are self-contained, not relative.
Relational attributes are always accidental.
3
Note the inclusion of the goal
The definition mentions infallibility (عصمة) which is a goal of logic. Goals are external to the essence of a thing.
Definition by goal (تعريف بالغاية) is a type of rasm.
4
Conclude that it is a rasm
Because the definition uses accidental (tool-ness) and external (goal) attributes, it cannot be a hadd; it is a rasm.
This is the central claim of the hashia.
