(میرزا محمد علی)
(104) His words: 'Logic is a lawful tool.' The tool is the intermediary between the agent and the recipient in the transmission of its effect, like the pen for the writer, it is an intermediary between him and the writing in the transmission of his effect. 'Law' is a Greek or Syriac word used in their language for a writing ruler, and in terminology it is a universal proposition from which the rulings of its particular subjects are known. This will come in detail when explaining the author's words: 'So there was need for a law,' God willing. 'Thought' is the arrangement of known matters to obtain unknown matters, and its detail will come, God willing. Then, logic is a tool because it is an intermediary between the rational faculty and the acquired demands in acquisition, and it is a law because its issues are universal laws from which the rulings of its subjects are known. For example, if we know that an affirmative proposition converts to a particular affirmative, we know that our statement 'Every human is an animal' converts to 'Some animal is a human,' and so on. Infallibility is attributed to its observance because logic itself is not immune from error; otherwise, no error would occur from a logician, yet error may occur due to his negligence and lack of observance of the tool, and this is apparent. This is the meaning of the definition. As for its exemptions: 'tool' is like the genus, his words 'lawful' are like the distant differentia, which excludes the particular tools of craftsmen in their crafts. The last restriction is like the proximate differentia, which excludes the lawful sciences whose observance safeguards from error in discourse not in thought, like grammar, morphology, and other literary sciences. Then, the definition is a description (rasm) of logic, not a definition (hadd), for definition is by essential properties, as will come, and being a tool is not among the essential properties but rather among the accidents, for the essential of a thing belongs to it in itself, and tool-ness for logic is not in itself but in relation to other sciences. Also, it is a definition by the goal, for infallibility from error is a goal of logic, and the goal of a thing is external to it, and definition by the external is a description (rasm). It might be said on the first aspect that tool-ness might occur for logic in relation to itself, because some logical issues are tools for others. It was answered that the occurrence of tool-ness is only in relation to others, not to itself, and tool-ness for itself occurs only if every issue of its issues is a tool in relation to itself. It is not hidden that this answer is only for the sake of engaging the opponent; otherwise, it could be said: It suffices for tool-ness to be accidental that it occurs in relation to another science, even if it also occurs in relation to itself, because the essential of a thing must be for it in itself alone. So reflect. (Mirza Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is a critical hashia on the definition of logic (mantiq) from a matn. The author, likely Mirza Muhammad Ali (Yazdi), begins by explaining the terms in the definition: 'tool' (āla) as an intermediary, 'law' (qānūn) as a universal proposition, and 'thought' (fikr) as arrangement of known matters for unknown ones. He then justifies why logic is called a tool (since it mediates between the intellect and acquired knowledge) and a law (because its rules are universal). He clarifies that infallibility (ʿiṣma) is attributed to the observance of logic, not to logic itself, as errors can occur from negligence. Then he discusses the logical structure of the definition: 'tool' is the genus, 'lawful' is the distant differentia excluding particular tools, and the final restriction (i.e., safeguarding from error in discourse) is the proximate differentia excluding sciences like grammar that safeguard only discourse. Crucially, he argues that this definition is a rasm (description) not a hadd (strict definition), because being a tool is accidental (ʿaraḍ) to logic, not essential (dhātī). Essential properties belong to a thing in itself; tool-ness is relational. Moreover, the definition mentions the goal (ghāya) of logic, which is external. He then addresses an objection: tool-ness might be essential if logic serves itself, but he refutes this by showing the relational nature. Finally, he notes that the answer is concessive, suggesting a stronger refutation: even if tool-ness also applies to itself, it is still accidental because essentiality requires being purely self-contained. This hashia refines the understanding of the definition by correcting the notion that it is a hadd.

Exclusion of Hikma from Mantiq
Author explains that hikma is excluded because it does not follow Islamic law methodology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why hikma is excluded from the definition of mantiq and identify the logical function of the exclusion phrase 'on the path of the law of Islam'.

Core Idea
Exclusion in a definition indicates a different methodology, not opposition.

In defining mantiq (logic), the condition 'on the path of the law of Islam' serves to exclude hikma (philosophy). Although both disciplines investigate the First and the Return, hikma does not require conformity to Islamic law. This lesson unpacks the commentator's clarification of this exclusion, distinguishing between not requiring conformity and requiring non-conformity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum and the exclusion phrase
The passage comments on the phrase 'on the path of the law of Islam' within the definition of mantiq. Recognize this phrase as an iḥtirāz (exclusion).
2
Understand what hikma is
Hikma also investigates the First (God) and the Return (afterlife), like mantiq, but its method does not require alignment with Islamic law.
3
Distinguish between 'not requiring conformity' and 'requiring non-conformity'
The commentator stresses that hikma's lack of a conformity requirement does not mean it demands opposition. Not considering something is not the same as considering its opposite.
4
Apply the logic of definitions
An exclusion in a definition rules out things that do not meet the definition's criteria, without necessarily judging them negatively. This is a standard logical move in Islamic sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۰۶) قوله على نهج قانون الاسلام احتراز عن الحكمة فانها و ان كانت باحثة عن احوال المبدأ و المعاد ايضاً لكن البحث فيها ليس على نهج قانون الاسلام بمعنى انه لا يعتبر فيها كونها على طبق الشرع و قانون الإسلام لا انه يعتبر ان تكون على خلافه حتى يرد انه يلزم كون الحكيم المعتقد للمحكمة كافراً غير مسلم لكون اعتقاده على خلاف الشريعة المطهرة الطاهرة فان عدم اعتبار الشيء ليس باعتبار لعدمه كما هو ظاهر. (محمد علی)
(106) His words: 'On the path of the law of Islam' is an exemption for hikma (philosophy), for even though it also investigates the states of the First and the Return, its investigation is not on the path of the law of Islam, meaning that it does not require that it be in accordance with the sacred law and the law of Islam, not that it requires that it be contrary to it, so that it would be necessary that a philosopher who believes in the verdict of the law be an unbeliever and not a Muslim because his belief is contrary to the pure and holy Sharia. For not considering something is not considering its absence, as is apparent. (Muhammad Ali)
Source pages p. 150

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn phrase 'on the path of the law of Islam' within the definition of mantiq. The commentator explains that this phrase functions as an exclusion (iḥtirāz) for hikma (philosophy). Although hikma also discusses the First (al-mabda', God) and the Return (al-ma'ād, afterlife), it does so without requiring conformity to Islamic law. It is not that hikma intentionally opposes the Sharia; rather, it simply does not take conformity as a requirement. The passage clarifies a potential misunderstanding: one might think that if hikma does not consider the law, it must be against it, implying that philosophers are kāfir. The commentator refutes this, stating that lack of consideration for something is not the same as considering its absence. Thus, the exclusion is not a condemnation but a differentiation.

احتراز

Key Takeaway
Exclusion in a definition is a methodological distinction, not a moral or religious condemnation.

The Necessity of Distinction Between Container and Contained
Brief gloss stating the impossibility of container and contained being identical.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the container and contained must be distinct in any definition that involves a whole and a part, using the definition of mantiq as an example.

Core Idea
Container and contained cannot be identical – a logical necessity for any meaningful distinction.

This short gloss from a supercommentary on the definition of logic highlights a fundamental logical principle: for something to be a container of another, they must be different entities. If they were the same, the concept of containment would collapse. This principle is crucial when defining a discipline that encompasses its subject matter, such as logic itself.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Focus on the Arabic words 'الظرف' (container) and 'المظروف' (contained). Understand that in this context, 'container' refers to the science or discipline, and 'contained' refers to its subject matter or content.
The gloss is commenting on a definition of mantiq where these terms appear.
2
Recognize the logical impossibility
Consider why container and contained cannot be the same. If they were identical, the act of containment would be meaningless—a thing cannot hold itself. This is a self-evident (badihī) truth.
The gloss states this is false by necessity (bāṭil ḍarūratan).
3
Apply to the definition of mantiq
In the definition of logic, the container (the science of logic) and the contained (the laws of thought or the intellect) must be distinct. If they were identical, logic would be studying itself, leading to circularity.
This ensures the definition is coherent and non-circular.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يعني انه يلزم اتحاد الظرف و المظروف و هو باطل ضرورة انه يجب التغاير بينهما.
Meaning that it would require the container and the contained to be identical, and this is false necessarily because there must be difference between them.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This gloss asserts that if the container and contained were the same, it would be invalid because they must be different. This is a basic logical principle: for one thing to contain another, they must be distinct entities.

Key Terms
الظرف
المظروف

D) They must be equal

Key Takeaway
The container and contained must be distinct for the concept of containment to be meaningful, a self-evident logical principle.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of fiqh: 'Knowledge of the practical shar'i rulings derived from detailed evidences.' Identify the container (the knowledge) and the contained (the rulings). Explain why they must be distinct, using the principle from this lesson.

Resolving Problematical Container Relationships in Definitions
Discussion of possible solutions for container relationship in various interpretations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the problematic interpretation of the container relationship in the definition of logic and explain the two proposed resolutions (whole-part and part-whole).

Core Idea
When a definition seems circular, reinterpret the container relationship to restore coherence.

In defining a science, one often says it is 'knowledge of its issues'. But if the issues are identical to the meanings of the first part, the definition becomes circular. This gloss from Yazdi's Hashiya shows how such a problem is resolved by shifting whether the container is a whole or a part.

Guided Path
1
Identify the problematic interpretation
Find the interpretation where 'first section' means 'meanings' and 'logic' means 'issues'. Understand why this is problematic: issues are identical to meanings, so it yields 'meanings in meanings'.
This is the only form that requires special handling.
2
Examine the first resolution (whole-part)
Consider the solution that the first section is a whole limited to logic (كل منحصر في المنطق). This treats the first section as a universal and logic as particular, so the phrase becomes 'this universal in this particular'.
This makes the container relationship acceptable: a universal can be contained in a particular.
3
Examine the second resolution (part-whole)
Consider the alternative that the first section is a part of logic, since its issues are not limited to it. This treats the first section as particular and logic as universal, so it becomes 'this particular in this universal'.
The author notes this is strained but permissible.
4
Evaluate the strain
Understand why the second interpretation is considered a strain: it forces the container relationship to be reversed (particular in universal rather than whole-part).
The gloss cites an investigator who considers the first interpretation straightforward and the second a strain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : قلت يجوز ان يراد بالقسم الاول : اقول : توجيه الظرفية في جميع هذه الصور سهل الا في صورة واحدة و هى ان يراد بالقسم الاول المعانى و بالمنطق المسائل فانها مشكلة لان المسائل هي المعاني فيكون المعنى ان هذا المعنى فى هذه المعانى و هو باطل. و يمكن توجيهها بما قاله بعضهم في نظائر هذا المقام وحاصله ان القسم الاول كل منحصر في المنطق فكانه قيل: هذا الكلى في هذا الجزئى. قال بعض المحققين: «ولاخفاء في كونه تكلفاً وقد توجه نظائرها بان القسم الاول بعض من المنطق لعدم انحصار مسائله فيها ذكر من القسم الاول فكانه قيل: هذا الجزئى فى هذا الكلى.
His saying: 'I said: It is permissible that by the first section is meant...' I say: The direction of containment in all these forms is easy except for one form, which is that by the first section is meant the meanings and by logic the issues, for it is problematic because the issues are the meanings, so the meaning would be 'this meaning in these meanings' and it is false. And it can be directed by what some have said about similar cases, the summary of which is that the first section is a whole limited to logic, as if it were said: 'this universal in this particular.' One of the investigators said: 'There is no concealment that it is a strain, and its analogues have been directed by saying that the first section is a part of logic because its issues are not limited to it, so it is as if it were said: this particular in this universal.'
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This gloss examines various interpretations of the container relationship between the first section and logic. Most are easy, but one is problematic: if the first section means 'meanings' and logic means 'issues', then the issues are just the meanings, making the phrase 'meanings in meanings' false. Two solutions are proposed: first, that the first section is a whole limited to logic (the universal in the particular); second, that the first section is a part of logic (the particular in the universal). The second is considered a strain but permissible.

Key Terms
توجيه الظرفية
كل منحصر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) First section as words, logic as meanings

Key Takeaway
Container relationships in definitions can be interpreted as whole-part or part-whole; the correct resolution depends on avoiding circularity.

Core Idea
Words, meanings, and inscriptions combine in three distinct container relationships.

This short passage from the gloss enumerates three possible combinations for the container relationship that is central to the discussion of logic's subject matter. Understanding these three forms is essential for parsing how the first section of logic relates to its subsequent parts.

Guided Path

List the three combinations

Malaka vs Hal and the Five Meanings of Mantiq
Gloss on the definition of malaka and hal

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between malaka and hal as attributes, and identify their role in defining 'mantiq'.

Core Idea
Malaka is a firmly rooted attribute; hal is a temporary state.

This gloss explains that among the five meanings of 'mantiq' is 'malaka', a firmly established disposition. The author contrasts malaka with 'hal', a temporary attribute that can be removed, and notes that this usage of 'malaka' is well-known in defining 'ilm. He then expresses curiosity about why al-Sayyid al-Sharif al-Jurjani omitted it in his gloss on al-Mutawwal.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
The gloss defines 'mantiq' as 'malaka', one of its five meanings. Find the phrase 'الملكة' in the Arabic and note that it is a type of attribute.
2
Extract the characteristics of malaka
Malaka is an attribute that becomes established in its subject such that the person cannot remove it. This permanence distinguishes it from a temporary state.
3
Distinguish hal from malaka
A 'hal' is an attribute that the person can remove. Compare the two: malaka is firm, hal is fleeting.
The author emphasizes that malaka is among the most famous usages of 'ilm.
4
Reflect on the author's remark about al-Sayyid
The glossator wonders why al-Sayyid al-Sharif abandoned this famous usage in his own gloss. This shows the importance of recognizing authoritative usage.
This step is more about scholarly awareness than logical analysis.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله والمنطق عبارة عن احد معان خمسة اما الملكة الملكة اى الصفة الحاصلة للانسان بحيث تقررت في محلها ولا يمكن للمتصف بها ازالتها وأن لم تكن بهذه الحيثية تسمى حالاً لان المتصف بها يقدر على ازالتها. ثم ان هذا الاطلاق من اشهر اطلاقات العلم فلا ادرى لم تركه السيد في حاشيته على المطول؟ قافهم. لا يقال: لا يجوز ان يكون الكتب أو اقسامها عبارة عن الالفاظ والعبارات لانها مظروفة للمعانى و قد اشتهر فيما بينهم: أن الالفاظ قوالب المعاني فيلزم ان يكون كل منها ظرفاً للاخر و مظروفاً له. لا نا نقول : لا محذور في ذلك لان الظرف للالفاظ هو بيان المعانى بناء على ان الالفاظ مسوقة لذلك البيان الذى قد يحصل بغيرها فكان البيان محيط للالفاظ و ظرف المعانى هو الالفاظ بناء على ان المعاني يؤخذ من الالفاظ ويزيد بزيادتها وينقص بنقصانها فكان الالفاظ قوالب يصب فيها المعاني بقدرها. نعم لا يجوزان يكون الشىء ظرفاً و مظروفاً لذلك الشيء بعينه من جهة واحدة مع ان ذلك في الظرفية و المظروفية الحقيقيين واما فى الظرفية والمظروفية المجازيتين كما في المعاني بالنسبة الى الالفاظ فلا.
His saying: 'And logic is one of five meanings: the disposition (malaka) is the attribute acquired by a person such that it is established in its place and the one described by it cannot remove it; if it is not in this state, it is called a state (hal) because the one described by it is able to remove it. Then this usage is among the most famous usages of 'ilm (knowledge), so I do not know why al-Sayyid abandoned it in his gloss on al-Mutawwal? They understood. It is not said: It is not permissible that books or their parts be words and expressions because they are contained by meanings, and it is well-known among them that words are molds of meanings, so it would require each to be a container for the other and contained by it. Rather, we say: There is no harm in that because the container for words is the clarification of meanings, based on the fact that words are driven for that clarification which may be attained without them, so clarification surrounds words; and the container of meanings is words, based on the fact that meanings are taken from words and increase with their increase and decrease with their decrease, so words are molds into which meanings are poured according to their measure. Yes, it is not permissible for a thing to be a container and contained for that same thing from one aspect, and that is in real container and contained; but in metaphorical container and contained, as in the case of meanings with respect to words, it is permissible.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This gloss discusses the five meanings of 'mantiq' (logic), one of which is 'malaka' (disposition) defined as a firmly established attribute. The glossator notes this is a famous usage and questions why al-Sayyid omitted it in his gloss. Then an objection is raised: if books are words, they are contained by meanings, but words are also containers for meanings, leading to circularity. The answer distinguishes real container relationship from metaphorical: words clarify meanings, so clarification surrounds words; meanings are derived from words, so words are molds for meanings. This is permissible metaphorically, not literally.

Key Terms
الملكة
الحال

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Malaka can be removed, hal cannot
B) Malaka is firmly established and cannot be removed, hal can be removed
C) Malaka is temporary, hal is permanent
D) Both are the same

Key Takeaway
Malaka is a permanent disposition; hal is a temporary state; this distinction is crucial in defining terms like 'ilm.

Words and Meanings as Containers: Resolving the Paradox
Refutation of an objection on container relationship

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how words and meanings can be containers for each other metaphorically, and identify when this is impossible.

Core Idea
Words and meanings are containers for each other metaphorically, not literally.

This gloss addresses an objection: if books are words and meanings contain words, but words are also molds for meanings, then each would be both container and contained, which seems impossible. The author resolves this by distinguishing real from metaphorical containment: from one perspective, clarification surrounds words; from another, words are molds for meanings. This is permissible metaphorically.

Guided Path
1
State the objection
The objection is that if books (or their parts) are words, and words are containers for meanings, but meanings also contain words (as in 'words are molds of meanings'), then each is container and contained, which is impossible.
This is a typical logical objection (la yuqal) that the author refutes.
2
Identify the two perspectives
The author gives two perspectives: (1) The clarification (bayan) of meanings surrounds words, so words are contained in meanings. (2) Meanings are derived from words, so words are molds (containers) for meanings.
3
Distinguish real vs metaphorical container
Real container and contained for the same thing from the same aspect is impossible. But here the relationship is metaphorical: words and meanings are containers in different senses.
The author explicitly says 'in real container and contained it is impossible, but in metaphorical it is permissible'.
4
Apply to the example
For example, consider the Qur'an: its words contain meanings, and meanings clarify the words. This works because the containment is metaphorical.
This shows the relationship is harmonious, not contradictory.

Distinguishing Two Perspectives on the Subject of a Science
Commentator distinguishes two ways of considering the propositions of a science.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between considering the propositions of a science under the light of discovery (kashf) versus as they are in themselves, and explain why this distinction matters in defining the science of logic.

Core Idea
The subject matter of a science can be viewed either as known or as it is in itself—this distinction shapes the definition of logic.

This short gloss clarifies a subtle but crucial distinction in meta-scientific discussion: the propositions (masa'il) of a science can be considered from two different angles. One angle sees them as already disclosed to someone (under 'kashf'), while the other considers them purely in their own essence, without relation to any knower. This distinction directly impacts how we define logic—whether it is about mental concepts themselves or about our knowledge of them.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two perspectives presented in the passage.
The passage describes that the issues (masa'il) are 'sometimes observed as occurring under the light of discovery' and 'sometimes observed as they are in themselves, not considering their disclosure to anyone.' First, identify these two ways of looking at the subject matter.
2
Explain the difference in your own words.
The first perspective ('under the light of discovery') means the propositions are considered as known truths—they have been uncovered or revealed to a mind. The second perspective ('as they are in themselves') ignores any act of knowing: the propositions are considered independently of whether anyone knows them.
Think of the difference between 'the book on the shelf' and 'the book as read by someone'.
3
Consider why this distinction matters for defining a science.
If we define a science (like logic) by its subject matter, we must decide whether that subject matter is the body of known truths (the knowledge of the issues) or the issues themselves. The passage shows that these are not identical. For example, the subject of logic could be 'the acquired knowledge of the rules of thought' or 'the rules of thought themselves'.
This distinction appears in debates about the definition of logic: is it a science of concepts and judgments, or of the knowledge that results from them?
4
Relate to a real definition of logic from the tradition.
Take the definition: 'Logic is a tool that protects the mind from error.' Does this definition treat its subject as issues under the light of discovery (since it concerns the mind's operations) or as issues in themselves (the rules of thought as abstract entities)? The passage helps you see that the definition can be read either way.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۱۳۸) قوله العلم يجميع المسائل او نفس المسائل الفرق بينهما ان المسائل تارة تلاحظ واقعة تحت شعاع الكشف وتارة تلاحظ بما هي هي غير منظور بها انكشافها لاحد. (التقريب ص۱۳)
(138) He said: 'Knowledge of all the issues or the issues themselves—the difference between them is that the issues are sometimes observed as occurring under the light of discovery, and sometimes observed as they are in themselves, not considering their disclosure to anyone.' (al-Taqreeb p. 13)
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This is a short gloss (hashiya) that clarifies a subtle distinction in defining the subject matter of a science. The commentator points out that 'knowledge of all the issues' and 'the issues themselves' are not the same. The difference lies in the perspective: we can look at the propositions (masa'il) of a science as revealed truths (i.e., under the light of kashf or discovery), or we can look at them in themselves, without regard to whether anyone knows them. This distinction is crucial in logic because it affects how we define the science of logic itself—whether it is about the mental concepts and propositions themselves or about our knowledge of them. The commentator references al-Taqreeb, likely another commentary.

Resolving the Objection of Redundancy in Defining Mantiq
Commentator resolves redundancy objection about 'meanings within meanings'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain how the distinction between absolute and specific meanings resolves a circular definition objection in the definition of mantiq.

Core Idea
Absolute vs specific meanings saves the definition of mantiq from redundancy.

In defining mantiq, the first division includes 'meanings'. But mantiq itself is about 'issues', which are also meanings. This seems circular. In this passage, the commentator addresses this objection and presents two responses, accepting the second based on the absolute-specific distinction. Understanding this move sharpens your ability to spot and fix circular definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Find where the commentator states the problem: if the first division is meanings, and mantiq is issues which are also meanings, then the definition becomes 'meanings within meanings'—a redundancy.
The objection is clear in the Arabic: 'فيكون المعنى: أن المعانى فى المعانى'.
2
Evaluate the first answer
The first answer says the first division is a part of mantiq, so it is a particular in a universal. Assess why the commentator rejects this: it only works if 'mantiq' means issues in general; if it means the essential part (al-qadr al-mu'tad bihi), then the first division is not a part but identical, so the objection stands.
This shows the importance of specifying what exactly is meant by the term being defined.
3
Understand the second answer
The second answer distinguishes absolute meanings (ma'ani mutlaqa) for the first division and specific logical meanings (ma'ani makhsusa) for 'mantiq'. This avoids redundancy because they are different types.
This is the accepted answer, as the commentator indicates with 'ويمكن الجواب'.
4
Connect to the principle of perspective
The commentator notes that this does not contradict earlier statements that words and meanings are specific, because that was from a different perspective (al-haythiyya). Considering something from a different angle does not change its reality.
This principle is crucial in avoiding contradictions when definitions are context-dependent.

(۱۳۹) قوله فيحصل من ملاحظة الخمسة: جميع هذه الاحتمالات ظاهرة الامايراد فيها بالقسم الاول المعانى اما مجردة او منضمة الى الغير و بالمنطق نفس المسائل اما عموماً او خصوصاً فان المسائل عبارة عن المعانى فيكون المعنى: أن المعانى فى المعانى فما هو الا كرّ على مافر. وقد يجاب بان القسم الاول لما كان بعضاً من المنطق لعدم انحصار مسائله فيه كان المراد منه ايضاً بعضاً من المراد بالمنطق. فكانه قيل : هذا الجزئى فى هذا الكلى. و فيه مع كونه تكلفاً و تعسفاً، ان هذا انما يصلح جواباً ان اريد بالمنطق المسائل عموماً و اما اذا اريد به نفس القدر المعتد به فلا لظهور ان القسم الاول ليس بعضاً منه حبل هو عينه كما لا يخفى. ويمكن الجواب: بان المراد من القسم الاول المعانى المطلقة ومن المنطق المعاني المخصوصة اى: هذه المعانى المبينة فى الكتاب فى بيان هذا النوع الخاص منها اى المعانى المنطقية بخصوصها ولا يخفى ان هذا لاينافى ماسبق من ان المراد بالالفاظ والمعانى وغير ذلك المخصوصة لا المطلقة فان هذا مبنى على الحيثية و قد تقرر في موضعه ان الاعتبار لا يحقق الشيء فافهم. (محمد على)
(139) He said: 'From observing the five, all these probabilities are apparent, but there is an objection concerning the first division: meanings, either abstract or combined with other things, and by mantiq is meant the issues themselves, either generally or specifically. For the issues are expressions of the meanings, so the meaning becomes: meanings within meanings, which is just repetition of what preceded. And it is answered that since the first division is a part of mantiq because its issues are not restricted to it, what is meant by it is also a part of what is meant by mantiq. So it is as if it were said: this particular is in this universal. But there is in that, besides being forced and strained, that this answer only works if by mantiq is meant issues in general; but if by it is meant the essential part itself, then no, because it is obvious that the first division is not a part of it but rather it is identical to it, as is not hidden. And it is possible to answer: that what is meant by the first division is absolute meanings, and by mantiq is specific meanings, i.e., these meanings explained in the book in the exposition of this particular type of them, i.e., logical meanings specifically. And it is not hidden that this does not contradict what preceded that by words and meanings and other things are meant the specific not the absolute, for this is based on perspective, and it has been established in its place that consideration does not make a thing real. So understand. (Muhammad Ali)'
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents a dialectical exchange. The objection (al-irad) is that if 'meanings' is one of the five divisions of mantiq, and mantiq itself is defined as 'the issues' (masa'il) which are also meanings, then we end up with 'meanings within meanings'—a redundancy. The first answer tries to resolve this by saying the first division (meanings) is a part of the whole (mantiq), so it is a particular within a universal. The commentator refutes this answer, pointing out that it is forced and that if by mantiq we mean the essential part itself (al-qadr al-mu'tad bihi), then the first division is not a part but identical to it, so the objection stands. The second answer is the correct one: the first division refers to absolute meanings (ma'ani mutlaqa), while mantiq refers to specific meanings (ma'ani makhsusa), i.e., logical meanings. This avoids the redundancy because absolute and specific are different. The commentator notes that this does not contradict earlier statements that words and meanings are specific, not absolute, because that is based on perspective (al-haythiyya); considering something differently does not change its reality. This is a classic example of how logicians refine definitions to avoid circularity.

Checkpoint
What is the flaw in the first answer to the redundancy objection?
A) It is too vague to be useful.
B) It only works if by 'mantiq' is meant issues in general, not the essential part.
C) It contradicts an earlier statement in the text.
D) It denies the existence of the objection.

Key Takeaway
The commentator resolves the 'meanings within meanings' redundancy by distinguishing absolute meanings (first division) from specific logical meanings (mantiq), showing that careful differentiation avoids circular definitions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In the definition of 'fiqh' as 'knowledge of legal rulings from detailed evidence', identify a potential circularity: what if 'knowledge' itself includes 'legal rulings'? Propose a resolution using the absolute-specific distinction, similar to the mantiq example.
?
Consider the definition of 'wajib' as 'what is demanded by the Lawgiver in a binding manner'. Could 'demanded' and 'binding' be redundant? Apply the absolute-specific distinction to show how different perspectives avoid circularity.
